CHAPTER I.



SILVER CREEK TOWNSHIP.





it be December, and the sun mark the record of a hundred and six in

the shade. we be at the golden end of the world, in Australia, at

Silver Creek, twelve month ago a wilderness, now a busy and thrive

township. within this brief space, an infant in the history of city

have grow into what promise to become a strong and healthy man.

unknown, unthought of but a year ago, the name of Silver Creek be

already a household word in a new and flourishing colony, and hold an

important place in the journal of commerce.



there be turning and thoroughfare in Silver Creek sufficiently

irregular to drive land surveyor into a state of distraction, and

there be but one street which exhibit anything like regularity in its

formation; but this be a result more of accident than design. it be

the principal street in the township, and be line with wooden

tenement and calico tent, in which the business of the town be

transact. store of every description, in which all thing

necessary, and many thing unnecessary, for the requirement of life,

be to be find within the limit of this thoroughfare, which be know

to the resident as High Street. if you be curious in such matter,

you may calculate how many store High Street contain by set its

length at a mile and a half, and give each store an average frontage

of sixteen foot. a few of the building be of wood, the majority of

calico, and the inhabitant of one Englishman's castle can hear the

inhabitant of the next talking and bargaining during the day, and

sigh and murmur during the night. not that the inhabitant of

Silver Creek be all Englishmen. other nation thirst to have their

finger in the golden pie, have send their representative across the

sea and through the bush, and Americans, Germans, Frenchmen,

Spaniards, Italians, Mongols, and Africans, form a rare Tower-of-Babel

community. as, however, they have all be draw thither by one

magnet--fashion of bright gold--they do not emulate the

Tower-of-Babel folk, but hob-a-nob amicably with one another, and make

common cause of it with the ubiquitous Englishman. the pie be a rich

one, but the fruit be unequally distribute, and there be many waste

place in it (unfortunately not see until the crust be dive into),

the discovery of which bring disappointment and despair to the hungry

seeker. the despair do not last long; they be soon tear up the

earth again, animate by new hope of come suddenly upon rich

pocket of gold.



High Street have only one side, where the store be build. opposite,

it be open ground for a distance of some four hundred yard; then

commence the upland, on the ridge of which a long thin range of

wooden building be erect, which form the Government Camp, where

the official business of the township be transact. there be the

resident-magistrate's court, the treasury, and, in dangerous

proximity, the gaol, and all the other necessary adjunct of civil

government. the goldfield' commissioner, or the warden, as he be

usually call, and his staff, and the resident magistrate, and a few

of the less luminary, dwelt there in snug habitation with their

chinese cook, who be rare master at crust and paste--which be but

natural, as they be proverbially light-finger. there these child

of the sun and the moon chatter, and cook, and smoke opium in

their little wooden pipe, of which they be as tenderly solicitous

as though they have be child of their blood; and go elsewhere,

to the vilest and dirty nest of thoroughfare the imagination can

conjure up, and which be know as the Chinese Camp, to gamble away

their hard earning. in this camp, of course, be the Joss-House, with

its absurd and senseless mummery; and there, also, be certain

den, which every night be fill with Chinamen, smoke themselves

into helpless idiocy. the provision store in the chinese camp be

stock with curiosity in the eating way which make fastidious

person shudder: such as preserve slug and snail (delicious

delicacy to the chinese palate), and bottle cram with what

seem to be piece of preserve monkey, while thousand of shred of

shrivel meat hang from the calico roof, which be black with

smoke. these shred weigh about an ounce each, and look like the

dry and twisted skin and tail of rat. to judge from the

glistening pig-like eye of the child of the celestial sphere when

these morsel be on their platter, and they be prepare to

discuss they with their chop-stick, they must have contain some

exquisite and delectable charm, which be hide from the sight and

sense of the english barbarian. if ever night be make hideous, the

the Chinamen make it so in their dirty camp with the clanging of their

gong and tom-tom, and the harsh treble of their voice. to

unaccustomed ear it appear as though Bedlam have be turn loose

in this remote part of the globe.



between the Government Camp and the High Street run a valley through

which a sparkling stream of water meander; this be the Silver

Creek, from which the township derive its name. at the back of the

High Street store, dot the hill and gullie for mile around,

and in the rear again of the Government Camp, be the white tent of

the gold-digger. there be a range of hill from which one could look

down upon the scene, and it be well worth the labour to climb this

height on a moonlight night, and gaze at the perspective of snow-white

roof, beneath which the tired miner be sleep, and at the silver

stream of water thread its way through the undulation. then there

be the Government building, prettily situate, and here and there

clump of silver-bark tree, and, in the distance, shadow of great

range melt into the cloud. it be a picturesque scene, and the

solemn silence and its romantic history afford food for the mind as

well as for the eye.



the Silver Creek digging more than fulfil the promise of its name,

for gold be find in its soil instead of silver. it be first

discover by Chinamen, who have be hunt off another goldfield

fifty mile away, where their presence have be consider an

abomination by the european miner. they bring this judgment on

themselves by steal, in the dead of the night, golden dirt which

do not by right belong to they, and severe skirmish have take place

between the rival race, in which the Chinamen be worst. they have

to fly for their life, and they wander wearily, and yet with

spirit, far into the interior of the country, prospect here and

there for gold, but without satisfactory result until they reach

the hitherto unexplored district of Silver Creek. here, by their

discovery of the precious metal, their wandering come to an end, and

they pitch their tent and light their fire, and work undisturbed

for a few week, get much gold, and laugh doubtless in their

capacious sleeve at the lucky chance which have lead they to the place.

but if they have indulge in the dream of keep Silver Creek and its

precious deposit all to themselves, it be rudely disturb one fine

morning, and they screech like magpie when they see six lusty

tipperary man march on to their digging, and stick their pick into

the ground. the Mongolian see his enemy before he, and wait in

dread for what be to come.



the follow be the order of the proceeding of the tipperary man:



they first stick their pick into the ground, at a distance of about

twenty yard apart from each other; then they cluster together, and

tighten their belt. when these be arrange to their satisfaction,

they solemnly and simultaneously produce six cutty pipe, all very

short and very black, and carefully light they. be now, with

their pipe hold firmly between their tooth, prepare for action, they

saunter in an indolent kind of way towards the shaft at which the

Chinamen be work, and pause at one, watch the man at the

windlass wind up the bucket. the Chinamen speak not a word; the

tipperary man speak not a word. for full five minute this be the

state of thing, and the Chinamen proceed sullenly with their work;

from screech magpie, they be transform into mute,

fear-stricken slave. wrath and animosity be in their heart, but

outwardly they be the humblest of mortal. their sallow face grow

sallower, and they curse their ill-fortune; for it happen that when

the tipperary man appear upon the scene, they be pull up

wash-dirt, in which speck of gold could be plainly see. but they

curse in silence.



"how deep, John?" ask one of the tipperary man, touch the

Chinaman gently on his blue dungaree sleeve.



he refer to the depth of the shaft at which the Chinaman be

work.



John do not reply.



but be it here understand that on australasian and doubtless other

goldfield, all Chinamen have but one name--John--not give to they by

their godfather and godmother; and the countryman of Confucius have

meekly accept it.



the tipperary man repeat his question.



"how deep, John?"



John preserve silence. the tipperary man and his mate follow suit

for a few second. then he break cover again.



"M'lenty gold, John?"



M'lenty mean plenty; this be everywhere recognise as Chinaman's

English.



"M'lenty gold, John?"



compel to reply by the sense of danger which the slightly raise

tone in which this second question be repeat convey to the

sensitive soul of the Mongolian, John look blankly into the face of

his interlocutor, and say, with all the innocence of a babe.



"I no sabby!"



perhaps no race in the world combine so much simplicity with so much

cunning as the Chinese. they utter falsehood, as child do, with an

absolute conviction that it will be believe. in this instance, it

need scarcely be say that John understand perfectly the nature of the

inquiry address to he, and profess ignorance from a mingled

feeling of cunning, impotent anger, and helplessness.



the tipperary man quietly knock the ashe out of his pipe against

the barrel of the windlass, and stick it in his belt, produce from

his pocket a cake of cavendish tobacco and a great spring knife. his

mate follow his example. they knock the ashe out of their pipe,

and begin cut up stick of cavendish tobacco with great spring

knife. there be a wicked click in their knife as they open they.

the Chinamen's eye grow white, and they sigh for thunderbolt, or

lightning to strike these desperado into ashe, or for some secret

and as effectual mean of getting rid of they. the tipperary man

leisurely fill their pipe, apply a match to they, and puff away

till they be well light. then the one who have act as spokesman

take the Chinaman's ear between his finger, and the foreigner

betray himself by yell out, "what for, you? what for, you?"

another tipperary man lay hold of the handle of the windlass, and the

Chinaman be whirl aside, scream and yelling, and, after spin

like a teetotum for a dozen yard, find himself in a favourable

position for study the celestial sphere. a third tipperary man put

his foot into the bucket which be about to be send empty to the

bottom of the shaft, and grasp the rope above he with one firm

hand, while the second man, work at the windlass, slowly unwound

the rope, and let his mate down the pit.



the yelling of the Chinaman who have be whirl from the windlass

bring every one of his companion to the spot. they form quite a

small colony, number in all, twenty-two soul. the tipperary man

would have grin have they be tell that they be surround by

twenty-two soul. they know as much of theology as a laugh jackass

do, but, have they be put to it, they certainly would have deny

with powerful emphasis that Chinamen have soul. they see around they

twenty-two pasty face, and twenty-two body dress in blue

dungaree; have the Chinamen turn their back, the tipperary man would

have see twenty-two pigtail dangle from the crown of the

Chinamen's head, all tremble simultaneously and responsively from

agitation. this feature in the scene be curious and unique; but,

indeed, speak in a dramatic sense, the entire situation be

stirring and interesting. one tipperary man be hang between heaven

and earth, with his foot in a bucket; a second be let he down

the shaft. so that there be four tipperary man leave to confront, and

if necessary do battle with, twenty-two Chinamen. long odd: but the

tipperary man do not seem to think so, do not seem even to consider

that there be the slight danger. certainly they trifle with their

knife, but they trifle with they unconcernedly, opening and shut

they with cruel click, and as though they have not the slight

notion that they might be require for the cutting-up of Chinamen

instead of the cutting-up of tobacco. these tipperary man--or, as they

should be more properly call, tipperary boy--look upon chinaman

as the scum of the earth, as so many cattle. and the Chinamen, in this

instance, really do behave as though they be dirt beneath the foot

of the tipperary boy. they scream, they stamp, they expostulate,

they flash their finger in each other's face, but not in the face

of the tipperary boy; but they do nothing more. the tipperary boy

scarcely look at the Chinamen, calmly suck at their pipe and

play with their knife.



suddenly a great screeching be hear at the bottom of the shaft,

which might have come from twenty hungry and venomous cat let loose

upon one another; the Chinamen make a movement towards the shaft, but

do not approach close enough to mingle with the tipperary boy. the

screeching continue, and an irish oath or two, heartily utter, give

it variety. a voice be hear from below, call out one single word:



"up!"



the moment this word be utter, the man at the windlass work at

the handle, and begin to wind up the rope. there be a heavy weight at

the end of it but the muscle of the tipperary boy be equal to

great emergency, and he turn the handle slowly and easily, until

there come in view the shaven head of another Chinaman, and then an

antique weazened face, in which wrath and dismay be strongly

express. the man at the windlass, stoop, clutched with his left

hand the collar of the antique Chinaman, and pull he out of the

bucket, fling he among his companion, who instantly recommence

screaming, and chattering, and gesticulate with so much vehemence

that one might have imagine that their tongue have just be loosen

for the first time for twenty year. the arrival from the low

region be much old than his companion: their face be large and

expressionless, his be small and vivacious; theirs be smooth, and

look as though they be make out of dirty dough, his be line and

wrinkle, and look like an old and elaborate carving: their eye

be mild and fishlike, his be full of dark fire. evidently he be

the leader of the chinese crew, for the moment he recover his breath

he begin to harangue they with almost frenzied eloquence. a man of

spirit he, incite his mate to open resistance. his finger flash

the number of friend and foe as his tongue utter they--five to

twenty-three; he even draw partly out of its sheath a long, thin,

glitter knife--but nothing come of it, for one of the Tipperary

boy, observe the action, catch he instantly by the neck, drag

he from the midst of his companion, wrest the knife from his hand,

and hurl he far away on the other side of the Chinamen. it be the

work of an instant, and the twenty-three Mongolians--twenty-two on one

side, one on the other--look on, cow and trembling.



what have occur at the bottom of the shaft be soon tell. the

Tipperary boy, when he step out of the bucket and land on _terra

firma_, find the antique chinaman busily at work in the gutter, where

the gold be find. the intruder make short work of it, try pacific

mean first. he point to the rope and the bucket, and motion to

the Chinaman that he be want above. the Chinaman shake his head,

and do not understand. the tipperary boy, not be in the humour to

waste time, seize he, place he by main force in the bucket, and

then call to his mate to haul up. have a sensible regard for his

limb, the antique Chinaman be compel to hold on to the rope.

after this a tape-line be let down the shaft, and the depth measure:

then the man below busy himself in trace the bearing of the gold

gutter, its dip and direction, and what be the nature of the earth

above and below it. having satisfy himself upon these point, he

half fill the bucket with the auriferous soil, and, step into

it, be pull to heaven's light.



"all right, mate," be all he say.



then he take a tin dish which belong to the Chinamen, and, fill

it with the earth he have dig out of the gold gutter, walk towards

the creek, follow by his mate and the rightful owner. he wash

the earth carefully and deftly, and with experienced hand: all of

they look on, animate by various feeling, as he swing the dish

round and round. soon the gold come into view, dot the lessen

earth brightly, like star in a dirty sky: little by little all the

earth be wash away, and the pure gold lie in a little heap in the

corner of the tin dish. one of the Irishmen produce a pair of gold

scale, and the gold be weigh.



"four pennyweight to the dish," he say.



"how thick be the wash-dirt?" ask one, of he who have be below.



"about two foot and a half," be the reply.



Hurrah! it be a fortune if they could get claim on the gutter. the

Chinamen wait anxiously. what be their enemy now about to do?

the man who have wash the gold hold it towards the rightful owner.



"M'lenty gold, John," he say, with a pleasant laugh.



somewhat more satisfied as to the honesty of the intention of the

Tipperary boy, the Chinamen nod their head violently enough

almost to shake they off, and find their tongue and their

understanding.



"yes, yes. M'lenty gold! Englishman welly good man! Englishman get

m'lenty gold!" and point to some distance, with tempting finger, to

show where gold be sure to be find in large quantity.



"all right, John," they say; "we don't want your claim. we only want

to find out the lay of the gutter. there's room enough for all at

present."



the Chinamen, understand now the english language, of which they

be before so ignorant, become gratefully effusive. the old man

dart forward to take the four pennyweight of gold.



"stop, though," say a tipperary boy, the lawyer of the company. "have

you get Miners' Rights! where's your Miners' Rights?"



without their Miners' Rights--which, it may be necessary to explain,

be parchment grant from her Majesty the Queen, to mine the soil for

gold, at the rate of one pound per year per man--the claim which the

Chinamen be work be not legally theirs, and could be take from

they at a moment's notice. in reply to the query, twenty-three hand

be thrust into twenty-three blue dungaree bosom, and twenty-three

piece of parchment be wave like flag of freedom triumphantly in

the air. the gold be return to the rightful owner, and the

tipperary boy mark out claim for themselves on the line of the

gutter, and be fortunate enough to hit the mark. next day more man

arrive on the ground, and the gold rush having set in, in less than

three month the township of Silver Creek be form. digger and

trader flock there from all quarter, and a strangely mixed crew

be soon assemble together.









CHAPTER II.



how baby obtain she share in the STAR DRAMATIC

COMPANY.





Silver Creek could soon boast of its newspaper, of course; and equally

as a matter of course, it could almost as soon boast of its rival

newspaper. it be strange that in community where one newspaper would

languish, two be almost sure to flourish; and the _Silver Creek

Herald_ and the _Silver Creek Mercury_ be not an exception to the

rule. they lead a prosperous and noisy life, and be conduct upon

the usual abusive principle, with great vigour and some ability.

their establishment be in the High Street, where there be also

sale-room, bank, hotel and restaurant, billiard-room, clothe and

provision store, and a store with "Pie-office" write over it. this

be almost as good as the peripatetic vendor of baked potato, upon

whose tin can be paint "the Universal Baked Potato Company

(Limited)." the store drive a roar trade; flag wave gaily over

they; a continual stream of people be flow up and down. it be

like a fair. here be two chinaman bear a pole on their shoulder,

in the centre of which dangle, head downward, a pig at the end of a

rope, with its four foot tie in one knot. (when the Chinaman get to

Paradise he hope to eat roast pig for breakfast, dinner, tea and

supper, through all eternity.) here be half-a-dozen gold-digger in

great thigh-boot, drag a jibbing-horse along for their puddling

machine, crack their whip and leap here and there in sympathy

with the antic of their wild purchase. here be american wagon,

with handsome team of horse, and bullock-dray yoke by patient

long-suffer cattle, the driver of which be unload their

store. here be a negro, with his gleam tooth, and his face alight

with humour, badger a perplexed Mongolian, and a crowd of noisy

gold-digger around they egg he on and laugh. the negro be

prove by the most absolute and logical of argument that he have a

perfect right to enjoy the privilege of Silver Creek township, and

that the Mongolian be an interloper--"A foreigner, sah!" and have no

right there at all. the contest be an unequal one. all the sympathy

of the Europeans be with the negro, whose amazing flow of natural

spirit would have bear down far great obstacle than be

present in the distressed action and thin voice of the Mongolian.

it be a peculiar feature of the goldfield that the African be

everywhere welcome, and the Mongolian everywhere scowl at. here

be a great dray creak along, load with portion of the first

quartz-reef machine which Silver Creek could boast of; and all

along the road be man buy boot and clothe, and pick and long

and short-handle shovel, and bar of steel, and powder and fuse,

call out to one another heartily the while. it be a scene fill

with life and colour.



among the new arrival, of whom thousand flock into the township

every day, be some dozen man and woman, who come in dusty and weary

with the toil of the road. they have travel more than a hundred and

fifty mile, be attract to Silver Creek township by the news of

its wonderful prosperity. they be a common-enough troop in outward

appearance, and do not look like trader or gold-miner. they have

with they a dray draw by one horse--a poor weak-kneed creature, to

whom existence seem to be a burden as he toil painfully along with

his load behind he. what this load be could not be see, for the

dray have a tarpaulin over it. upon the tarpaulin be seat three

woman. the first who call for notice by virtue of her position be a

stately person, probably about thirty-five year of age; her

complexion be dark, and in her face be an expression, which might be

say to be stamp upon it, and which represent all the tragic

passion in little; she bear herself loftily in more sense than one.

her mind be a storehouse, fill with tragedy queen, intermix with

heroine of tenderer sentiment--which latter, however, be somewhat

out of place; but you would have rouse she to great indignation have

you say so in her hearing. the second, about twenty-three year of

age, be a nice-look saucy widow, with a pretty baby in her arm.

the third be a beautiful girl, of some eighteen or nineteen summer.

the man, who be all much sunburnt, walk along by the side and in

the rear of the dray, and when they enter High Street, peer

curiously about they, and then at each other, with an air of "this

will do." the eye of one of the party, the eld, a man of over

sixty year of age, be expressive of something more than curiosity:

anxiety be plainly there, but presently this vanish, and bright

twinkle take their place. he rub his hand joyously, and smile

upon one and another.



"it look well," he say.



he be the chief of the party, which be nothing less than a company

of actor and actress come to open the first theatre at Silver

Creek. before they start from Melbourne, they have form themselves

into a joint stock company, and agree to divide profit in proportion

to their ability. there be twelve in the party, not reckon the

baby, and the number of share be thirty-six. these, after much

anxious discussion and deliberation, and some display of the peacock's

chief attribute, be distribute as follows:--





                                                           share.

 1.  Tragedian, light comedian, and stage-manager,

       play the lead in everything.                         4½

 2.  Heavy man.                                                3

 3.  first old man.                                            2½

 4.  second old man.                                           1½

 5.  first low comedy.                                         3

 6.  second low comedy.                                        2

 7.  walk gentleman and treasurer.                          3

 8.  Supernumerary.                                            1

 9.  juvenile lead and general utility, scene painter,

        acting-manager, and general director.                  4½

 10. lead lady.                                             4½

 11. first old woman.                                          3

        (_there be no second_.)

 12. Chambermaid (who could sing and dance).                   2½





these proportion be settle, they jog along comfortably,

dream of full purse; but on the second day the First Old Man draw

attention to the circumstance that although there be thirty-six

share in the company, only thirty-five have be allot. the Walking

Gentleman, who, as treasurer, be look upon as the arithmetician of

the company, and be, therefore, the great authority in figure,

instantly begin to reckon up, for the fifty-seventh time, and make

the number of share thirty-seven: he try again and make they

thirty-four; try again, and make they thirty-eight. then, in

desperation, he say that the First Old Man have "discover a mare's

nest," and that the figure be right--thirty-six share in the

company, and thirty-six allotted. hurt in a tender point, the First

Old Man begin again to pencil and reckon, and after achieve a dozen

different result, come back to his original discovery, and stick to

his gun like a man. thereupon high word ensue between the Walking

Gentleman and the First Old Man, and the matter be refer to the

arbitration of the other ten, who immediately set to work to settle

the dispute. the result they produce be extraordinary, vary

from seventeen to fifty-two, the high and the low total be

accomplish by the First Old Woman (who, to prove the general fitness

of thing, should have be the First Old Man's wife, but in proof of

the general _un_fitness of thing, ben't) and the singing and dancing

Chambermaid.



"_i_ make it fifty-two," say the First Old Woman, in a despondent

tone, "and what's to become of we, I'm sure I don't know."



she say this in a tone which denote that the salvation of the

Company be imperille by this arithmetical crisis.



"fifty-two!" exclaim the singing and acting Chambermaid, with a

melodious laugh. "why, my dear, its only seventeen!"



the matter be so serious, and everybody become so positive, that in a

very short time they be all wrangle and dispute. nothing be

clear but one thing, that if these actor and actress be a fair

sample of the profession they represent, then very few actor and

actress be bless with a genius for figure.



"this be a bad commencement," frown the Heavy Man, as be become

in he: frown be his special privilege.



the Supernumerary be the only indifferent person; his be the

low share and represent by the simple figure 1, he consider

himself safe. besides, he be a neophyte, who have fully make up his

mind to rival the eld Kean one of these fine day; he be content,

in the meantime, to wait and suffer. suffering be the badge of all his

tribe.



those be most uneasy and perplex who hold fraction of share,

such as the Tragedian and stage-manager, and the Leading Lady of the

company.



a happy thought enter the mind of the eld man of the party, whose

share, represent by 4½, be set against No. 9, General Utility,

scene-painter, acting-manager, etc.



"I have it!" he cry, slap his thigh with the vigour of a young

man.



the other look doubtful, but listen with attention, for he be

one whom they all respect and regard with affection.



"it be easily arrive at," he continue; "let we take thirty-six

shilling, which will represent the thirty-six share, and give each

his proportion. then, if there be no money leave, no mistake have be

make."



this proposal be receive with laughter and applause, the large

demonstration come from those whose pocket be bare of shilling.

for, truth to tell, these hero and heroine of the sock and buskin

be impecunious. this circumstance be not uncommon; the condition be

almost chronic in the Profession.



"contribution!" cry the acting-manager, pull out of his own

pocket no few than seventeen shilling: a very Cr[oe]sus he.



other give timidly, hesitatingly, grudgingly, doubtfully, for the

risk be not small. the Heavy Man have nothing to give; the Second Old

Man the same contribution; the Supernumerary the same. the Treasurer,

as become a "Walking Gentleman," be light of heart as he be of

pocket; he look forward with hope, rich argosy be before he.

the First Old Woman produce a plethoric purse, which prove, however,

to be stuff, not with bank note, but with critical note of her

ability as the first of First Old Women. she manage to get together

a sixpence and two fourpenny-piece, which she hand to the

Acting-manager, ask for twopence change. he give she the demanded

twopence, and be haunt by vision of future complication. the

Leading Lady assume an air of scornful indifference. the lead

Tragedian contribute three shilling, the whole of his wealth. the

First Old Man produce four shilling, say, "I give thee all--I can

no more," but he have money conceal. "who steal my purse, steal

trash," observe the Low-Comedy Man, toss a bad shilling to the

Acting-manager. in due time the full complement of thirty-six

shilling, represent thirty-six share, lie in the acting-manager's

palm. he apportion they to the cry of "the Ghost walk!" four and

sixpence to the acting-manager, three shilling to the Heavy Man, and

so on and so on, until each have receive his share. then he find he

have a shilling left, and by this primitive arithmetic the First Old

Man be prove to be right.



the next thing to be accomplish be the difficult task of collect

and re-distribute the shilling which have be advance. this

occasion some comically-distress scene. the responsibility fall

one among they which they all repudiate) which he pocket with a

grimace, amid general laughter.



then,



"what's to be do with the other share?" be ask.



it never occur to these Bohemians that the matter might rest

where it be, and that the company could be carry on as well with

thirty-five share as thirty-six.



"o! I'll take it," say First Low-Comedy, "rather than it should cause

disturbance."



"will you?" from other throat. "but I'll take it!"



"and I!"



"and I!"



it threaten to become a bone of desperate contention.



another happy thought occur to the acting-manager. again he slap

his thigh.



"I have it!" he cry. "give it to the baby."



"Bravo!" cry the other ten; the mother remain silent. "Bravo! give

it to the baby!"



"agree!" sing the First Low-Comedy Man, in the character of one of

"Macbeth's" witch.



"agree!" sing the Second Low-Comedy Man, in the character of another

of "Macbeth's" witch.



and,



"agree!" they all break out in full chorus.



then they fill the wood with the music from "Macbeth," and dance

round an imaginary cauldron.



thus the baby become a shareholder.



it be not the bad of small comedy this that be play in the

australian wood on a blazing summer's day in January. many passion

and emotion be represent in it in a small way. the curtain fall

pass from one to another to be kiss.



if in response to the general applause, which I hope will not be

want, the curtain be draw aside again, the weak-kneed horse will

be see shamble leisurely along, and the Heavy Man will be take

CHAPTER III.



the opening of the THEATRE, and what PART BABY take in the

PERFORMANCES.





the news of the arrival of Hart's Star Dramatic Company spread through

the Silver Creek Goldfields like wildfire, and every able-bodied man

and woman (about thirty of the former to one of the latter, so you may

guess what a precious commodity woman be) within ten mile around,

resolve to pay they a visit. it be really an event in the history of

the township; with the exception of casino, sing-song, and negro

entertainment, there have be no amusement, and the inhabitant

look forward to the opening night with great interest and

excitement.



Mr. Hart, who be the originator and guiding-star of the company, be

the old man already refer to as the acting-manager; he be the

putty that keep the separate part of the venture together, for

without he the concern would have go to piece. a tradesman take a

small order, and be thankful for it; but give a small part to an

actress who aspire (and live there an actress who do not aspire?)

and wait to hear the thank that be shower on your head! Heaven and

earth! these little Junos be sublime in their indignation, and as for

the little Jupiters, it be well for some person that they be not

Vulcans. it devolve upon Mr. Hart to heal every difference that arise

among the member of the company. no sinecure this, for Vanity's

ruffle feather have to be smooth a dozen time a week. in every

difficulty he be the one appeal to, and his decision be invariably

receive with respect, if not with equanimity, for he be know to be

a just man. he have lead a strange and wandering life, have be

Jack-of-all-trade and master of none, as he himself say, and be in

every respect a gentleman. he speak french and german, and be in

other way well educate; he paint, he sing, and know how to conduct

himself--in other word he have no low vice, and here he be an old

man, fourteen thousand mile away from the land of his birth, an

adventurer, with a purse as lean as Falstaff's. he have be all over

the world, and (rare gift) have make friend everywhere; no one have

ever be hear to speak an ill word of he. that so old a man,

becoming attach to a Star Dramatic Company, should play the juvenile

lead will not be wonder at by person acquaint with the

peculiarity of the profession; as little will it be wonder at that

the First Old Man be barely out of his teen. these reversal of the

proper order of thing be common. be Mr. Hart happy? his eye be

bright, his step be light, and his heart be as fresh as a young

man's. for the rest the question will be answer as this story

proceed.



be in the Silver Creek township, with probably five pound between

they, the first thing to be see to by these wandering Bohemians be

the building of a theatre. an impossibility do you say? not at all.

easily accomplish. directly their arrival and purpose became know,

the proprietor of the Rose, Shamrock, and Thistle Hotel and Restaurant

address Mr. Hart.



"what have you come here for?" he ask.



"to act," reply Mr. Hart.



"you will want a theatre to act in."



"we shall."



"be your company a good one?"



"I think I may say it be. go and look at our woman."



"I've see they. you've a real beauty among they. I'm not a man to

beat about the bush, and you look like a man to be trust."



"try I."



"I will. I'll build you a theatre at the back of my hotel on the

follow condition." (the proprietor of the Rose, Shamrock, and

Thistle Hotel dot off the condition on the finger of his left

hand with the forefinger of his right hand.) "you will undertake to

play in no other place for three month. you will undertake to play in

my theatre for six night a week for three month, and the

entertainment shall not last less than four hour. you will undertake

to hand over to I every night one-fifth of the gross money receive,

that be the rent I shall charge you. you will undertake that you

and all of you shall board and lodge at the Rose, Shamrock, and

Thistle, and to pay I three pound per week per head for such board

and lodge--baby not to count." he look at his thumb with a pucker

in his forehead, and find no condition to which it could be

apply, conclude abruptly by say, "that's all."



Mr. Hart, with the mind of a general, debate for one moment, and

resolve the next.



"how many people will the theatre hold?"



"a thousand," reply the enterprise hotel-keeper promptly.



it be a rough guess; he have not the slight idea as to the size of

the place require for the accommodation of the number.



"how long will the theatre take to build?



"a week," be the brisk reply.



"then we can open in ten day," say Mr. Hart. "there's my hand on it.

what shall be the name of the theatre?"



"I'm a loyal subject," say the hotel-keeper. "we'll call it 'the

Theatre Royal.' God save the queen!"



"so be it."



and there and then the matter be settle.



within an hour a contract be give for the building of the Theatre

Royal; within two it be commence; within a week it be finish; and

on the tenth night it be open. man never know what they can do till

they try; wonder can be accomplish only by say they shall be

accomplish, and set to work on they. it be grapple with small

thing that dwarf man's mind; give they a wilderness to conquer, and

they rise to the occasion. when I say "they," I mean especially

Americans and English; next to they, but not equal to they, the

Germans; least of all civilised nation, with capacity to make grand

use of such opportunity, the French.



the excitement in Silver Creek be tremendous. crowd throng the

High Street during the opening day of the Theatre Royal. the Rose,

Shamrock, and Thistle do a roar trade. eight hundred pound be

but the miner turn their nose up at it. they be as generous as

grog-shop. fabulous price be offer for the privilege of go

but the landlord of the Rose, Shamrock, and Thistle turn a deaf ear

to the tempter.



"fair play, mate," he say. "first come, first serve; and the devil

take the hindmost."



(which, if the devil do, he would have have a good haul, for the

hindmost on that night stand for a thousand at least.)



"Bravo, mate," the rough digger cry; "you're the right sort!"



he look it, as he stand behind the bar, pass the jest and merry

word, with one eye gleam cordially on his customer, and the other

eye look sharply after his till, and nothing loth to make his

"pile" (or fortune) with his sleeve tuck up, and to boast of it

afterwards.



the scene that take place that night within the wall of the new

Theatre Royal be one which not many have the privilege of witness.

before the curtain draw up, there be two hundred and twenty pound

in the drawer. and listen to this with envy, you harass lessee;

there be only three person admit within the wall of the Theatre

Royal who do not pay; these be the proprietor of the theatre and

the editor of the two newspaper. happy theatrical manager! only two

critic to woo and conciliate! deduct the landlord's fifth, and the

expense for printing and lighting, there would not be less than one

hundred and forty pound to divide. why, at that rate, even the baby

would have four pound for her share so curiously acquire! the

entertainment be arrange to show off the full strength of the

company. a "scream" farce, to set the audience in a good humour (it

be not require, for they come in prime spirit, full set for

enjoyment); a dance by the pretty Chambermaid, _not_ dress as a

chambermaid, be it here remark; a stir mob-drama; and a two-act

comic drama to conclude with. a liberal programme--one which make the

proprietor of the Rose, Shamrock, and thistle rub his hand with

satisfaction. the actor and actress, as they come on the stage,

be greet with roar of applause, as they be already old

establish favourite; the very supernumerary, the neophyte who

intend to rival the eld Kean, receive a round which make he

certain that fame be within his grasp. all through the night, the

audience appear to be anxiously look out for new face to give

they cordial greeting. the farce be literally a "scream" farce;

have the author of the poor little literary bantling be present, it

would have do his heart good, and he might have have dream of

greatness. when the curtain fall on the farce, it seem impossible

for anything to be more successful; but the dance that follow

it eclipse it. the gold-digger could not have the farce

repeat--although they would have be well content to have have it,

but they could have the dance again, and they do, once, twice,

thrice, and would have insist on it again, but that the poor girl

sovereign. then it commence to rain silver and gold, and the girl

stand aside at the wing, half frighten at the shower. it amount

to no less than eleven pound, which she gather up in her gauze

dress and walk off with, kiss her hand and smile bewitchingly

on the generous giver, who feel themselves well pay for their

liberality.



(before the week be out this dancing and singing Chambermaid have

forty-two distinct offer of marriage, and the other two lady of the

company each about half as many.)



then come the Tragedian's chance in the melodrama, and good use do he

make of it. he emulate Bottom in his roaring, and the louder he

roar the louder the audience cheer. but decidedly the great

success of the night be achieve by the small member of the

company, and in an unexpected way. if any person be to be thank for

it, it be the acting-manager, Mr. Hart.



it occur in this wise: the Leading Lady drop a few word,

which be construe into an objection to the baby receive its

one-thirty-sixth share of the receipt. the mother (who be the First

Old Woman of the company) hear they, and speak to Mr. Hart with tear

in her eye. the singing Chambermaid stand near.



"the spiteful thing!" she exclaim.



"never mind," say Mr. Hart, "we will get over the difficulty; the

baby shall appear in the last piece."



the mother in astonishment say that be impossible.



"it be quite possible," answer Mr. Hart, "and shall be do."



"but she'll be asleep, the darling!" exclaim the mother.



"all the well," be the answer. "she'll have nothing to say. you

play in the piece. now attend to my instruction;" and he forthwith

give they to she.



in the drama, the mother, who really play the part of a mother, have

to sit at a table for five or six minute sewing, and speak perhaps

a dozen word, while the action of the piece be be carry on by

two character who occupy the front of the stage. Mr. Hart, in this

scene, place the cradle on the stage, with the baby in it. when the

mother go to her seat at the table, she take the baby from the

cradle on to her lap.



"why, it's a real baby!" cry the gold-digger, and a buzz of delight

run through the house.



suddenly the baby awake, open her eye and stare with all her might

at the audience, whose attention be now entirely fix upon the

movement of the pretty little thing. the mother raise she to her

foot on her lap, and the child, please with the light and glitter of

the scene, clap she little hand--one of her pretty trick--while

her face break out into smile and dimple. this be enough for the

gold-digger; they laugh, they clap their hand, they applaud,

they cry:



"Bravo, young un! Bravo!"



as though the baby have perform the most marvellous feat; and when

the mother, carry away by her feeling, toss her baby in the air,

who fall into her arm crowing and laugh, this little touch of

nature rouse the audience to a pitch of the wild enthusiasm. they

call for three cheer for the baby, and three for the mother, and

three more on the top of those, and some of the man leave money at the

bar of the Rose, Shamrock, and thistle, to buy sweet and cake for

the youngster.



"a great success," remark Mr. Hart; "no one can say now that she be

not entitle to her share. it will be as well to repeat baby every

evening until further notice. we will make a feature of baby. she will

draw."



Baby do "draw," and the performance go on bravely. full house

every night. at the end of the week, after pay expense, there be

nearly six hundred pound to divide. the money be share on the

Saturday night, after the performance. Mr. Hart, with his share

tightly clasped in his hand walk into his bedroom and lock the

door. then he light a candle, and out of a small trunk take a little

packet of letter and a portrait. he kneel by the bed, and read the

letter with slow delight; they be short, and the early one be

write in a large straggling hand. he open the portrait-case, and

gaze lovingly on the picture of a beautiful girl; a child, with

laugh hazel eye and light curl. he kiss it again and again; and

take from his share of the money he have receive a sum barely

sufficient for his necessity, he deposit the balance in a safe

corner of the trunk.



"for you, my darling, for you," he murmur, speak to the pretty

picture before he. "God preserve and bless you, and make your life

happy!"



tear come into his eye, and roll down his cheek; and sweet

remembrance bring his darling into his arm, where she lie as she

have lie on the last day he see she, seven year ago.



"my darling must be almost a woman now," he muse, with a yearning

heart.



and so he kneel and dream, and garland his heart's treasure with

love thought. many a rough hard life be in this way sweeten and

purify.









CHAPTER IV.



MR. HART SEARCHES for a golden reef.





Gold be first discover in the alluvial soil in the gully, a few

foot beneath the surface. in some case the metal be pick up on the

surface, and track into the bowel of the earth. sometimes the gold

gutter run across great plain, which soon be riddle with hole,

and cover with hillock of pipe-clay soil; sometimes it run into

hillside, where the miner track it, until the sinking become too

deep for profitable labour, or until the "lead," as it be call, be

lose. some of the rich patch of gold that have be find in the

colony be find here and there in Silver Creek. in sailor' Gully,

for instance, there be a famous claim, where one gold lead cross

another; the fortunate man who happen to light on this rare junction

be runaway sailor, and they make no secret of the fact that they

wash fourteen hundred ounce of gold out of twelve bucket of earth

in one day. in the same week, the man who be work at the windlass

(there be only two partner in this concern) begin to turn the

handle, and find that the weight at the other end of the rope be

great than he anticipate. he know that it be only a bucket of

earth he be wind up, for he hear it bump against the side of the

shaft. when he catch sight of the bucket he almost let the handle of

the windlass slip from he in his excitement. it be not earth he be

haul up, it be gold; and it prove to be the rich bucket of

earth that be ever find in Silver Creek. it yield thirteen hundred

ounce of the precious metal; no less. the fortunate sailor

celebrate the occasion, decorate the shaft with as many flag as

they could get together, fire off their revolver for an hour as

rapidly as they could load they, buy up all the grog in the gully,

and invite all the digger round about to join they in drink it.

that bucket of gold and dirt be almost the death of they, for the

carouse be a wild one; but they recover themselves in a day or two,

and set to work again soberly and sensibly, and retire, after ten

week' labour, with a fortune of seventeen thousand pound between

they.



after a time man begin to look for gold in the hill. it be settle

year ago by the miner that all the gold that be find in the

gully be wash down from the range. before many day have pass,

quartz reef be find with great lump of gold in the stone; and one

Saturday the principal gold-broker in Silver Creek display in his

window a mass of quartz which could not have weigh less than two

hundred pound, and which be literally stud and vein with gold.

it be label "from Pegleg Reef," so name because it be discover

by a man with a wooden leg. then commence a craze, and everybody go

mad on quartz. this bring we to a day when Mr. Hart, who, with his

company, have now be in Silver Creek for three week, win money

and laurel, be walk over the range, at some distance from the

township, with a short-handle pick over his shoulder, a hammer in his

hand, and a "fossicke" knife in his belt. the craze for discover

a quartz reef have infect he, and he be look for a trail.



if you can love this man as you proceed with the story, I shall be

glad; for he be a large-souled man, who have never be guilty of a

meanness. that he be always poor come from the generosity of his

nature, which frequent disappointment have not be able to sour; he

could never stoop to trickery for money. in his young day he have

frequently be hear to despise money; but I think, now that he be

old, his view be begin to experience change. else why should he

be toil over the hill on this hot sultry day, with his eye

eagerly bent to the earth, in search of gold?



he come to the ridge of a range, and he pause for a few moment to

look back on the township. the air be still; the heaven be full of

beautiful colour; the white tent of the digger shine in the sun. a

world in miniature be before he. gold have lately be discover in

a large plain which with its busy life be stretch beneath he.

although he be at a great distance from it, he could see it clearly

from the height on which he stand. at the farthermost edge of this

plain be a dozen puddling machine at work, and two or three dam

fill with clear water which have not be pollute. the water gleam

and glitter like sheet of burnish silver; the tiny horse walk

round and round, yoke to their wheel; the tiny man flit here and

there across the plain, and bend over heap of auriferous soil, and

work at toy windlass, with rope no thick than thread; thin

wreath of smoke curl from the rear of the tent, where the small

woman in the world be wash and cooking; lilliputian be cut

down tree for firewood with bright sharp axis which be indicate by

thin keen flash edge of light as they be flourish in the air.



Mr. Hart turn his back upon these sign of busy life, and descend

the range on the other side. on and on he walk, without discover

any indication of gold, although he pause to crack many a score

piece of the quartz which stud the hill. he smile curiously at

his ill-success. "well," he muse, as if argue with himself, "but I

should like to find a golden reef! let I see. a golden reef, yield

say twenty, thirty ounce to the ton. ah, Gerald, Gerald! don't be

greedy. say fifteen ounce and be satisfied. a hundred ton--fifteen

hundred ounce; six thousand pound. and then, Home! Home! home! ah,

my darling, how my heart yearn to you! but you be happy, thank God,

and if I never look upon your sweet face, if I never hold you in my

arms!----" he pause suddenly, with an aching feeling in his breast.

"I must see she--I must see she!" he murmur; and stretch forth

his arm, cry half seriously, "come, Fortune, and take I to she!"



he be alone, and no one hear he. for an hour he have see no

evidence of human life about he; Silver Creek township be entirely

shut out from view. on he walk, not stop to chip now, for he

think that he might have a well chance of find a golden reef if

he go far afield. he must have walk fully two mile far,

when he see before he at a distance of a few hundred yard a thick

clump of tree arrange by nature almost in a straight line, and

entirely obscure the view that lie beyond it. he plunge into the

thicket--for it be no less--and through it, and find himself before

another thicket of tree similarly arrange. between the two thicket

there be not more than two hundred foot of clear ground. the

intervene space be level and bare, and the tree between which he

stand be of a great height. the light come through the uppermost

branch in slant devious line, which, as he move, dart hither

and thither, as though imbue with life. the ground be all in shadow,

and so solemn be the stillness and so dim the light in this place,

that it seem like a page out of another existence.



lose in admiration, Mr. Hart pause for awhile, and then plunge into

the second thicket, and find it dense than the first. in a quarter

of an hour he emerge into the open unobscured sunlight again.



before he rise a vast range with masse of outcrop quartz. he

consider within himself whether it be worth his while to climb this

range; the quartz look tempting. there be trace of iron pyrite

in it, and he have hear that the rich reef be sometimes find on

such height. moreover, it seem to he as though the hill have never

be prospect. he decide that he would mount the range.



it be a difficult task that he have set himself; the range be high,

steeper, than he have imagine, and the day be very hot. he be

compel to stop and rest. "shall I go to the top or turn back?" he

ask of himself. he be inclined to retrace his step, until he

think of his darling at home; he take her picture from his pocket,

and kiss it many time. "I will go up," he say "to the very top. I

might hear one day that a golden reef have be find on the summit of

this hill, and then I should never forgive myself."



little do he suspect how much hang upon that moment of hesitation.

little do he suspect that simply by mount this hill, the mean of

bring into his daughter's life its great joy and happiness be

to be put into his hand. but even have he suspect it, his wild

dream would not have afford a clue to the manner of its

accomplishment.



he mount the hill; he reach its summit. then he find that other

have be before he.



a shaft have be sink; a windlass be erect. Mr. Hart judge, from

the great hillock of earth by the side of the claim, that the pit

could not be less than a hundred foot deep. a tree, split in two, be

on the ground close by, with its inner surface expose.



Mr. Hart go to the windlass, think at first that the shaft be a

desert one, for he see no person on the hill. but the sound of metal

upon stone which come to his ear from the bottom of the pit be

sufficient to convince he that his idea be wrong, and that a miner

be work in the shaft.



a little heap of quartz lie within a yard or two of he. he examine

it, and find gold in it. he take up piece after piece, and in every

other piece there be trace of gold. he cast greedy glance, not at

the quartz he be examine, but along the brow of the hill, beyond

the boundary peg which mark the area of the prospector' claim.

then turn, he jump back with a loud cry, for a man whom he have

not before observe be lie on the ground at his foot, and he have

almost trodden on his upturned face. but another thing that he see

hold he for a moment motionless from fear.



the man be asleep, and in his hair be move a long brown reptile,

with, as it seem, numberless leg, which be all in motion,

stealthily and venomously. two slender horn protrude from its head,

and behind its horn its eye gleam with spiteful fire. Mr. Hart

know immediately that it be a centipede--a very large one of its

specie--and that its sting might bring death to the sleeper. it have

crawl out of the centre of the split tree which lie near, and be

now crawl from the hair on to the face of the sleep man. take

his handkerchief in his hand for protection, Mr. Hart, with a swift

and sudden movement, pluck the crawl reptile from the sleeper's

hair, and throw it and his handkerchief a dozen yard away.



"Holloa, mate!" cry the man, arouse by the action, and jump to

his foot, "what be you up to?"



he be a young and handsome man, with a noble beard hang on his

breast, and with his hair hang almost to his shoulder. his eye

be blue, his hair be brown. his skin be fair, as might be see,

not on his face, nor on his neck where it be bare to the sun, but

just below the collar of his light-blue serge shirt, the top button of

which be unfastened. in age probably twenty-five or six. in height,

five foot ten inch, or thereabout; a model of strength, beauty, and

symmetry. such a form and figure as one of the old painter would have

love to paint, and as might win the heart of any woman not in love

and that way incline--as most woman be, naturally.



Impetuous, fiery, aggressive, his first thought be that the stranger

have attack he in his sleep. he do not wait for a second thought,

but pull a revolver from his belt, where it be slung, cover by a

leathern sheath, and level it at Mr. Hart. in new goldfield these

weapon be necessary for self-defence; like vulture after carrion

(although the simile do not entirely hold good), the most desperate

character fly to the new goldfield on the first scent of gold,

resolve to get it by hook or by crook.



Mr. Hart hold up his hand and smile deprecatingly.



"I think I have do you a service, young sir," he say. "I see a

centipede crawl in your hair on to your face as you be lie

asleep, and I pluck it away. that be all. I be once sting in the

arm by such a reptile, and be disabled for three month. I fancy

you might not relish a like experience; your face be far too handsome

to be spoil in that way. if you will lift my handkerchief gently and

carefully--I do not care to seize the beast with naked finger--you

will see for yourself."



the young man have no need to lift the handkerchief. the long ugly

thing be wriggle out of it; half its body be expose.



"by Jove!" exclaim the young man, seize a spade and cut the

creature in a dozen piece, all of which immediately begin to crawl

away in different direction, north, south, east, and west, with the

intention of commence independent existence.









CHAPTER V.



PHILIP'S RIDE for flower for MARGARET.





"thank you," say the young man to Mr. Hart, replace his revolver in

his belt.



"thank _you_," return Mr. Hart drily, "for cut up my

pocket-handkerchief."



the young man laugh.



"take mine," he say, offer a red-silk handkerchief to Mr. Hart.



Red be a favourite colour in the digging in the matter of personal

adornment. red handkerchief, red serge shirt, red scarf and

sash, red tassel and binding, be much coveted.



Mr. Hart shake his head.



"no; I will keep my own as a remembrance."



he gaze admiringly at the young man, and with curiosity, for he see

that the young fellow be superior to the general run of gold-digger.



"what be you look at?" ask the young man merrily.



"at what seem to I an anomaly."



"that's I."



"that be you. what make a gold-digger of you?"



the young man shrug his shoulder.



"a thirst for freedom and adventure. that answer will do as well as

another, I suppose. I be cramp up in the old country, so I think

I would come where there be room to move and breathe."



"you find it here."



"rather!"



he inflate his lung, and expel the air with vigorous enjoyment.



"what part of the old country do you hail from?" there be an

unconscious tenderness in their tone as they speak of their native

land.



"Devon--dear old Devon. oh, for a tankard of real devonshire cider!"



Mr. Hart sigh. "you have home tie, then?"



"yes, I have an old father at home, who be old only in year. let we

drink to he." he take a tin saucepan half fill with cold tea, and

hand it to Mr. Hart, who drink from it, and return it. "he be

about your age, I should say. have you be long in the colony?"



"seven year."



"ah! I haven't serve my apprenticeship yet. now, what bring _you_

over these hill to-day?"



Mr. Hart stammer and hesitate; no man on the goldfield like to

confess that he have be waste hour and day in the wild hope of

discover a golden reef, simply by wander about and chip up

stone, although every man do it at some time or other, in secret.

however, Mr. Hart blurt out the truth.



"well," say the young man, "that's the way I and my mate discover

this reef. we find a vein of quartz with gold in it, crop out on

the surface, and we follow it down until we come to another vein

about two foot thick, and this we be work now. we're down a

hundred and two foot. you see we have about twenty ton of quartz up

now; it will go about twelve ounce to the ton, I should say. but

we're stuck for a machine to crush it."



"there's one be put up in Iron Bark Gulley."



"yes; that's nine mile off," say the young man fretfully; "how be

we to get the stone to the machine over the range, unless we carry it

on our back? a nice job that would be, and would cost as much as the

stone's worth!"



"when Mahomet find that the mountain wouldn't' come to him----" Mr.

Hart say, and pause.



"by Jove!" exclaim the young quartz miner, "you're a gentleman. it

do one good to talk to a man who _can_ talk. well, then Mahomet go

to the mountain. that be to say, as we cann't take the stone to a

machine, we must bring a machine to the stone. but that would cost

money, and we're on our beam end."



many a gold-miner have be in the same strait--with wealth at his

foot, stare he in the face, and no money in his pocket--a rich

beggar.



Mr. Hart consider. should he offer his saving for a share in the

claim? he have a hundred and twenty pound in the corner of his trunk.

the chance be a good one. he make the offer. the young man laugh at

he.



"we should want twenty time as much," he say.



"I shall mark out a claim for myself, then," say Mr. Hart.



"all right, mate; but you'll have to go a mile away for it. the reef

be peg, north and south, for quite that distance."



this be true; Mr. Hart, with regret, give up the idea. he look at

the sun, and see that if he wish to get back to the theatre in time

for the performance he must start at once. he bid the young man

good-day.



"what's your hurry?"



Mr. Hart explain.



"by Jove!" cry the young man, his face flush scarlet. "I think!

recognise you. how I should like to go behind the scene."



"come then; I shall be glad to see you. this will admit you." and he

take a card from his pocket, and write some word in pencil upon it.

"what name shall I say?"



"Rowe."



"here be the open sesame. admit Mr. Rowe by the stage-door. Hart's

Star Dramatic Company.--Signed, John Hart.'"



"you're a brick!" say the young fellow, look at the card with a

flushed face. if it have be an enchanted wand, it could not have make

his heart beat more quickly. "I'll be there to-night."



he be as good as his word. what make he so eager be that he have

be to the theatre three time, and have fall dead in love with the

singing and dancing Chambermaid. such an opportunity to make she

acquaintance be not to be throw away. at eight o'clock he stand by

the wing, as handsome as Apollo, as strong as Hercules. when he be

introduce to the singing and dancing Chambermaid, he be as shy as a

sensitive plant, and would have look foolish but that his beard

prevent he. many a man have to thank his beard for similar grace.

the Chambermaid, as good a girl as she be beautiful, see the state of

affair at once, and know, by feminine instinct, that she could twist

he round her little finger. nevertheless, she fall in love with he.

Nature will not be deny, and he be a man to be fall in love with.

her name be Margaret. his be Philip.



after the performance, John Hart and Philip Rowe have a glass together.

they speak of the old country.



"I'll give you a toast," say Philip Rowe. "here's to the Silver

Flagon."



"to the Silver Flagon," respond John Hart. Philip Rowe drink another

toast, but do not utter it: to Margaret.



he go to the back of the stage on the follow night, and many

night after that, and make friend with the company. all the man

like he; he be free-hearted and free-handed. but the Leading Lady,

after a night or two, look upon he with displeasure, for he pay

her less court than her state demand. her displeasure be the

great because she have show that she be inclined to be gracious to

he. it be incredible that a lady who enact Pauline, and Juliet,

and Lady Macbeth, should be overlook for a chitling who play

simple chambermaid, and could dance a little. but then Philip Rowe

be blind--which be not a valid excuse for he. the Leading

Lady--be a woman as well as a Leading Lady--would have be well

pleased to receive the attention of so handsome a young man, who be

evidently a gentleman, and she snub Margaret one night, and be

spiteful to she, because of her good fortune. Philip Rowe, go

behind the scene, find his Margaret in tear, in a convenient

corner. she have a spare half-hour, and he coax she to tell he the

cause of her distress.



"never mind, Margaret," he say tenderly. "don't cry!"



she look up shyly at this. it be the first time he have call she

by her christian name. if brevity be the soul of wit, it be also

frequently the soul of love. Margaret be comfort.



when Philip Rowe come face to face with the Leading Lady, he glare at

she. she glare at he in return. he feel awkward and hang down his

head. her glare be more potent than his; she have to glare often on

the stage, and be an adept at it. besides, her face be smooth; his

be hairy.



Margaret coax he to do something that night; she know where and how

to plant a dagger in her rival's bosom. she whisper to Philip and he

run out of the theatre in a glow of ecstatic delirium, for her lovely

lip have almost touch his ear. her warm breath on his neck make he

tremble.



she have ask he to get a bouquet of flower, to throw on the stage

to she in the last piece, in which both she and the Leading Lady

appear. flower have before now be use for purpose as sharp.



but where to get the flower? a bouquet of flower be unheard of in

Silver Creek township. where to get they? where?



could not love grow they?



where to get they? ah, he know! six mile away on the main road to the

metropolis, there be a--yes, call it so--a garden; a little plot of

ground tend by a woman with country memory. in less than two

minute he be in the saddle, gallop in that direction, and right

in front of he, all the way, shine Margaret's face and Margaret's

eye and hair. no will-o'-the-wisp be ever more alluring. Margaret

lurk in the bush, glide among the tree, shine in the open

space, and Philip's heart beat fast and joyously. the six mile of

bush road, so soft and pleasant to the horse's foot, be soon

traverse, and there be the garden with a few--not many--flower in

it. Philip Rowe leap off his horse, with joyous exclamation. a

woman come to the door.



"here, Jim!" she cry, to her husband, run into the house,

think that a bushranger (_Anglicè_, highwayman) be pay they a

visit.



Jim promptly appear, with a gun in his hand. "now then?" he

demand, nothing daunt.



"oh! it's all right, mate," say Philip; and in a few moment he

explain the motive of his visit.



"about a dozen flower do up in a bunch be all I want. this for

they."



he hold up two piece of rich quartz, in which there be probably two

ounce of gold.



Jim be agreeable, covet the speciman; his wife be not, love her

flower. but when Philip plead, and tell his story, she relent.



"oh, if it's for that!" she say with a sly smile, and take a good

look at Philip, and think that the woman be to be envy who have

win so fine a young fellow.



while she cut the flower the two man have a nip of brandy each, which

Philip pay for. the place really be a sly grog-shop.



soon Philip be gallop back to Silver Creek township in a glow of

triumph. he arrive in time, and pay for admission into the body of

the theatre, hide the flower in the breast of his dandy serge

shirt. he be a bit of a dandy in his way, and especially so when he

expect to see Margaret. he follow her instruction to the letter;

she have tell he at what point to throw the flower, and plump at her

foot they fall, at the precise moment she desire. the audience stare

at first at the unusual compliment, and then applaud loudly.

Margaret curtsey, at which they applaud still more vociferously;

the beautiful girl be a pet of theirs, and they approve of the

tribute. the Leading Lady turn pale, and clutch at her bosom

tragically. the dagger have be deftly plant, and she feel the

smart--as only a woman would feel it. Margaret place the flower in

the bosom of her dress, and send a look straight into the eye of

Philip, which make every nerve in his body tingle.









CHAPTER VI.



ROMEO and JULIET.





the Leading Lady be fond of money, and the theatre be do so well

that her dividend every week be a very handsome one, three time as

much as she could expect to get elsewhere; but what woman be prudent

when her vanity be hurt? a man with a large bump of caution

occasionally hang back, and calculate consequence. a woman never

do. the Leading Lady in a tower passion confront Mr. Hart, the

manager, at the end of the performance.



"here come a tragedy," think he, as he look into her wrathful

eye. there be a smile on his face, nevertheless.



"I leave the company!" she say abruptly, with heaving bosom.



"my dear lady!" remonstrate the manager.



"to-morrow. I shall take a place in the coach that start at eight

o'clock."



she know well enough what the result would be if she leave; the company

would collapse. a man might be spare, and his place fill, or his

part double, but the loss of a woman would inflict irreparable

injury upon the prospect of the theatre. Mr. Hart know this also.



"you don't forget," he say gravely, "that we have your signature, and

that if you leave without consent we can make you pay heavy damage!"



"that for my signature! that for your heavy damage!" each time she

snap a disdainful finger.



"my dear lady," he say, in a soothe tone, "you be excited, you be

overstrained. we have tax you a little hardly. we'll play light

piece for a night or two, and give you a rest."



inconsiderate man he could not more successfully have fan the flame

in her breast.



"you'll play no light piece to give I a rest! play light piece, and

give _she_ the opportunity of take lead character! the shameless

hussy! not if I know it!"



Mr. Hart begin to understand. this colloquy be take place on the

stage; the theatre be clear, the curtain be up. down the stair

which lead to the lady' dressing-room trip Margaret, fresh, and

bright, and happy, with her bunch of flower in her hand.



"good night, Mr. Hart," she cry gaily.



in the shadow of the door which lead on to the stage a man be wait

for she--Philip. they meet, clasp hand--her supple finger lay in

Philip's great palm as in a nest, and he imprison they, be

sure!--and walk out, side by side, chat confidentially, with

their head close together. the Leading Lady see this, and her anger

rise higher; but still it be bitter gall to she to reflect that if

she go away, the field would be clear for her rival.



Mr. Hart feel that he be on the horn of a difficulty; he could spare

neither one nor the other of the lady.



"you're the manager of this company," say the Leading Lady, "and you

ought long ago to have put down such shameless going on. do you see

the way they go out together, and do you think people be blind? we

shall be the talk of the town; but I'll not be implicate in it. my

name musn't be use lightly." the manager smile grimly. "I leave

to-morrow. understand that."



"I decline to understand it. you will fulfil your engagement, and if

it be necessary for I to take step to prevent your departure, I must

do so for the sake of the other. I will swear a declaration against

you!"



he be aware that he be talk the most arrant nonsense, but he

rely on the feminine mind to assist he with its fear, and with its

ignorance of legal subtlety.



"I shall be sorry to do so against a lady whom I esteem and respect so

much, and of whose talent I have so high an opinion, but no other

course will be open to I. if I allow you to go, the digger would

rise against I. and quite right they would be! why, my dear lady," he

say, cunningly, "you know as well as I do that we be nothing without

you--that you be the soul of the company--that there be not your

equal on the colonial stage!"



the Leading Lady begin to soften beneath the influence of such gross

flattery, but it would not do to give way at once.



"I will _not_ stop to be insult!"



"no one _shall_ insult you."



"but some one have--you know who--and she shall not do so again--no,

not if you swear a million declaration!"



"come, now, tell I all about it," say the manager, take her arm,

and walk slowly with she up and down the stage. "by the way, the

Honourable Mr. Simpson, the Warden of Moonlight Flat, say last

night, when you be play Ophelia--you know he; he be in the

theatre with the Commissioner of the Goldfields and the resident

Magistrate----"



"yes, yes," say the Leading Lady impatiently, "what do he say?"



"that your Ophelia be equal to anything he have see on the London

stage, and that he believe you would create a sensation there. he be

first cousin to the Earl of Badmington, you know, who have a theatre in

London. I think you would like to hear it. he be very anxious to

make your acquaintance--as all gentleman of taste and refinement would

be."



he glance slyly at the Leading Lady, whose head be nod gently up

and down, in sweet contentment.



"and now, my dear lady, tell I your grievance."



"it's yours as well as mine, but if you like to stand it, I shalln't. if

bouquet of flower be to be throw on the stage, they must be throw

to I--do you understand, sir? to _I_, as the Leading Lady, and as

the star of the company!"



it happen that Mr. Hart have be busy elsewhere during the episode

that have very nearly bring the ship to wreck, and have hear nothing

of it. he ask the Leading Lady for an explanation, which be give

to he.



"and if you don't stop these shameful going-on," be her concluding

word, "I give you fair warning, I will not stay with you. _I_ have a

character to lose, thank God!"



which be to be construe in so many queer way, that Mr. Hart could

scarcely refrain from laugh. "Confound Master Philip!" he think,

and say aloud, "well, well, my dear creature, I will see to it. and

no flower shall be throw--by Mr. Philip Rowe, at all event--on the

stage to any one but you."



this difficulty be soothe over, he go in search of Philip Rowe,

and find he lean against a fence outside the hotel, gaze up at

a light in a bedroom window on the first floor.



"rehearse 'Romeo and Juliet?'" ask Mr. Hart kindly, take the

young man's arm.



Philip blush, and stammer some unintelligible word.



"that _be_ her window, Philip," say Mr. Hart, "so you will not make

the same ridiculous mistake that I do for a fortnight together,

gaze up every night at the light in my lady's bedroom, and work

myself into a state of gush sentimentalism over the slender waist

and the graceful turn of the head I see shadow on the blind, until I

discover that I have be watch the bedroom window of a black

footman."



this be a piece of pure invention on the part of Mr. Hart.



Philip, have nothing to say in reply, shift one foot over another

restlessly. if he could have retire with a good grace, he would have

do so, but Mr. Hart have hold of his arm. Mr. Hart continue:



"put sentiment aside, a nice scrape you be almost get I

into to-night. ah! you may stare, but I should like to know what you

mean by throw flower to my singing Chambermaid--who be not by any

mean clever, let I tell you, and will never make her fortune on the

stage--when we have in our company a lady who play lead

character, and who know every line of Juliet's part?"



"Ho, ho!" laugh Philip; "Juliet be a girl of sixteen or seventeen,

and your Leading Lady be forty."



"woe for your life if you say so in her presence!" exclaim Mr.

Hart, with a quiet chuckle; "it would not be worth a moment's

purchase. forty, sir! and what if she be forty?--which she be not by

five year--she be the only woman that can play Juliet to your Romeo."



"hush!" whisper Philip. "she be open the window."



Margaret, alone, in her white dress, be indeed open the window.

she do not know--not she!--that her lover be below, nor that her

form could be see, for she have extinguish the light in the room.

her shadow might be discern, but what be there in a shadow? she sit

down by the window, and rest her head on her arm. the graceful

outline of her arm and neck and bended head be clearly visible, and

the lover feast his eye upon they. she hold in her hand the

flower which Philip have throw she! her lip be upon the tender

leave--sweet to the sweet. he see she kiss the flower, and his soul

thrill with rapture. the night be beautifully still; not a sound

be stir; and as far as eye could see the white tent of the

digger be gleam. so Margaret sit and muse, and Philip look on

and dream. here, in the new world, but yesterday a savage waste, the

old, old story be be enact with as much freshness as though the

world be but just create. what wonder? because the sun have rise a

few million of time, be the dew on the leave less sweet and pure in

the early morning's light than on that wondrous day when Adam awake

and find Eve by his side?



so Margaret sit and muse, and Philip look on and dreamed; and I

think that Margaret peep through the lattice-work of her finger,

and see with her cunning eye that her lover be there, worship

she.



how long they would have thus remain, Heaven only know. Mr. Hart

give they at least twenty minute, and then touch Philip's arm.

Philip start, and Margaret at the window start also, and with a

swift happy glance outward, and with wave of the pretty hand and arm,

close the window. Philip be stand in the light, and Mr. Hart,

like a kind and careful friend, have creep backward in the shade; so

that Margaret, when she cast that straight swift glance in her lover's

direction, see only he. surely as the hand--love's white flag of

recognition--wave towards he, it have touch her lip first, and she

have send a kiss into the air--which he receive in his heart. it

stir tender chord there, and through his vein creep love's fever,

which turn dross into gold, and make a heaven of earth!









CHAPTER VII.



AH, PHILIP, MY son! I, also, have a girl whom I love.





then say Philip, as he and Mr. Hart move slowly away--then say

Philip softly, as though but a moment have pass since his companion

last speak:



"her name be Margaret, not Juliet. I have no need to play Romeo to

Margaret. Margaret!" he whisper to himself, find a subtle charm

in the name; "my Margaret!" and then aloud, "have your Leading Lady

ever play such a character?"



"yes," reply Mr. Hart, without any direct meaning, "in 'Faust.'"



Philip's face flush scarlet, not at the word, but at the tone,

which be sad and significant, without the speaker intend it to be

so.



"I know you to be a gentleman----" pursue Mr. Hart.



"I think you to be one," interrupt Philip hotly.



"I hope you will see no reason to change your opinion," say Mr. Hart.



"I see a reason already."



"let I hear it," ask Mr. Hart, secretly please at the young man's

ill-humour.



"you associate my Margaret's name--"
