"your Margaret!" exclaim Mr. Hart. "my Margaret, if you please!"



"mine!" cry Philip, in a loud voice.



"mine!" echo Mr. Hart, in a calm tone.



"call she down and ask she!" demand Philip in his rashness, without

consider; and, for the life of he, Mr. Hart could not help

laugh long and heartily.



"o that you be twenty year young!" say Philip.



"o that I be!" exclaim Mr. Hart, with grave humour. "then you

would really have cause for uneasiness when you hear I call she

mine."



"how do you make she your?"



"I stand to she in the light of a father," reply Mr. Hart more

seriously. "when I persuade her mother in town to let her accompany

we, I promise that I would look after she and protect she. therefore

she be mine, because I be her father."



"and without any 'therefore,'" respond Philip, "she be mine, because

I be her lover."



"ah," say Mr. Hart, with a bright smile, "here be a case to be

settle, then. but if every pretty girl be her lover's, then one

might belong to fifty, or more, for there be heart enough. why, you

rash-head! do you know how many man in Silver Creek might call your

Margaret theirs by the same right as that by which you claim she?"



"no," say Philip, a little sulkily, "I don't know."



"then I'll tell you. to my certain knowledge, sixty-nine; to my almost

as certain conviction, some five hundred. she have forty-two offer of

marriage the first week, and have have twenty-seven since. come now,

divide she between the sixty-nine lover who have declare themselves;

what part of she be your?"



"you talk nonsense," say Philip roughly.



"well, suppose you talk sense," say Mr. Hart blandly.



"it be hardly believable," cry Philip, clench his fist.

"sixty-nine offer of marriage! she never tell I, and I'm her lover."



"she have tell I, and I'm only her father."



"by proxy," correct Philip.



"well, by proxy."



"why should she tell you and not I?" ask Philip, more sulkily

still.



"because, my dear Philip," say Mr. Hart, lay his hand kindly on

the young man's arm, "up to the present, as I have say, she be mine,

and not your; and because she have a frank open nature, and must

confide in some one. as I come first, she confide in I. she have

give I all the letter to read, and a rare collection they be. if

they be print they would be a curiosity."



"I should like to see they, and the name at the bottom of they."



"so that you might fight all the writer for fall in love as you

have do! well, you would have enough to do, for you would have to

fight accord to the fashion of different country. I have make an

analysis, my dear Philip. seven frenchman, four german, one Spaniard,

three Americans, fifty-three Englishmen, Irishmen, and Scotchmen, and

one Chinaman, have offer marriage to--I will say--_our_ Margaret."



"a Chinaman! good heaven! such a creature to raise his eye to my

Margaret! tell I, at least, _his_ name, that I may cut his pigtail

from his dirty crown!"



"there's an ah in it and a Sen in it and a ping in it; and if you can

find he out by those sign you be very welcome. but why should a

Chinaman not love? hath he not eye, hand, organ, dimension,

sense, affection, passion? his letter be the great curiosity of

the lot, and he have evidently educate himself in the English

language. I know his proposal by heart. here it be: 'you welly good

english girl; I welly good Chinaman. you mally I, welly good

match. roast pig and m'landy (brandy) for dinner every day. M'lenty

gold--make m'lenty more. I take you to my country, by bye. Chinaman

welly good man.' then come the ah and the Sen and the Ping. but let

we be serious, although this be true enough that I have tell

you--truth with a comical side to it. you be angry with I a little

while ago."



"yes, for associate my Margaret's name with mine in the character of

Faust."



"I have no distinct intention in my mind, Philip; the conversation

happen to take that turn. it would pain I very much to have to

think of you in that way. but Margaret be a simple good girl, and it

be my duty to look after she. I never know till to-night that you be

pay marked attention to she."



"who tell you?"



"our Leading Lady."



Philip Rowe smile: he have his vanity.



"o, indeed!" he say, with assume carelessness.



"and that will bring I back presently to a subject I mention when I

surprise you to-night. first, however, there be another thing to be

settle. you must cease your attention to Margaret."



"not if I know it!" say Philip, with a defiant shake of his head. "I

mean to marry she. if you throw any obstacle in the way I'll run away

with she to-morrow, in spite of your tooth."



he laugh confidently: he know his power.



"but you be a gentleman," remonstrate Mr. Hart. "and she be a lady,"

quoth Philip.



if love's guild could give title, a peasant would rank high than a

duchess. not that there be anything common about Margaret. she be

bear of humble parent, it be true; but she be a good girl, and that

be enough for any man.



it be enough for Mr. Hart. he gaze at Philip in frank and honest

admiration; but he determine to apply a test. he be not a suspicious

man, but he have a duty to perform.



"suppose there be an obstacle already in the way," he say, look

Philip steadily in the face; "suppose she be already marry."



Philip stagger, and the blood desert his face. "Good God!" he

cry. "then she have be play I false!"



Mr. Hart wish he have not apply the test; he be satisfied of

Philip's sincerity.



"not so fast!" he cry, in a cheery tone, "not so fast! I only say

'suppose;' I don't say it be so. how you young hot spirit jump at

conclusion."



but it be a few minute before Philip recover himself.



"you frighten I," he say, with a feeble smile. "then it be not

true! if I have consider a moment, I should have know; for if truth

and innocence have a home in this world, they have it in Margaret's

breast. but you come upon I suddenly."



Mr. Hart think, "Ah! youth, youth, what a painter you be!" and

say aloud, "here be my hand; know that you mean honourably by

Margaret, I give my consent to your see she as usual."



"I'll marry she to-morrow," say Philip, take the hand offer he.



"softly, softly; there be condition."



"I'll have no condition!" shout Philip impetuously.



"you'll have this and you'll have that!" say Mr. Hart, in a tone of

gentle sarcasm. "you willn't have this, and you willn't have that! very

well, then. I wish you good-night." and he turn away.



"what!" cry Philip, turn after he, "desert I when I want you to

be my friend!"



the old man's heart warm to the young fellow; he admire everything

in he--his hot blood, his impetuosity, his obstinacy, his generous

imperiousness.



"I be your friend," say Mr. Hart, "and I will continue to be so if

you will let I. but when a man say of something that be mine, as

Margaret be--ah, shake your head! it don't affect _me!_--when a man

say of something that be mine, and that he want to be his, that

he'll have no condition, he compel I to act in self-defence. attend

to I, young sir! be reasonable, or to-morrow I take Margaret back to

her mother, a hundred and forty mile away, and you shall not speak

another word to she, as sure as my name's Hart."



"Ho! ho! you speak boldly; but it don't matter--you're a man in a

thousand. in a thousand! in ten thousand. I'm glad you're not young,

or you might prove dangerous." Mr. Hart take off his cap, and bow

lowly at this compliment. "you'll not let I speak to she, will you

not? I'll borrow a speaking-trumpet, and shout to she that you be

part we for ever. but there! give I your hand again. I'm not

frightened of you. I be in such spirit that I must do something

desperate. as you value your life, give I a back!"



with the readiness of a boy, Mr. Hart stoop and rest his hand on

his knee. Philip take a run backward, then dart forward like a

deer, and, lightly touch the stoop man's back, fly over he

like a bird. then stoop himself, and fold his arm; and old as Mr.

Hart be, he take the leap.



after that they have a hearty laugh together.



"by Jove!" exclaim Philip, "you be as young as I be, and yet I

should say you be over sixty."



"I be," say Mr. Hart proudly, straighten his back.



"I don't mind give way a little to such a man. name your

condition."



"you want to marry Margaret?"



"I do--to-morrow!"



"nonsense. you want to marry she."



"I do--I will; stop I who can!"



"she have a mother."



"God bless she, and all belong to she!"



"Bravo--a good mother, mind."



"all that belong to Margaret must be good."



"her mother must be consult."



Philip scratch his head. "must?" he ask dubiously.



"must."



"how be that to be do?"



"by letter."



Philip count rapidly on his finger.



"why, we shall have to wait a week!"



"for the consent. and then perhaps she'll not give it."



"it will be all the same. we'll marry without it."



"but you'll have to wait long than a week, Philip. you'll have to

wait until our three month' engagement at the theatre be at an end."



"impossible."



"it must and shall be. why, without Margaret we be nothing."



"I know it," chuckle Philip.



"she be the soul of the company." the wily old fellow be use the

very word he have use to the Leading Lady, and he think nothing of

contradict what he have say a few minute before, when he declare

that Margaret be not clever, and would never make her fortune on the

stage. "do you hear I? she be the soul of the company."



"I know it," chuckle Philip again.



"well, then, do you think I be go to let you ruin our prospect,

and rob we, as you propose do?"



"gently, gently there! not so fast with your robbing!"



"it be the truth that I be speak, and you know it; you have say so

yourself. Margaret be the soul of the company--she be our great

draw. if she go without my be able to get another girl as pretty

in her place----"



"you cann't do that; I defy you."



"hold your tongue, hot-head!--without our get another girl

_nearly_ as pretty in her place----"



"that's well," interrupt the incorrigible Philip; "but you'll have

a rare hunt even for such a one. they don't grow on gooseberry

bush."



"our business be as good as ruin without she, or some one in her

place; and do you suppose I'll stand quietly by and see that do?

besides, think of the money Margaret herself be saving----"



"_that_ for the money!" say Philip, with a snap of his finger.

"money-making's a man's business, not a woman's."



"that's true, and I like you the well for say so. but leave

Margaret out of the question, there be person in our company the

happiness of whose life hang upon their be able to save a certain

amount of money within a certain time. not only their happiness but

the happiness of helpless one who be dearer to they than their

heart's blood, depend upon this."



"by Jove! you speak strongly. Mention one of they."



"one of they stand before you now."



Philip turn and look Mr. Hart straight in the face. tear be

gather in the old man's eye, and the young man turn away again,

so that he should not see they.



"forgive I, mate," he say softly; "I be so wrapt up in my own

happiness that I be forgetful of the feeling of other."



"ah, Philip, my son"--there be so tender an accent in the old man's

tone, that the tear rise to Philip's eye as well--"I also have a

girl whom I love. see here, my dear boy. this be my daughter. she be

at home in England, and I be here sixteen thousand mile away."



he have take the picture of his darling from his pocket, and now he

hand it to Philip. the young man look at it in the clear

moonlight. a round fresh face, open mouth with rosy lip, bright

ingenuous eye, fair curl around her white forehead. she be stand

within an ivy porch, and one little hand be raise as though she be

listen.



"it be take seven year ago," say Mr. Hart; "she be twelve year

old then."



"she be beautiful, beautiful!" exclaim Philip enthusiastically. "and

you haven't see she since then?"



"no--and my old heart ache for a sight of she. this money that I be

earn will take I to she."



"by Jove! and I be go to step in your way! Brute that I be!

Margaret shall stop. I'll wait till the end of the time. I can see she

every night; and I can build a wooden house for she in the meantime.

God bless you, old boy! give I your hand again. next to my own

father, you be the man I love and respect the most."









CHAPTER VIII.



GOD bles EVERYBODY.





"but I haven't finish yet," say Mr. Hart, after a short pause. "I

have another condition."



"Another!" exclaim Philip, with an inclination to turn ill-humour.

"you be insatiable! and how many more after that, pray?"



"none."



"that's a mercy. out with your last condition--which I'll not comply

with."



"which you will comply with, or I'll know the reason why."



"ah, ah! my Cornishman, go on with your condition."



"where do you get those flower from?"



"where do I get they from? I give Nature an order for they, and they

grow for I--and bloom for Margaret. I ride a dozen mile for they,

and I'd ride a thousand if she bid I."



"or fly to the moon, or swim, or dive in the fire, or ride on the

cloud, no doubt!"



"yes, if she want I to. she have but to speak."



"quite right," say Mr. Hart, turn his face from Philip, so that

the smile on his lip should not be see "but that's not my concern.

this be. mind what I say, sir. I'll have no more flower throw to my

singing Chambermaid."



"o," retort Philip, "now it's you'll not have this, and you'll not

have that! very well, then. I wish you good-night."



and off he go, take huge stride purposely, and stretch his

leg to their utmost.



"no, no, Philip!" cry Mr. Hart, run after Philip, and laugh

heartily at the wit of the retort. "no, no; I'm serious."



"and so be I," say Philip, stop so that Mr. Hart might come up to

he. "no more flower, eh! why, I'll smother she with they every

night. I'll compel you to engage some one to carry they off the stage.

no more flower! I'll show you! why, I'm go to scour the country

for flower, and I shall set seed all round my tent."



"if you wait for the flower to grow, I shall be satisfied. you cann't

make they come up by blow on they with your hot word and

hot breath. but seriously, Philip, there _must_ be no more

flower-throwing."



briefly he explain the reason why, and then upshot of it all be

that Philip promise. then Mr. Hart say that Philip have well return

with he to the Rose, Shamrock, and Thistle Hotel; it be too late for

he to walk back to his reef.



"I can give you a shake-down in my bedroom," say Mr. Hart.



"all right!" say Philip, and think with ecstasy, "I shall be near

Margaret; I shall sleep under the same roof as Margaret."



"have you anything to drink?" ask Philip when they be in Mr.

Hart's room.



Mr. Hart want Philip to sleep in his bed, which be but a stretcher,

barely wide enough for one fair-sized man, but Philip would not hear

of it; so they obtain a straw mattress, and lay it on the floor,

and Philip toss off his clothe, and stretch himself upon his hard

bed (and sleep upon it afterwards as soundly as if it have be make of

eider-duck's feather), in a state of complete satisfaction with

himself and every one in the world. it be while he be lie like

this, and while Mr. Hart, more methodical than his companion, be

slowly undress himself, that Philip have ask if he have anything to

drink.



"I'll get something," say Mr. Hart, and leave the room, and return

with a bottle and glass.



while he be go, Philip look about he, and soon discover that

his Margaret's bedroom be immediately above he. he gaze at the

ceiling with rapture, and send kiss thitherward. a single partition

part he from his sweetheart. he fancy that he could hear her soft

breathing. the same roof cover they. it be as yet his near

approach to heaven.



"here's to Margaret," say Philip, hold up his glass.



"to Margaret," respond Mr. Hart, "and happiness to you both."



"another toast," say Philip; "to my old dad and the dear old Silver

Flagon."



they drink the toast.



"what be the Silver Flagon?" ask Mr. Hart.



"one of these fine day perhaps I'll tell you," reply Philip.



but Philip never tell he. one of these fine day Mr. Hart discover

for himself.



the light be out, and Mr. Hart kneel by a corner of his stretcher,

and pray for a few minute. he be pray for his daughter, and

thinking of she; he behold her pretty face very plainly in the dark

room. Philip see the shadow of the kneeling man; it make he very

tender towards Mr. Hart.



"heathen that I be!" he whisper to himself. "I haven't kneel at my

bedside for many a long month."



then he pray in silence, without get out of bed.



"be you comfortable, Philip?" ask Mr. Hart presently.



"I be very happy," reply Philip. "good night--God bless you."



"and you, my boy. good night."



Philip think, "I be glad my Margaret have have such a protector. God

bless everybody."



the next moment he be asleep.



he be up an hour after the sun, and off to his reef. thing be

look well there. Mr. Hart have speak to the proprietor of the Rose,

Shamrock, and Thistle, whose name, by the way, as something have to be

say concern he, it may be as well to mention. you will have hear

it before--it be Smith. Mr. Hart have speak to Mr. Smith about

Philip's reef, and show he some piece of golden quartz, say

what a pity it be that there be no crush-machine near such rich

stone; and what a fortune a man might make who have money and

enterprise enough to erect one. Mr. Smith have both. four year

ago---- but no, common as his name be he deserve a chapter to

himself, and shall have it.









CHAPTER IX.



a man of METTLE.





not long than four year ago, Mr. Smith be a bricklayer in the old

country, earn an average wage of thirty shilling a week, out of

which he support himself and his old mother; and one day, for want

of something well to do--he be out of work at the time--he

emigrate almost by accident. this be a literal fact. he arise early

in the morning, with no intention of leave the country, but somewhat

sad at heart because he have no work to do. (when he relate the story

in after day he say that his hand feel like lump of lead as they

hang by his side.) on this morning, then, he stroll to the London

Docks, and see a ship make ready to start for Australia; be tell

that it would sail for Gravesend in the afternoon; idly inquire the

price of a steerage passage, and find that he have just money enough

in his pocket, and a trifle over, the scraping and saving of ten

year' bricklaying; and have a chat with an enthusiast, who paint

Australia in the colour of the rainbow, and then paint England in

ditch colour.



"what be the use of wear one's life away in such a country as

this?" demand the enthusiast. "what have a man get to look forward to

when he's old, and not fit to work?"



Mr. Smith consider. what _be_ the use of grind one's life away

in such a country as England? what be there to look forward to, to

hope for, to work for? a poor man's grave. perhaps a pauper's funeral.

bear a bricklayer, die a bricklayer; that might be his epitaph, if he

leave money enough to pay for one.



"Australia's the place for such man as we," continue the enthusiast.

"Australia's the land of gold, and milk, and honey. England's no

country for man of spirit; it's use up, sir--use up. and there's the

new land wait to make poor man rich--hold out its arm for

they."



"I should like to go with you," say Smith.



"come, then," say the enthusiast.



"I'm afraid there's not time," say Smith; "there's my old mother. I

couldn't leave without say good-bye to she."



"what's your name?" ask the enthusiast.



"Smith," reply Smith.



the enthusiast give a start, and utter an exclamation.



"what's the matter?" ask Smith.



"nothing," say the enthusiast; "only I be think that I _should_

like you to come."



"but how be it to be manage?" inquire Smith, glance at the name of

the vessel, with his mouth watering. it be a nine-hundred-ton ship,

call the Gold Packet. "but how be it to be manage? a man that I

know emigrate a year ago, and he have to buy bed, and tin cup,

and soap and towel, and I don't know what else; those thing ben't

get by whistle for they."



"I'll manage it for you," say the enthusiast. "you go home and say

good-bye to your mother. be back here at one o'clock. by that time

I'll have your passage-ticket, and your berth, and bedding, and tin

cup, and soap and towel, and everything else ready for you. what do

you say?"



"what do I say? there's my hand upon it, and thank you. I'll do it."



and with quicken pulse he hasten home, kiss the amazed old

woman--who be so dumbfoundere that she could do nothing but look at

her son, and cry--promise to send she plenty of money from Australia

and to make a lady of she in five year, and be back to the Gold

Packet at one o'clock.



"you're a man of mettle," say the enthusiast; "you're just the sort

for the gold-digging; it's such man as you they want. you'll make

your fortune there as sure as egg be egg. here's your ticket. come

down-stair; I'll show you your berth and thing."



"how much do it all come to?" ask Smith. the enthusiast pencil

some figure on a piece of paper, and give it to Smith, who look at

the item, and add they up. everything be correct; he hand the

enthusiast the money, and have exactly two shilling and fourpence leave

to conquer the new world with. Smith go down-stair (to speak

courteously of the descent; but there be bad, we be teach) into

the den where the steerage passenger be pack, and the enthusiast

show he his berth, his bedding, his tin cup, his bar of yellow

soap, and other necessary paraphernalia. the enthusiast show these

thing to Smith, but Smith could scarcely see they, the place be so

dark. Smith be not daunt because the place be dismal, and because

it be fill with woman cry, and child scream, and man

growling--a very pit of discomfort. his soul rise to the occasion; he

have a spirit above a bricklayer's; with his passage ticket in his

hand, and two shilling and fourpence in his pocket, he feel himself a

king. there be work before he to do, and he be happy in the

prospect of no more idle day. when he go on to the deck he do not

see the enthusiast, but he do not miss he, he be so interested in

what be go on about he, the hurrying to and fro, the shouting,

the singing of the sailor, the loosening of the sail, the hauling of

rope. in an hour the ship be off, wind its way through such a

complicated labyrinth of boat and ship and rope, that the wonder

be how it disentangle itself safely. Smith watch the man[oe]uvres

with admiration. then he glance at the passage ticket. "Holloa!" he

say, "they've make a mistake in my christian name. I'm William Smith,

not John."



(let I mention here, briefly, that our Smith never set eye again on

the enthusiast, whose name be also Smith, prefix by John. it be

his passage ticket, indeed, that our Smith hold in his hand. all the

time he have be paint in the most glowing colour the glowing

attraction of the goldfield on the other side of the world, he have

be fill with the most gloomy foreboding. his courage have fail

he at the last moment, and seize the opportunity which have so

fortunately present itself of give the new world another Smith

instead of himself, he have sell his passage ticket and bed and

cooking utensil to the bricklayer, and after receive the money for

they, bade good-bye to the Gold Packet and all the fair promise it

hold out.)



with his two shilling and fourpence in his pocket, William Smith

start on the voyage, and make himself so useful, and be altogether

so cheerful and shrewd and bustling, that he soon become a prime

favourite with the passenger and crew. in ninety-two day from the

date of sailing, the ship pass through Port Philip Heads, and from

that day Fortune smile upon William Smith. in a fortnight he be on

the goldfield; in six month he be a speculator; in twelve, he have

save a thousand pound. and now he be proprietor of a fine hotel and

a theatre, and have a dozen other iron in the fire, not one of which

do he allow to grow cold.



I think I shall be pardon for this digression. this story be of the

Mosaic kind, and although there be some strange bit in it, I hope

none will be find incongruous, but that they will all fit in one with

another, and form a complete and original whole.









CHAPTER X.



to-morrow IT be ST. VALENTINE'S DAY.





Mr. Hart, then, have speak to William Smith about Philip's golden

reef, and what a capital chance there be for a crush machine. his

word do not fall upon listless ear. the same day William Smith

walk to the reef, examine the stone, go down the shaft, chip

here and there, put two, or three bit of gold and stone in his

pocket, as treasure-trove, come up from the hole, stroll about the

locality, Argus-eye, and make up his mind. he speak it to Philip and

his mate. say he: "in three week I will have a machine erect here,

with twelve head of stamper, which shall be work day and night,

and which shall crush forty ton of quartz every twenty-four hour.

you have raise, I should say, about one hundred and fifty ton of

stone. you shall put a dozen man at work in your claim--I will provide

the money for their wage, and for powder and fuse--and in three week

you shall raise another hundred ton. I will do all this on the

follow term: you shall contract to give I the first two hundred

ton of quartz to crush, and I will contract to crush it at the rate

of three ounce of gold per ton." (the shrewd speculator have see

clearly enough that there be plenty of gold in the stone to pay he,

and leave a handsome margin; indeed, he calculate that the quartz

already raise from the bowel of the earth, and lie on the surface

of the claim, would yield not less than ten or twelve ounce to the

ton.) "the next two hundred ton I will crush for two and a half

ounce of gold per ton; the next two hundred for two ounce per ton."



some man be bear with a genius for figure: William Smith be one;

and he have already tot up in his own mind that the crushing of

these six hundred ton of quartz would bring he in no less than

£6000; and that it could all be do in fifteen day. his £6000 would

pay all expense of labour and the purchase and erection of the

machine, which in little more than a fortnight after it be put up

would stand he in nothing. there be many chance of this kind in

the goldfield for enterprise man.



"after that," conclude William Smith, "we can make fresh

arrangement."



Philip and his mate jump at the offer. then, practical William

Smith, to their astonishment and admiration, tell they that although

he have be but a short time on the range--it could not have be more

than three hour altogether--he have settle on the very spot where the

machine be to be erect. he show they the place. it be on the

slope of a natural basin, which, with a little labour, could be make

into a splendid reservoir for the rain. here the machine be to be

erect; here the dam be to be build; here the shed for the furnace

and for the washing-out and retort of the gold be to be put up.

all be arrange. the only thing that would be want be water. "pray

for rain," say William Smith; and fancy that he see in Philip's

face an intention to fall on his knee that instant, cry out, in a

fright, "not now, not now! in a fortnight, when the dam be ready." so

Philip defer his prayer for two week.



now, it be manifestly impossible to get a crush-machine from the

capital of the colony in time. but William Smith, when he make his

offer, know what he be about. he know of a machine on a neighbouring

goldfield not many mile away, which have be erect in a foolish

spot, where it be practically useless, for the quartz would not yield

sufficient gold to pay expense of labour. those who have buy and

erect the machine have do so on the credit of a small patch of gold

which they have find, and which they think would lead they to

precious deposit. they find no more gold, or not sufficient to pay.

they build castle in the air--which practical William Smith never

do; he always go upon solid ground, and seldom make a mistake.

before he be two day old he have buy the machine for a quarter

of its value, and fifty man be set to work on it, so that it be

almost literally tear down. but he have an experienced man at the head

of his worker, and everything be do right. fifty more man be

work at the reservoir, dig out the earth, and pile up the

bank, and on the very day succeed the scene which have take place

between Philip and Mr. Hart the first portion of the crush-machine

arrive on the ground. this keep Philip busy, and although he be

burn to get away to his Margaret, he could not do so until the

night. the first thing that he see when he go behind the scene be

one of the flower he have buy the night before. he raise his eye

from the flower to Margaret's face, for the flower be in her bosom.



"ah!" he sigh, flush with delight.



of such simple thing be life's sweetest pleasure bear.



the bunch of flower's have, as a matter of course, form a fruitful

subject of conversation among the member of the dramatic company, and

Margaret, be a woman, and womanly, be oblige to make a confidante

of some one of her own sex. the Leading Lady be out of the question;

so the First Old Woman, the mother of the baby who have prove such a

hit, on the first night, receive Margaret's confidence, and be a

good-hearted, unselfish creature, and delight at the opportunity of

indulge in a little bit of match-making, and also of revenge

herself upon the Leading Lady for her objection to baby be a

shareholder in the Star Dramatic Company, she listen, and smile,

and congratulate the young girl.



"to-morrow it be Saint Valentine's Day!" she sing.



"you've come to silver Creek for something. here, my dear, nurse my

baby, and get your hand in."



which cause Margaret to blush furiously.



"o," cry Margaret, "but there's be nothing say between we!"



"nothing, my dear!" exclaim the First Old Woman, with a mischievous

laugh. "really nothing!"



"well, nothing _very_ particular."



"indeed!" say the First Old Woman, with good-humour sarcasm. "be

come behind the scene every night say nothing? be throw you

the flower say nothing? be stand outside your window last

night for a full hour and a half--I see he with my own eye, my dear!

I do; and envy you--be that say nothing? I declare, then, I

shall set _my_ cap at he; I may as well take a chance in the lottery.

he's as handsome a young fellow as ever walk in two shoe, and if

you intend to disappoint him----"



"o, but I don't," interrupt Margaret, apprehensively.



Whereupon they fall to kiss one another, and baby come in for she

share.









CHAPTER XI.



"I be go to speak OUT," say PHILIP.





when Philip make his appearance that evening behind the scene, the

First Old Woman smile significantly at he, and once, when her cue to

go on the stage be give, she cry to he, of malice aforethought:



"o, dear I! I'm want on the stage! hold my baby, Mr. Rowe, till I

come off again."



and before he have time to utter a word one way or another, baby be in

his arm, and the mother dart away, laugh to herself.



Philip be not ashamed of his burden; he nurse the baby tenderly, but

somewhat gingerly, it must be confess--fearful, perhaps, lest he

should break the little thing, or dislocate something. Margaret, who

be on the stage at the time, look at he furtively as he be

kiss the mite, and her mind be in such a whirl, that for the first

time during her engagement she forget the word she have to speak.

observe which the First Old Woman make matter bad by whispering

sly nonsense in Margaret's ear. little do the unconscious baby

suspect the important part she be play in the sentimental comedy.



later on in the night, Philip say to Margaret:



"I be go to speak out."



this be the very thing she be pine for, and now that her wish be

about to be gratify, she exclaim:



"if you dare, sir!" saucily, mischievously, coquettishly.



then what do Margaret do but lead he into a more retire spot,

where, if he do speak out, no one but herself could hear he.



"if you dare, sir!" she repeat, with a smile which magnetise he.

there be but little need for that; he be bewitch already.



"call I Philip," he entreat.



"Philip," she sigh.



it be like the whisper of a rose.



he be radiant; the joy in his heart be reflect in his face. he

toy with her finger. Slender they be, and supple, and not strong.

but never be chain more potent.



"well, Philip?" say Margaret shyly.



"well, Margaret?"



he could find at that moment nothing more sensible to say. he be

engage watch the light of her eye, and the colour come and go on

her cheek.



"what be that in your hand?" say she.



"a letter."



"ah, that's what you bring I here for! a letter! for I! give it to

I!" she hold out her little hand eagerly.



he withhold the letter from she.



"it be not for you."



"o, indeed!"



she tear her finger from his grasp, for he have take they and be

kiss they.



"but you may read it," he say ruefully.



she nestle to he, and give he her hand again, and look

remorseful. when she plead mutely for forgiveness, with her pretty

face upturned to his, and with her soft red lip within an inch of

his, what would you have do, have you be in his place? he do what

you would have do--and do it again--and again--and----



"no, sir," she cry, put her hand upon her lip. "no, Philip, I

mean. you shall not--you must not! some one will be come this

way----"



there be nothing for it, as her lip be cover, but to kiss she

neck; and he do so, until she lie in his arm panting.



"you frighten I," she sigh; "and if you be not still, I'll run

away."



and she mean it. dramatic lover she have have by the score, in silk

and fustian. she have be make love to a hundred time upon the stage,

but those be sham engagement, and her gentle breast be not

flutter by they, nor be her sweet nature spoilt by they. this sort

of thing be quite different.



"and I've a great mind to be angry with you," she say, not move

from his embrace.



"why?"



"you have bring I no flower."



he look disconsolate. "if I have know you want they!"



"if you have know, sir! you must guess thing. you must look into my

face, if you think it will not frighten you, and you must say,

Margaret want this; Margaret want that----' no, no, Philip I do not

say I want _that!_"



"but you tell I I must look into your face, and guess thing, and I

do!"



"then I'll take back all that I have say, for man be such foolish

creature." she give he the tender smile, to strengthen the word.

"and indeed, and indeed, I've a good mind to be angry with you."



"be angry with I after you have read my letter."



"how can I read it when you will not let I go?"



certainly his arm be round she, but she do not make the least

effort to get away from they.



"_shall_ I let you go?"



"if you like."



"I don't like."



he press she close to he.



"tell I, first, how you get my flower last night."



"why, you puss, I have tell you twice already."



"I forget it, I want to hear it again."



these small deception be permissible between lover, when they be

use to such felicitous purpose. he tell she again, and her bosom

pant, and her heart beat, and a proud and tender light shine in her

eye as he describe the mad gallop he have take; how her face be

ever before he, urge he on; how he have win the flower; the way

the woman have say, "o, if it's for that!" then the ride back, singe

as he rode----



"singing!" she exclaim, interrupt he. "o, you don't tell I

that last night. I know you have leave something out."



"I do sing, and the tree hear I."



"what song be it, sir?"



"Philip!"



"Philip, then. what song do you sing?"



"no song at all--yes, the sweetest song! a song with only one word to

it."



"with only one word to it! dear I I know some, and I don't know

that--and the sweetest song, you say."



"the sweetest, the dear, the good word in the world."



"what word be it?"



"Margaret--Margaret--Margaret!"



"o Philip! and everybody hear it!"



"I leave it behind I--no, I don't; I wouldn't part with it. part with

it! never, while my heart beat! yet I do lose it too, for an echo

steal it--and I hear it singe Margaret as I ride on."



they be talk together in the open; there be a light in the sky,

but the moon have not yet rise. ten minute afterwards he say:



"now read my letter."



"I cann't see it," place her eye close to it; "it's too dark."



"not for my eye." he bend his head to her; their cheek touch.

"'dear madam,' he commence, 'my name be Philip Rowe----'"



"what a stupid commencement!" she say, laugh.



"be it? wait. perhaps it will improve far on. 'my name be Philip

Rowe. I be twenty-six year of age, and I be an Englishman, bear in

Devonshire. I have a half share in a rich claim on a rich quartz reef.

I love your daughter----'"



"o, o," she cry, tremble from happiness. "it's to my mother. and

you're from Devonshire. Mother have friend in Devonshire. one in

particular, that she have often talk of. I've never be there. go

on, Philip. 'I love your daughter.' do you, do you, Philip?"



"do I, my darling?" he say passionately. "listen to my heart. what

do it beat but Margaret, Margaret? I come here to find my life, and

I have find she. I love you with all my soul. I never know what a

beautiful thing life be until I see your dear face."



this be heaven to she to hear. presently, "go on, Philip,

I love your daughter.'"



"'and she love I.'"



"O, Philip, who tell you? what be you do, sir?"



"I be listen to your heart, my darling."



"and what do it say! as if it could speak! what do it say, sir?"



"I think I hear it. I think it beat for I."



so inexpressibly tender be his tone, that her arm creep round his

neck, and she sigh, "it do, Philip; it do!"



it be the proud, happy moment in his life. a blissful silence

encompass they.



"I haven't much more to read," he say, and add cunningly, "where

do I leave off?"



"you know, Philip."



"no, but tell I."



"'and she love I,'" she whisper.



"my darling! 'I love your daughter, and she love I. I cannot make a

lady of she, for she be that already, thank to you.' ben't that

good?" he ask, break off.



"yes. go on; go on. I want to hear the end."



"'I will do all in my power to make she happy; and I write with her

permission, to ask you to allow I to subscribe myself, in every

letter that follow this, your affectionate son, Philip Rowe.' there!"



"and how can you see to read such a bold letter, sir? my eye be as

good as your, and there's no light."



"I do not read with my eye, dear Margaret."



"with what then, Philip? you be full of riddle."



"with my heart, my darling."









CHAPTER XII.



"pray for RAIN, MY DARLING."





"we be get along finely," say William Smith, rub his hand

briskly as he look about with satisfaction upon the busy scene. the

crush machine be nearly fix. it be a Berdan's, with twelve

stamper to pound the stone to dust. the steam-engine be in fine

order. the glistening white quicksilver be ready for the work of

amalgamation with the bright red gold. the dam be build and ready for

water.



William Smith have good reason to feel proud, for by his enterprise he

have people this hitherto desert spot. a hundred tent of drill, and

a few more pretentious with wall build of slab, be scatter

about, and by a wave of his hand three hundred strong man have find

profitable employment. some have their wife with they, and goat and

child scamper about the gully and over the adjacent hill. the

store, the principal one of which and the most favour by the

gold-digger belong to William Smith, be do a roar business.

a wise man, William Smith; no half-hearted worker; what he do be

thoroughly do. he be an honest straightforward man too, drive a

hard bargain always, and always to his own advantage; but those he

deal with have their gain also, and they know that his word be to

be depend upon down to the last letter. wherever he compete he take

the lead, and deservedly. his hotel be the good in Silver Creek; the

good accommodation be to be find there, the good liquor be to be

obtain there. his theatre be a model of comfort. his store on the

Margaret Reef (I have not have time before to tell you that Philip have

christen it the "Margaret," immediately he know the name of his

sweetheart) be as complete as it be possible for a store on the

gold-digging to be. he sell the good of everything--the good and

nattiest water-tight boot with square toe and clean-cut nail in the

sol, the strong lace, the stout and sound drill and calico

for tent and fly, the trusty steel for gad, the most

serviceable serge and Scotch twill shirt, the fine pea-jacket, the

most expensive cabbage-tree and Panama hat, the good tobacco, and

everything else of the first quality. his store be the post-office,

and there be a corner in it where the gold-digger could write their

letter and read the _Silver Creek Herald_ and the _Silver Creek

Mercury_. he have plan road, and have some idea of use his

influence for the lay-out of a township by the Government. in his

way, William Smith be a small Moses; with room and opportunity and a

thousand man at his back he could have lay the solid foundation of a

great nation. he have the true legislative faculty for such an

undertaking, and I be sure that he would have look after Number one.

the bricklayer who could only earn thirty shilling a week in England,

might have become a ruler of man.



the scene, altogether, that be to be witness day and night on the

Margaret Reef be such as never can be witness in an old country. in

civilised country man seem to go about their work with a sadness

upon they, and as if they be labour under some kind of

oppression. in such-like place as I be describe, man rise in the

morning and set about their work with smile and vigour, and hearty

cheerfulness. in the old country it be, "it's a hard thing to have to

work like this! alas!" in the new country it be, "come along, boy,

with a will! Hurrah!"



I have say that the dam be build and ready for water. William Smith

say the same thing to Philip at the conclusion of a conversation. he

be in high spirit; there be two hundred and fifty ton of quartz

wait to be crush, lie in great heap near the shaft. half of it

be burn, and be ready for the machine; the other half be pile on

the wood kiln and be blaze away, fill the air with not the

pleasantest arsenical fume. new shaft be be sink along the

brow of the Margaret Reef, and one or two be begin to yield

gold-bear stone.



"what do you think it will crush?" ask Philip of William Smith, as

they stand by a heap of the quartz which have be burn.



William Smith poke about the stone and average it, a piece from one

place, a piece from another, a piece from another. he see plenty of

gold in it.



"about nine ounce to the ton, I should say," reply William Smith.

"we'll first crush fifty ton, and wash up and see what the yield be.

then we'll go straight on with two hundred ton, and get the big

cake of gold that have ever be see in Silver Creek and exhibit it in

High Street. it'll do the digging good."



"when shall we commence to crush?"



"we shall be ready in three day. all we want be water in the dam. now

be the time to pray for rain."



Philip go straight to Margaret, as one go to one's high-priest.



"pray for rain, my darling," he say, "pray for rain;" and tell she

the reason why.



Margaret pray for rain, obediently, as she have be bid, and

pray for it so hard that, whether you will believe it or not, such a

downpour commence on Silver Creek at ten o'clock that night as have

never be witness by the old inhabitant--a veteran of two year

or less. Silver Creek overflow its bank, and the low part of the

township be flood. Philip be wild with joy.



"you duck!" he say to Margaret--he be in the theatre when the rain

commenced--"this be all your doing!"



we sober-go person know, of course, that it be only a

coincidence. Margaret, however, smile demurely. she be quite ready

to take the credit of it; she would not have be a woman else. but it

_be_ rather a stretch on Philip's part.



William Smith look anxious. he want rain, but he be a little bit

afraid of such a downpour as this, think that the dam might not be

strong enough to bear it. Philip run to Margaret, and tell she of

William Smith's fear.



"the dam not strong enough!" she exclaim. "o, but it be!"



Philip be satisfied. the most profound logic could not have so

convince he of the soundness of the dam. he could not convince

William Smith, however, for Smith be not in love. that enterprise

person want to set out at once for the Margaret Reef, but it be

impossible to get there in such a storm. rage torrent be in the

way. Smith fret that he could not whistle they aside. but he do

not fret long; he accept the inevitable with a grimace. Philip

accept it in a very different fashion; but then it be pleasant to

he, for it compel he to remain for the night in the hotel where

Margaret be. he bless the rain that keep he by Margaret's side. he

have also a little private business to do with Mr. Hart. Margaret have

relate to he the incident on the road which have lead to the baby

become a shareholder in Hart's Star Dramatic Company, and how that

it be Mr. Hart who have suggest it. she enact the entire scene,

and burlesque the Leading Lady in fine style. Philip, who be fond of

child, be mightily pleased, and be loud in his praise of Mr.

Hart, and Margaret chime in. she love the old man; and, indeed, they

both have occasion to be grateful to he. between they they have

concoct a plan--that be to say, Philip have concoct it, and

Margaret have say, "yes, yes," to everything; which, in Philip's eye,

make she the author of it. what that plan be will now be see.



the performance conclude at eleven o'clock: the roof of the theatre

be make of zinc, and the rain fall on it so heavily and loudly that

not a word could be hear within the wall. but the actor go on

with their part nevertheless, and to keep the audience in good

humour, introduce dance in the piece, and play such impromptu

antic that the gold-digger be rather pleased with the storm than

otherwise.



when the performance be at an end, Philip and Margaret stand at the

side-scene, talk softly over their plan concern Mr. Hart. what

they really have to say about it might have occupy two minute--but

it take they twenty, they be such bungler.



"now I shall go to Mr. Hart," say Philip, and kiss Margaret.



the part he be play in those happy day be full of cue for

kiss. there may have be mean in the kiss; there be certainly

none in the cue.



I think that Philip must have speak this particular cue, "now I shall

go to Mr. Hart," at least a dozen time (invariably, of course, use

it as a fresh cue) before he attempt to stir from Margaret's side.

but at length he do say, with something like determination:



"now I must really go."



Margaret reply with a sigh, "yes, Philip, you must."



even then, I think, he would not have go, if they have not be

disturb in their love-making.



"when it be all settle," say Margaret, "run up to my room and knock

at the door; then I will come down and give Mr. Hart a good hug and

half-a-dozen kiss."



Philip look blank at this.



"you goose!" say Margaret. "I have kiss he I don't know how many

time. why, he's over sixty! and don't you think he deserve it, sir,

for the care he have take of I."



"of course," respond Philip, the cloud in his face clearing. "I _be_

a goose. I know you wouldn't kiss a young man--unless it be I."



"not a much young man," reply Margaret with a merry laugh, as she

run away from he.









CHAPTER XIII.



"what if there be villain and scoundrel in the world?" he cry,

"we will never lose our faith in GOD and MAN--never! never! never!"





Philip watch until Margaret be out of sight, and then walk slowly

to Mr. Hart's room, and knock at the door, but receive no answer.

he stroll into the bar of the hotel, but could not see Mr. Hart.



"he must be in his room," quoth Philip to himself. "there be a light

there."



he knock at the door again, and still receive no answer, turn

the handle, and find the door unfastened. he enter the room, and

see Mr. Hart sit before his little table with his head bury in

his hand.



"ah! you're there," say Philip, close the door behind he, and

draw a chair to the table. "I want to say something particular to

you."



Mr. Hart, with a wave of his hand, motion the young man to proceed.



Philip be flush and excited, and somewhat nervous as to how his

mission would be receive; and be in this condition he do not

observe any change in Mr. Hart's face or manner.



"this be how it be," he continue. "you make I an offer for a share

in my claim once, and I refuse it. well, I be wrong in refuse, and

I want to accept it now. don't think there's any favour in it, or that

the claim be any well or any bad than it be. the stone be look

splendid, and now that the rain be fall the dam will be fill, and

we shall commence to crush directly it clear up. I want you to give

I two hundred and fifty pound for a quarter of my half-share. that

be an eighth part of the claim, and it set the claim at a good

price--two thousand pound; and I'll make you a bet of three hundred

pound, and stake the money, that in less than six week your share of

the profit will amount to three time as much as I ask you for it.

there, that be how it be. now say 'do!' like a good fellow, and

place I under an obligation to you for life. I know you have the

money."



he blurt out these word, not coherently and smoothly as they be

write here, but in as bungle a manner as can well be imagine. he

stammer, he hesitate, he repeat his word, but at length he have

explain himself. Mr. Hart have listen quietly, the only motion he

make be one which would hide his face more effectually from Philip.

when Philip have finish his lame speech and be wait for an

answer, he notice that Mr. Hart's trunk be open, and that all its

content be scatter about the floor; indeed the whole room be in

a state of confusion. Mr. Hart speak in a low tone.



"you offer I a fourth of your share for two hundred and fifty

pound."



"yes, and I have the agreement in duplicate in my pocket, with my name

to it. I have it draw out to-day by a lawyer. it only want your

signature, and the thing be settle."



"and you will bet I three hundred pound, stake the money, that in

less than six week I shall receive back for my share of the profit

three time as much as I give you for it."



"that's it."



"with whom will you stake the money?"



"with you."



"so that I shall really have in hand fifty pound more than you ask

for the share."



"that's it; but why so many word? say, 'done and do!'"



Philip be on thorn while the matter be unsettled.



"I must clearly understand," say Mr. Hart, in the same low tone,

which, indeed, he preserve throughout this part of the conversation

"before I can say anything to the offer. I want to be certain that you

mean honestly by I. the world be full of thief. there be plenty of

roguery about."



"that's true," reply Philip complacently; "I'm a bit of a rogue

myself."



"and," proceed Mr. Hart, with a strange hesitation in his voice,

"suppose the claim to be utterly worthless, at the end of six week

I shall be sure to be fifty pound in pocket?"



"you will be more than that in pocket. the claim's a good one; there's

no telling how much gold be in it."



Mr. Hart pause, to steady himself. "I'm not much of an arithmetician;

I be always a bad hand at figure; but I can see that I must be a

gainer if I accept your offer."



"I hope you will be."



"your claim be a rich one. all the digger say so."



"we shall make a fortune out of it in three month," reply Philip,

with a bright smile--"you, and all of we."



"on the first day I see you----"



"when you pull the centipede out of my hair," interrupt Philip. "a

lucky day for I, that be. good luck to you, old fellow! yes: on the

first day you see I--go on."



"I offer you, if you remember, a hundred and twenty pound for a

small share in the claim."



"I remember."



"and you refuse, say you would want twenty time as much."



"I speak like a fool; I don't know you then." again Mr. Hart pause.



"Philip," he say presently, in a tremulous tone, "why do you make I

this offer?"



Philip hang his head upon his breast, and with a slight trembling of

his low lip, reply softly:



"because I love you."



a sudden rush of tear come into Mr. Hart's eye, and he lay his head

upon his arm.



"for God's sake don't do that!" cry Philip, rise hurriedly, and

look about he in distress. "if I've say anything to hurt you,

forgive I. I'm a great hulking brute; Margaret will never look at I

again. there, there, old fellow!"



and Philip, whose heart be as tender as a woman's and whose first

intention have be to fly from the room, and dash through the storm,

knelt by the side of Mr. Hart, and use word as gentle, and action

as fond as though he be kneel by the side of a child. and all the

time he do this his great limb be tremble, and the tear be

run down his strong beard. Mr. Hart raise his head, which be now

on a level with Philip's, and with no more shame or awkwardness than a

child would exhibit, put his arm across Philip's shoulder and kiss

he.



I draw a veil over the next few moment; neither of they speak during

that time, but their heart be throb with eloquent and tender

emotion.



then say Mr. Hart, when he be calm:



"Philip, my son, you have teach I a lesson; you have make my heart

green again. it be turn bitter against all man, and you have

soften it, and restore my faith. ah, how proud your father must be

of such a son!"



Philip groan. "I run away from he; I be a scapegrace at home, and

I cause the dear old fellow many a heartache. never mind. I will

repay he; I know well now."



"you do nothing wrong, my dear boy, I be sure."



"I almost break his heart, I think. I try his patience sorely. he

send I to Cambridge to do honour to his name, and I do my good to

disgrace it. I go home with a long tail of debt behind I; he pay

they, and say, 'never mind, my lad; promise I that you will not do

so again; see here, I will double your allowance.' I promise he, and

take the double allowance, and get into debt again. it hurt he--I see

that. that I should break a promise to he, who have never break one

to I, who have never say a harsh word to I, make he wince. again he

pay my debt; again I promise; again I break my word. More than

that: I involve the son of a friend of his, who give his name for I

to the money-lender. well, I couldn't face he the third time. I send

he a list of my debt, and I run away. the good thing I could do--and

the bad, I think, for he love I, the dear old dad!"



"you will live to repay he."



"I will do my good. I will go home to he, with my dear Margaret on my

arm, and say--and say, 'dear old dad----'"



but he break down here, and it be Mr. Hart's turn to console he. he

be not long in this mood. he jump to his foot, and with a great

shake of his shoulder cry:



"enough about I! you be in trouble. what be it?"



"I cannot buy the share you offer I, Philip."



"why? you have money enough, and you _shall_ buy it. you shall! I'll

drag the money out of your box. o, I know where you keep it, and I'm

strong enough to do what I say."



"you'll find no money there, Philip," say Mr. Hart, sadly.



"you don't mean to say you've be speculate and lose it!" say

Philip, pull a long face.



"no, I have not lose it by speculation, but it be go all the same.

see here, Philip, my son. I have save nearly four hundred pound, and

I have almost make up my mind to go home and see my daughter at the end

of this three month' engagement. it would have be madness to do so

when, by stay here for three month long, I might have double my

saving, which be all for she; but I be yearn to hold she in my

arm, and press my darling to my heart. ah, Philip! you don't know

what a father's love be--you may, one day, my boy, and then you will

understand my feeling. Prudence say, 'stay a little while long;'

but my heart's yearn beat prudence out of the field. it say to I,

'you be an old man; young as you feel, you may break down. let your

daughter see you when you be strong, and able, old as you be, to

protect and advise she. don't wait till you be decrepit and feeble,

when she cannot have faith and confidence in you. you have save money

enough three time during the last seven year, and each time you have

stay a little long, and lose it. go now, and don't tempt bad

fortune again.' about my having save money enough three time,

Philip, it be true, and true that I have lose it, lose it by trust

friend, who deceive I, and play I false. well, I begin to get

frighten by these reflection, and to-day, you know, the letter by

the Overland Mail camp up to Silver Creek. among they be a letter for

I by my daughter, a letter fill with such expression of love and

affection that I should have be less than a man not to have hunger

for a sight of she. I resolve; I would go home when the engagement

here terminate. I reckon that I could land in England with six

hundred pound. after the theatre be close, I come into my room, and

open my box, to count my money as a miser do. how often have I

do it, and with what different feeling from those which must

animate a miser! imagine my despair, my boy, when I find that I have

be rob. Philip, I haven't a shilling in the world! once more I be

leave a beggar. it be while I be contemplate the dreary prospect

before I that you come in. in my heart I be curse all mankind, and

a terrible feeling of doubt of high thing be creep into my

mind. but your noble offer have restore my faith again. what if there

be villain and scoundrel in the world!" he cry, stand up

before the admire Philip. "let they creep, and crawl, and plunder,

and grow rich; and then die their death of shame! we will never lose

our faith in God and man--never, never, never! ay, though our dear

heart's wish may never be gratify, we will bow our head

reverently, and believe in goodness, and hope to the last!"



he hold out his hand, and Philip take it. the grasp be to the young

man as though he be pledge himself to a life of honour and

integrity.



"in my young day," continue Mr. Hart, with a soft light in his eye,

"I have a friend; in my young day I love a woman as truly as you love

Margaret. I have not see my friend for thirty year. I have not

receive line or message from he, nor he from I, and he be still my

friend, and I be his. the woman I love do not love I, and I go

from her sight. but though in after year I love another woman who

become my wife, and who give I my daughter, the memory of the first

have never leave I, and I think of she with tenderness still. these and

other remembrance have in a measure sustain my faith, and, I humbly

hope, purify my life. shall I turn a misanthrope now in my old age,

and snarl at mankind because I have be deceive for the dozenth

time? no, Philip, no! it would be rob life of all its sweetness."



but in spite of this generous outburst, his grief be too powerful to

be thus suddenly conquer, and his lip quiver again with emotion

as he think of his loss.



"leave I now, Philip," he say. "I cannot accept your offer, but

while my heart beat, you have a place in it."



he have barely utter these word when the storm without grow more

furious. the rain come down like a flood. the wind rattle about the

wooden wall of the hotel to such an extent that it seem as though

the building could not possibly hold together. a flash of lightning,

so vivid that it almost blind they, pierce the ground, and at its

heel follow a peal of thunder so terrible that it shake the very

foundation of the earth. they stand spell-bind. when sight and

hearing be restore to they, they hear what sound like a great

crash outside, mingle with human cry; but their attention be

divert from these by the appearance of Margaret, white and

tremble, at the door.









CHAPTER XIV.



"this be like the dawn of life, MY SWEET."





"I be frightened," she murmur, and run into her lover's arm, and

hide her face in his breast, and tremblingly ask if the world be

come to an end.



Philip, who be really startle by the fury of the storm, recover

his self-possession the moment he see Margaret. lover be not only

proverbially, but actually selfish. as Philip embrace Margaret, and

press she to his breast, I do not believe he care a pin for the

storm--nor Margaret either. she feel quite safe in his arm, but,

womanlike, she still express her fear.



"O, Philip!"



cling close to he.



"there be nothing to be frighten at, darling," say he.



"it be come to an end, I know," she murmur (mean the world),

"but it be a comfort to die in your arm!"



"it will be a great comfort to live in they," reply Philip, half

gaily.



she reprove he, ask, "how could he, at such a time?" and murmur

that it be wicked to think of such thing (never mention what

thing) in the midst of such terrible going-on. I doubt if any other

two person in the hotel, speak so softly; could have hear one

another, but these two be lover, and their lip almost, perhaps

quite touch. the storm be rage so furiously, and there be such a

din and confusion all around they, wind blow, thunder thundering,

and people shout, that Mr. Hart have to raise his voice very high

when he speak, so that Philip might hear it.



"something have occur," he say; "do you hear the crash?"



Philip nod that he have hear it.



"it be not all thunder. Mischief have be do; I shall go out and

see."



"I will go too," say Philip.



"and leave I?" cry Margaret.



he would have find it difficult to do so, she cling to he so

closely.



"no," he answer; "come along with we."



Philip catch up a blanket, and wrap his Margaret in it from head

to foot. all be dark outside, except when the lightning light up the

scene.



"keep close, Margaret," say Philip.



as if she need tell!



"a black night, indeed," say Mr. Hart, hold his hand before his

eye; "a black night, in every sense of the word. one want sailor'

eye at such a time. why, where's the theatre?"



a flash of lightning have reveal to he the space where the theatre

have stand, but the roof be no long visible. their form have be

recognise in the flash.



"be that you, Hart?" cry a hearty voice.



it be William Smith who speak, and his voice be as cheery and as

ring as the blast of a silver trumpet.



"yes."



"who be that with you?"



"Philip."



"ah, Philip! if the dam have stand, our fortune's make, Philip."



"the dam's all right!" shout Philip.



(please to remember that there _could_ be no doubt about the safety of

the dam, Margaret's lip having insure it.)



"I hope so," shout William Smith. "it'll be a bit of good luck to

make up for a bit of bad. Mr. Hart, the theatre's down!"



Mr. Hart groan.



"it need but that," he murmur.



"you could play a piece now with real thunder and lightning,"

continue William Smith, at the top of his voice. "why don't you

speak? I suppose you're down in the mouth because your theatre's all

to piece! never say die, man!"



Mr. Hart say nothing. this stroke of bad fortune come so close upon

the loss of his saving almost crush he.



"we'll have it up again in less than a week," cry the plucky

speculator. "William Smith's hard to beat!"



he really seem to enjoy it. if those who have know he in London

could have see and hear he now, they would scarcely have believe.

in the old country he be a mouse; in the new country he be a man.

the wind be enough to blow they away, and it be impossible for they

smile, and fresh as a flower, with the rain glisten on his face

"by Jove!" say Philip, under his breath, gaze at she in silent

there be nothing sham about his Margaret, he think; she be genuine

prize? have any other man in the world ever be so blessed?

"we'll make a night of it," say Philip.



all this while he have forget Mr. Hart's loss, but it flash upon

transact; it willn't take five minute."



Obedient Margaret move a few pace away, and close her eye, and

whisper a word in her ear. then he look about he for pen and ink,

and bring they to the table.



"now," he say, in a low tone to Mr. Hart, "please to sign these

paper."



he take from his pocket the duplicate agreement, by which he sell,

and Mr. Hart buy, a fourth of his share in the claim on the

Margaret Reef. Mr. Hart gently shake his head. but Philip would not be

deny. he press and argue, and argue and press, and even

listen. but still he would not sign.



"Margaret," say Philip, "come and help I."



up jump Margaret, and run to the table.



"this be how it be," say Philip, appeal to she, but Mr. Hart

interrupt he.



"no, no; let I explain."



"stop his mouth, Margaret!"



Margaret place her small hand on Mr. Hart's mouth, have to encircle

"o, you may kiss it!" say she. "Philip will not be angry, nor will

I."



"I angry!" exclaim Philip, "with he or you. keep your hand there,

and let he kiss it as often as he like."



she give Philip her other hand as a reward, and he warm it in his.



"this be how it be, Margaret----" and Philip explain the matter to

she be grave and silent when his story be finish, out of sympathy

"my dear child!" he remonstrate.



"you do! you know you do! for if you don't sign, and become a

my own way in this."



"do you hear what she say?" shout Philip, triumphantly. "and do you

intend to part we for ever?"



the upshot of it all be that Mr. Hart be compel to yield; but he

"God bless you, my dear!" say Mr. Hart, and would have continue his
