"so after all," say Philip, "it's only lend you the money for a

week or two."



"it be give I the gold," observe Mr. Hart.



"you give I Margaret," reply Philip softly; "and do you think she's

not worth more than all the gold in the world! I be your debtor still,

and shall be all my life."



Delicious word, both to utter and hear.



they sit together until sunrise, and Margaret fall asleep in her

lover's arm. live there the man who have not enjoy some such

heavenly minute as these? Philip taste then the most perfect

happiness in his life.



when the sun rise, the storm clear away. Margaret awoke, and sigh

and blush, and look tenderly at Philip, and Mr. Hart find

something so interesting at his window that he be compel to keep

his back to they. they forgave the rudeness; and presently come also

to the window, and look out upon a glorious sight. the sky be

glow with grand colour. broad masse of golden light fringe with

purple, which change gradually to crimson, rise from the dip of the

horizon. brightly shine the sun in its bed; the sky be dot with

feather-cloud of rosy red in the east, and fairy island of the

lovely shade of blue, fleck with white, move towards they from

the west. raindrop seem to hang, like glisten eye, between

cloud and land; the heaven laugh; all be sweet, and fresh, and

beautiful.



so, in another land, which lie beneath they, and on another morning,

when summer be wane, the old man shall stand, after a strange and

eventful night, gaze on the sunrise with grateful eye and grateful

heart, embrace she who be dearer to he than his heart's blood.



"this be like the dawn of life, my sweet!" whisper Philip to

Margaret.



"of _our_ life, Philip," she whisper.



Mr. Hart hear they.



"a happy dawn," he pray. "may it bring a happy day!"



but prayer could not avert what be soon to come.









CHAPTER XV.



PHILIP be convinced of the EFFICACY of MARGARET'S PRAYERS.





William Smith, the practical, the indefatigable, the restless, the

dauntless, the man of action, who seemingly could do without sleep,

and who have become a hero by contact with opportunity--(well,

that be my opinion, and I alone be responsible for what be here

written)--William Smith, I say, burst into the room, cry:



"Come, Philip, come! to the Margaret reef!"



Margaret dart out of Philip's arm; she would not let all the world

see. Smith know how matter stand between Philip and Margaret, and he

wink at Mr. Hart, and do not look at the lover--that be,

significantly.



"ah!" say Philip, reluctantly come back to earth--and water, I

might say; "the dam!"



"yes," say William Smith, "the dam. I tell you you might pray for

rain. now pray for the dam."



"I know a prayer," think Philip and pray; "Margaret!"



"you get to bed, my girl," say William Smith to Margaret; "all the

danger's over now, and all the harm's do. the horse be outside."



"I shall want one," put in Mr. Hart.



"you!" exclaim William Smith. "what interest have you in the dam?

see to your theatre."



"what interest!" say Philip. "why, he happen to be a shareholder in

the Margaret Reef. don't you know?"



"no; but I'm glad to hear it. good luck to the Margaret, and all

concerned in it. I'll have a horse ready for you in a jiffy." (a new

kind of conveyance for a horse to be harness to.)



out he go again, and before he return, Margaret have disappear,

first tell Philip that she be go to pray for the dam. Philip

be satisfied that her pray be well than the good of puddle.

before the man mount, they have a look at the theatre; it be a mass

of ruin. the wind only have not only blow it down, but have blow

piece of it mile away. in a gully, four mile from the spot, into

which a pick have not yet be stick, the first thing that be find

some month afterwards by man who go to seek for gold be a scratch

wig belong to the Low Comedian: which puzzle the prospector. they

do not go to that gully to find scratch wig. some part of the

wardrobe belong to the actor be bury beneath the ruin of the

theatre, but a great deal have be blow away. Most of it be bring

back, at odd time, by digger and their wife, who have rare laugh

over the queer vestment. some of they make a great commotion in the

township one day, by march into High Street, dress most absurdly.

Charles the Second, in a red wig and with Macbeth's shield on his arm,

be follow by Clown, with heavy eyebrow, moustache, and Lord

dundreary whisker; behind he come one who be half roman and half

Scotchman; and a perfect piece of patchwork bring up the rear. a

fine jollification follow, you may be sure, when they halt at the

Rose, Shamrock and Thistle.



as William Smith and his companion be gaze on the ruin of the

theatre, a dozen labourer come up, and under the direction of one

begin to clear away the fall timber. Mr. Hart and Philip look to

William Smith for an explanation. he give it they. while the storm be

rage, he have make a contract for a new theatre. it might almost be

think that he sleep with one eye open. Mr. Hart say as much.

William Smith laugh.



"it would be a useful thing to be able to do," he say. "but what be

you wonder at? William Smith never lose a day."



he be a kind of man to put heart into man when misfortune overtake

they. he would say, "if bad fortune give I a slap in the face, I

don't lay down and whimper." (he be not particular as to his grammar,

although he have a proper respect for knowledge and education.) "I

don't lay down and whimper," say he; "I tuck up my sleeve, and set

to--with a will."



when they be in the saddle, and ride along towards the Margaret

Reef, they see evidence of the same kind of spirit in other man.

number of tent have be literally tear into shred by the storm;

valuable shaft have fall in; tool and windlass and puddling

machine have be sweep away by the flood, which in many place have

make hill of gully and gully of hill. all be confusion, but man

be work everywhere, with goodwill, to repair the damage. very

different be the face of these man and woman from the face of some

poor people I see a short time since, in the crowded city in which

these word be write, after an extraordinary high tide in the

river, the water of which have overflow its bank, and wash into

the cellar where they live and sleep. in the new country the man and

woman bustle about vigorously, with face almost cheerful; in the

old, they stand, bang their head dolefully, and with not spirit

enough amongst they to make one good worker out of a hundred. but the

case be different.



as William Smith and his companion ride along, look this way and

that, Philip suddenly cry "o!" as though he be shoot, and turn his

horse's head to the west, whereas the Margaret Reef lie to the north

of they. away he gallop, as though for dear life, with no thought of

the Margaret Reef in his mind, and William Smith and Mr. Hart follow

he. they go only some five hundred yard, but the horse have to

make some big leap over new watercourse in that short distance.

Philip jump off his horse, and tie the animal to a fallen tree,

set to work help some man to dig the earth away from a tent which

have be nearly bury by the caving in of a hill. see what be the

matter, William Smith, who be at first dispose to grumble, jump

off _his_ horse, and in another minute he and Mr. Hart be by the

side of Philip, with their sleeve tuck up. Philip work like a

young Hercules, and when sufficient of the earth be clear away, he

cut a great gash in the canvas roof, and, stoop over with a rope

tie round his waist, tenderly lift two child from the chasm, and

hand they to the gold-digger. he be like a steam hammer, that can

come down one minute with an awful thump and beat ten ton of metal

into shape, and the next can come down with a tap gentle enough to

fashion a thin leaf into the likeness of a delicate flower. after the

two child come a woman, whom he raise in his arm as though she

weigh about an ounce, and at sight of whom the gold-digger, see

that she be alive and comparatively unhurt, raise a great shout. and

one, her husband, who be lie on the ground, cripple, burst into a

passion of grateful tear. I should like to tell you the story of this

family, but I have not time just now. Philip and his companion could

scarcely escape from the person they have help to rescue, but they

have other work to look to, and having ascertain that there be no

more human life to be save, they mount their horse, and resume

their course. at the foot of the range, on the other side of which the

dam lie, Philip pause for a moment to breathe the spell of Margaret's

name, but William Smith dash straight on. the first thing that meet

their sight be wreck of canvas tent and break tent-pole lie

about. William Smith bite his nether lip, but say not a word. he be

already calculate the cost of another and a strong dam; what he

chiefly regret be the waste of time and water. the pant horse

reach the brow of the range, and the man leap off. William Smith

do not stop to ask question of his workman, but run swiftly onward,

to see with his own eye. he be an old and a weak man than

Philip, who race at his heel, but he be the first to reach the dam.



"Hurrah!" he scream. "Hurrah! hurrah!" and Philip follow suit, and

make the hill resound again with his joyous shout.



a fair sheet of water lie before they, wink in the eye of the sun.

the head man--I cannot call he master; there be no such thing, in

the sense that we in England understand it--meet William Smith with a

smile face, and they shake hand. but both of they sober down

within a minute.



"a tolerable piece of work this of your," observe William Smith, in

an off-hand way.



"middlingish," be the reply, in an indifferent tone.



this imply that make such a dam as this be nothing to he. give

he a real difficult job to accomplish, such as join two sea, or

level a mountain a few thousand of foot in height, or make a

new river within a week or so, and then you might be able to see what

he could do. to construct such a dam as this, however, be really no

joke. it be a masterly piece of work, and it be execute in a

masterly manner; there be not a flaw in not it, a crack in its side.

they examine it carefully, critically.



"if it will stand such a storm as last night," say William Smith, "it

will stand anything."



Philip, as you may guess, be overjoy; but he be unjust. he give

all the credit to Margaret. he compliment the responsible man in a

cool way, which imply, "it be capitally do; but you have Margaret

to thank for it, you know."



Philip's faint praise do not affect the contractor. he be not

vain-glorious; he have undertake a piece of work, and have do it

well, and be satisfied, having be well pay for it.









CHAPTER XVI.



the christening of the WILLIAM SMITH.



before two day have pass, the fire be light in the boiler, and

the quartz-crush machine commence its merry rub-a-dub-dub. the

ugly black rook that be willnot to cluster in huge flock in the once

deserted wood and make night hideous with their rusty voice, cease

for a time their harsh cawing and their seemingly interminable

circular flight--wonder, doubtless, as the sound reach their

ear, what new and strange monster it be that have invade their

domain. for it be evening when the iron-shod stamper first begin to

thump. it be but a trial. before actual work commence, a little

ceremony have to be perform. the quartz-crush machine have to be

christen.



William Smith mention this to Philip, say it be a necessary

ceremony.



"all right," say Philip, and run straight to his princess.



the First Old Woman be with Margaret; they be snip up old

dress, and make they into late new one. a new piece be to be

play at the theatre that evening.



"Margaret," say Philip, "we be go to have a christening."



"o, O!" cry the First Old Woman, and set off laugh.



Philip do not condescend to notice she, nor would he so much as smile

at a mock baby she fashion in a moment out of the dress piece, and

dandle in her arm. Margaret do, and pull it away from she.



"we be go to christen the machine, Margaret."



"who be to be godmother?" inquire the First Old Woman briskly.



"who!" exclaim Philip. "why, who but Margaret, I should like to

know."



Margaret's eye sparkle more brightly. the proposition delight she.



"you'll have to break a bottle of Moselle against the machine,

Margaret. you would like to do it, wouldn't you?"



Margaret nod, and give Philip a bright look. "o, don't make a

stranger of I!" cry the First Old Woman.



the remark be suggest by Philip's stoop over Margaret under the

pretence of whispering to she, but really to kiss she--be tempt

to do so by the look she have give he. William Smith join the

party.



"we've settle it all," say Philip to he.



"all what?"



"about the christening. Margaret will set the machine a-going."



but William Smith have settle it another way. "Margaret can christen

the next machine," he say. "the Warden's lady will christen this."



"the Warden's lady will do no such thing!" cry Philip.



"she have promise to do so," reply William Smith calmly. "don't be a

fool, Philip. who have it in his power to be our good friend in the

Margaret Reef? the Goldfields Warden. who grant lease, who settle

all dispute as to boundary and encroachment, who, in short, rule

Silver Creek? the Goldfields Warden. who rule the Goldfields Warden?

his wife. nothing can be clear."



Dissatisfied Philip refuse to see the logic of the argument. but

William Smith be wise in his generation; he be very desirous of

ingratiate himself into the good grace of the lady who be at the

head of society in Silver Creek, know the value of her influence.

he make further effort to convince Philip, but Philip would not be

convince. love and prudence be at dagger draw within he. William

Smith appeal to Margaret.



"you be a girl of sense. it be for Philip's good."



"Mr. Smith be right," say Margaret to Philip. "I don't care a pin

about it."



she say this with a pang of disappointment, for she do wish to

christen the machine; but she recognise the soundness of William

Smith's argument. so Philip be overrule.



I say it be to be a little ceremony. William Smith make it a big

one.



he prepare a great feast, and invite all the bigwig of Silver Creek

township to come to the christening. no infant be ever more honoured

than this iron baby with its twelve head of stamper and its iron

cradle ready to receive and imprison the gold. not one person refuse

the invitation, and a great many come who be not invite, and who,

be cordially welcome, go home in the evening with a skinful and

a bellyful. the Goldfields Warden, the police magistrate, the chief of

the police, the commissioner, the lawyer, the editor of local

paper, and all the less luminary of Silver Creek be present.

William Smith have capture a Judge, who happen to be pass that

way, within twenty mile of the township; and he be there, in all his

glory, and right well be he treat, and right well do he speak, and

do not say a cross word even when William Smith slap he

familiarly on the shoulder.



talk of your laying of foundation-stone by prince and noble and

member of parliament, with their set speech and stale platitude!

the present christening beat all such ceremony out of the field.

never could such a sight as this be see in the old country. free

hand, free heart; everybody serve alike; all stand together,

shoulder to shoulder, man to man. be thankful that I have not time to

describe the entire proceeding in detail. those who wish to read of

it more fully can send to Silver Creek for the _Herald_ and the

_Mercury_, where (suppose the copy not to be all sell) they will

find fourteen column of description--no less; and in small type, too.

there be a supplement to each paper, and William Smith buy a

thousand copy of each, and scatter they broadcast over the land

and over the sea. when his old mother in London receive the paper,

and have the account of the grand doing read to she, she could at

first hardly believe that she have bear he; but she soon recover

herself, and relate to the gossip who sit about she, and whom she

be regale (be quite a lady now with the money William Smith

regularly send she), insignificant incident in her son's baby life

which shadow forth the great position he be one day to make for

himself. if he have hear they, they would have be new to he, for he

have no remembrance of they. but when do a mother ever forget the

small trifle relate to the baby she suckle at her breast? in the

glowing report of the christening in the _Silver Creek Herald_ and

_mercury_ William Smith's name be mention ninety-seven time, and

there be a wonderful unanimity in the praise bestow upon he for

his enterprise. he deserve all the good thing that be say of he,

for such man as he be the life and soul of new community.



and all this time I have not tell you the name of the machine. well,

not a soul know it before the word pass the lip of the Judge, who

act as spokesman on the occasion. truth to tell, no one think of

it. be request by William Smith to perform the ceremony, the

Judge rise, and stand on an eminence before the great baby, say it

strike he as a strange thing that when he ask William Smith what

be to be the name of the infant, William Smith scratch his head,

and say he do not know.



"it show the modesty of the man," say the Judge, assume a judicial

attitude--"and true greatness lie in modesty--not to have think of

the only name which this iron infant can appropriately bear." (William

Smith chuckle slyly at this. the idea of call he modest! a man

who could laugh in the face of a storm, as he could and do!) "I can

say nothing in praise of William Smith," continue the Judge, "that he

do not deserve. he be a representative man; in he enterprise,

industry, forethought, and that truly british quality, Pluck, be

typify. although I have only be in this thrive township a few

hour, I have hear enough of he, and see enough of he, to make I

wish to hear and see more; and I look forward to the day when I shall

welcome he as a member of the Legislative Assembly which make the

law for this prosperous colony. I hear that William Smith have make up

his mind that this machine shall turn out the large cake of retort

gold which the gold-digging have yet produce. he will do it, if he

have make up his mind to it, for nothing can check or frustrate

determination when it be in partnership with common sense and sound

judgment--as it be in this case. in christen this machine the

'William Smith,' I pay a fitting tribute to the man by whose

enterprise it be place on this spot;" et cetera, et cetera, et

cetera.



no occasion to speak of the cheer with which the Judge's oration be

receive; but loud and deafening as they be, they be nothing to

the volley that be give when the wife of the Goldfields Warden, as

the leader of fashion in Silver Creek, break the bottle of champagne

against the machine, and dub it the William Smith. then, everything

be prepare, the first shovelful of golden quartz out of Philip's

shaft be throw beneath the stamper by the Judge, and the machine

commence its music, and every man and woman present drank success to

it, in sparkle Number two Moselle. with three time three! and three

time three again! and again! and again!



after the lady of the Goldfields Warden break the bottle of champagne

against the machine, cunne William Smith beg her acceptance of

the handsome speciman of quartz and gold which have be find in

Philip's claim. she thank he and smile sweetly on he, and

converse with he, tell her husband afterwards that William Smith

be a most superior man, and have evidently move in good society in

the old country.



you understand that Margaret be at the ceremony of the christening.

she look lovely, not only in Philip's eye, but in the eye of all

the man and the unfashionable woman. would you like to know how she

be dress? her gown be of pale-blue muslin, daintily trim with

ribbon of the same colour. around her white throat and slender wrist

be frilling of delicate lace. and on her head be the sweetest hat,

whisper of which must have float across the sea and set the

fashion here, notwithstanding that other lady may claim the credit

of design it. it be a broad flap Leghorn hat, turn up

coquettishly on one side with a bunch of cornflower, with a

blue-gauze veil float behind it. and if any lady quarrel with

Margaret's taste, or with my description, and say I be wrong in my

particular, I shall be glad to hear from she.



the few fashionable lady--number not more than half-a-dozen--who

be present, act as they act in more civilised circle. they put up

their gold spectacle, and survey Margaret as they would have

survey a curiosity, and canvass and appraise her feature and her

clothe. they render she a kind of patronise justice; they say

she be pretty, and dress in fair taste, but they speak of she in a

tone that plainly proclaim she be not of their order. Margaret

care not a whit for their look; she be very happy. the gold-digger

regard she with pride and admiration, make it a sort of boast--as

though it reflect credit upon themselves--that Silver Creek could

show the prettiest girl on any gold digging; so Margaret be

surround with friend and admirer. she be present to the Judge,

who say many fine thing to she, and she not only carry off the

palm in beauty, but also in manner and conversation. Philip's joy and

delight in she know no bound; he discover fresh charm in she in

every new dress that she wear, and if she have not restrain he, he

would have make open love to she before all the people. she be

compel to give he a few moment now and then, so that he might

have opportunity for secretly press her hand. she be as proud of

he as he be of she, for as she be the handsome woman he be the

handsome man there.



the fine lady be more than gracious to he, sighing, no doubt,

that Heaven have make they such a man; but he have no eye for any but

Margaret. the Judge converse with he, and in conversation show off

his learning, as even such high and mighty person as judge be glad

of the opportunity of do, by introduce a quotation from Horace.

Philip immediately cap it by another; and the judge, after his

first surprise, there and then set his stamp upon Philip, and say in

the hearing of the fine lady that if Philip happen to come to

town, he would be glad to see he at his private house. this fly

round, and Philip become a king; even William Smith pale before he.

but William Smith be not to be hurt by this; so long as his

speculation be go on all right, he be satisfied. he long to

hear the music of his machine, beat out of the quartz the bright

gold, so much of which would fall to his share; for after it be

christen, it be only set go for a few minute; then it be

stop, so that the workman engage on it might make merry with the

other.



if you have see the jolly face of the jolly crowd of gold-digger and

their wife and child, and the pleased and more sober face of the

gentleman and their lady; if you have see the new tent with their

decoration which William Smith have put upon the ground; if you have

see the leaping, and racing, and other sport which William Smith

improvise, give handsome prize to the winner; if you have see the

attendant carve away at the beef and mutton, under the shadow of a

great canvas roof, without wall, for the purpose of showing, mayhap,

that every one be free to enter, and welcome to partake of the good

cheer provide; if you have see the waving of flag and hear the

laughter and clapping of hand--you would have think you be at an

english merrymaking of the very fine description. and a couple of

year ago the spot in which it be hold be a wild tract of country,

over which the foot of twenty white man have not pass. now hundred

of man be work vigorously there from sunrise to sunset, work

and hope and scheme and live their life, and thousand more

would soon flock around they; now the hollow echo their shout, and

the forest of tree fall beneath their axis; now the eye of forge

be glow in their lair, and the music of the anvil rang along

the hill; now digger sit around the blaze tree of a night, and

smoke their pipe, and tell their story, and speak of their

chance, or in more tender tone, of dear friend in the old land so

many thousand of mile across the sea; now the woman, as with

grateful heart they look at their healthy well-feed child run

about the hill or lie asleep in their cot, think of the future

with hope and pleasure; now man be on the earth and in its bowel,

tear the golden rock from its bed; now steam be do its wondrous

work, and gold be be send down to the port, to make man rich in

the old country, and to pay well wage to the work man.



some who be on the Margaret Reef on that day thought of these

thing. the Judge for one; William Smith for another; our dear friend

Mr. Hart for another; Philip for---- but no. I must be veracious;

Philip think of no such thing; he have enough to do with Margaret.



when the bustle of the ceremony be over, and people be more free to

act in accordance with their whim and fancy, Philip conduct

Margaret to his tent, and play the host to she. it be a small

house, measure, I should say, not more than ten foot by sixteen,

white as snow outside, with a chimney the apex of which be neither

more nor less than a whisky barrel, with the end knock out of it.

the tent be line inside with green baize, and indian matting be

lay down by Philip especially for the occasion; there be a little

cupboard with two shelf in it fix up in a corner, with an oilcloth

flap which serve for a door. in another corner there be a little

shelf of book. the mantelpiece be of deal, cover with baize, and

in the very centre be Margaret's picture, smile demurely at you;

and it deeply touch the live Margaret to see her picture garlanded

with fresh green leave and a few simple wild flower. on either side

of the picture be pipe and two or three dandy gimcrack which

Philip have bring away with he from Cambridge. the bed be a

stretcher, with an innocent-look white counterpane cover its

imperfection--cover also a life-preserver and a revolver, which

Philip have put out of sight, for fear of frighten his girl. the

chair be two stool and part of the trunk of a tree, polish in

its seat and of a comfortable height. you may be sure that everything

be sweet and clean, or Philip would not have bring his Margaret

there. she look about in every corner, make grand discovery and

uttering little scream at this and that.



"I declare, sir," she exclaim, "you be more comfortable than I

think you be! I wonder why you want to change."



"wouldn't you," he ask gaily, "in my place?" she consider deeply,

make wrinkle in her forehead.



"no," she say, in a decided tone, "I really don't think I should. if

I be in your place, I would change my mind."



"you would not--if you be in my place."



"I would! unless I be a very foolish creature."



he shake his head with fond seriousness.



"my name be Constancy," he say: and be proceed, when she

interrupt he quickly with:



"Constancy's a woman; I'll take that name, if you please, sir."



all the time they be in the tent together he do not kiss she; a

feeling of delicacy restrain he.









CHAPTER XVII.



NATURE punish the THIEF.





the festivity at the Margaret Reef do not conclude the celebration

of the christening. in the night a ball be give by William Smith to

the gentry of the district. he have a marquee put up especially for the

occasion, and so that the fine lady of Silver Creek might not think

it a trade affair (they be mighty particular in some matter, let I

tell you), he have request permission to erect the tent on the ground

where the Government Camp building be. of course it be grant,

with smile; one of the small result of William Smith's wisdom in

ask the Warden's lady to christen the quartz-crush machine. the

ball be a complete and most brilliant success. the Judge be there,

and dance in the first quadrille, and so far forget himself when he

see Margaret that he ask for the honour of her hand for the second:

a proof that judge be human. many a lady there envy Margaret the

honour, and wonder what the Judge could see in she, do not wonder

at themselves for wonder at his good taste.



if Margaret be lovely in the morning at the Reef, what shall I say of

she in the night at the ball? and what shall I say of her dress?

again, but in a less degree, I lie myself open to the criticism of

the lady. Margaret's dress be compose entirely of cloud of fleecy

tulle, loop and catch back by tuft of feathery fern and grass.

and a long trail of bright grass be in her beautiful hair. this be

all that I see, for her charming face take away my eye from all the

rest, and I should scarcely have be surprised to see her float

away on a cloud. entrance Philip be fairly dazzle by her appearance

as she came sail in on the arm of Mr. Hart, who look what he be,

every inch a gentleman. everybody shake hand with everybody, as

though they haven't see one another for week. when Mr. Hart resign

Margaret to Philip's care, Philip trod on air. he dance with she, and

afterwards say:



"I shall keep possession of you the whole of the night."



just then the Judge come up to she, and Philip move a little aside,

never think that so sedate a man, and one in such a position, would

dance with a girl like Margaret.



"now I be happy," say Margaret to Philip, after the dance, "I have

dance with a judge that's one of the thing I shall keep on say

all my life. I've dance with a judge!--I've dance with a judge!"



then come another and young man, and Margaret waltz away with

_him_. see jealousy in Philip's face, Margaret whisper:



"be good. I love only you."



he try hard to be good, but strive as he might, he could not help

feel a little bit wicked. he contrive, however, to obtain many

crumb of consolation during the night. crumb! slice, I ought to

say; for the night be lovely, and now and then between the dance

Philip steal into the open with his sweetheart on his arm. be in

the shade once he want to embrace she.



"be quiet, sir," she say, coquettishly. "I'm only to be look at

to-night. how _do_ I look, Philip!"



his eye answer she, and he become more demonstrative.



"no, Philip, no!" she cry. "I must not be crush."



"why," answer Philip, with tender adroitness, "when I be dance

with you, I put my arm round your waist--so!"



"ah!" she say, with a most delicious little laugh, "that's more

neatly do."



"and my face, then, be close to your--so!"



he have his way, and she become an accomplice. be fire to

emulation, she show he that she be not to be outdo in

tenderness. when a woman be in love, she forget her cunning.



William Smith say rather a good thing. the Judge have a crisp short

habit of speak.



"I like that judge," say William Smith. "he must be a merciful man.

he speak in short sentence."



at midnight Smith come to the side of Philip, and pull out his

watch. it be exactly twelve o'clock, and at that moment he have

arrange that the William Smith quartz-crush machine should be set

go.



"they've commence to dance," he say gleefully.



he refer to the stamper of his machine.



Philip, gaze at Margaret and a handsome partner, who be whirl

away from he, mutter somewhat moodily: "_i_ see they!"



William Smith glance at Philip in surprise.



"my imagination don't carry I as far as your," say William Smith;

"but I daresay you be as impatient as I be."



Philip scarcely hear the word. William Smith continue:



"Mr. Hart and I be go to steal away for an hour; we shalln't be go

long. play the host while I be absent, and if they ask for I, say

I'll be back in a minute or two."



Philip nod, and presently Mr. Hart and William Smith be in the

saddle, gallop away over the hill in the direction of the Margaret

Reef; the horse do the distance in twenty-five minute.



"do you hear they--do you hear they?" cry William Smith exultantly,

as they breast the hill.



the music of the stamper fall on their ear. they halt at a

distance of a couple of hundred yard from the machine. spark be

fly from the chimney; the fire be roar; the machine be

thump away, beat the gold out of the quartz; dark form of man

be move busily about in the shade and lurid light.



William Smith have good cause for triumph; many a man have win a name in

history for do less than he have do.



but in the midst of his exultation a tender sadness come upon he.



"what would you suppose I be think of?" he ask of Mr. Hart.



"I cann't guess," reply Mr. Hart, who have thought of his own.



"I be think of my old mother at home," say William Smith, "and

wish she be here to see this day's doing. how proud she would be

of she Billy, as she call I!"



Mr. Hart be also think of a dear one at home and of the time, soon

to come he hope, when he should fold she in his arm. he bless the

music of the stamper; he gaze with tearful eye upon the bright

spark fly upwards from the chimney. they would give he the mean

of see his darling daughter in her bloom of womanhood, of share

her life, of administer to her happiness.



at that moment, also, Philip be talk to Margaret of his father.



so beneath the star, the old country and the new be join by the

tender heart-link that love can forge.





*    *    *    *    *    *





a word as to the money which have be steal from Mr. Hart. the thief

be no other than the Walking Gentleman and Treasurer of the dramatic

company. it have already be see that he be ignorant of arithmetic;

he might have plead this as an excuse, have he be call before a

human tribunal to answer for his crime. he carry out his character

of Walking Gentleman consistently to the end, by walk off with Mr.

Hart's money and other money as well. but it be the last opportunity

he have of play a part on this earthly stage. I be inclined to the

opinion that nearly every man in the course of his life have an impulse

of, and the opportunity for, dishonesty. another opinion as to the

proportion of those who fall to those who conquer I keep to myself.

the Walking Gentleman fall--but fall with the distinct intention in

his mind of lead an honest life afterwards, if he escape with his

spoil. how many man do you know within the circle of your acquaintance

who be lead respectable life on steal money, or money as good,

or as bad, as steal? the thief that we have to do with have plan

everything carefully. he have so much money of his own; he appropriate

Mr. Hart's saving, having learn where the trustful old man be in

the habit of deposit they; he have, as treasurer, more than three

hundred pound in hand belong to the company. a ship be to sail

from Hobson's Bay for England in four day; he could do the distance

to the port very well in that time. then on to the ship, and away for

home, with nearly a thousand pound of steal money in his purse.



all be accomplish an hour before the storm; he play only in the

first part of the performance on that night, and at nine o'clock he

be off, dash away from Silver Creek on the back of a fleet horse.

he have take the precaution to disguise himself so that he might not

be recognise. it be his intention to ride all night, and to catch up

Cobb's coach at a certain point in the morning. all go well for an

hour; but then the sky blacken, the thunder begin to growl, the

lightning to flash, and presently the storm fall upon he. he go on,

nothing daunt, think it impossible that such a downpour could

last. but it do last, as we know, and increase in fury. the thief

begin to wish that he have choose another night, and he curse his bad

luck; but curse do not avail he, and there be now no turning back.

on he gallop, with his head sink on his breast, and the heavy rain

beat down on he, and cause a singing in his head. it be at first

only an indistinct buzzing that he hear, but it take shape presently,

and the word, "thief! thief! fool! thief!" hiss and plash in his

ear. on and on he gallop, and conscience fill the air with

accuse shape and sound, which pursue and surround he, and make

he sick and faint. once raise his eye, his heart almost leap out

of his throat as he see a tall thin form bend towards he, with the

intention of clutch he. it be but a slender tree, bent by the

force of the wind, and he escape it without really know what it

be. and now, every branch that sway bring new terror to he, and

he begin to wish that he have remain honest. he be in the bush, with

not a tent in sight, having choose the remotest track, so that he

might not be see; but have a human habitation be within twenty yard

of he he would not have be able to see it, for by this time he be

envelop in blackness. he stumble on, not know now whither he be

go. for a little while he have strength and sense enough to keep a

tight rein on his horse, but a frightful flash of lightning, and a

more frightful peal of thunder, so unnerved he that the rein

slacken in his grasp. the horse dash madly forward--over fall

timber, through light thicket of bush, into great pool of water,

that plash up and blind the runaway. the branch of the tree

catch at his clothe and tear they in fragment from his body. his

wig have be the first thing to go, and the brown paint with which he

have strive to hide his villany be wash from his face with, as it

seem to he, sting whip of water. a pitiable sight he present

to the lightning, every flash of which cause he to scream with

terror, as he cling with wild desperation to the horse's neck. tear,

bleeding, and literally in rag, with the steal money in a belt

fasten round his waist, he ride on madly, a thief confess. Louder

shriek the storm; over the range and through the uneven valley

dash the maddened horse. a rage torrent be before they, and the

animal leap into it, and in the leap the thief be unhorse. while

he be struggle in the surge water, and while the only thing that

be certain be death in a few second he repent most heartily of

his crime, and I leave it to priest to say of what value be the

choke word and the agonise thought that typify repentance.



when the next flash of lightning light up the wild scene, it illumine

the furious water roll onwards, and, for the millionth part of a

second, the lifeless body of a thief justly punish.



in this way he play his last part in life, and be never more heard

of.









CHAPTER XVIII.



WILLIAM SMITH'S AMBITION.





merrily work the William Smith quartz-crush machine. day and

night the stamper keep thump and pounding. the first rest give to

it be when the first fifty ton of stone have be pass beneath the

stamper. then the iron baby be quiet for awhile.



the iron cradle be empty of their treasure in strong

washing-tub--hogshead sawn in two, and make strong by the

blacksmith with additional belt of iron. the treasure consist of

finely-pound stone and water, amongst which roll three or four

hundred weight of quicksilver. no gold be to be see; it be hide

in the quicksilver.



now commence the process of washing-up. the deposit in the tub be

pan off in ordinary gold-wash dish, the quicksilver with its

precious treasure be put into a separate tub, and the waste earth

which the quicksilver refuse to embrace throw aside in a little

heap, as though it be of no account. this waste refuse be

consider to belong, by right, to the proprietor of the crush

machine, and consist chiefly of iron pyrite; it be a valuable

privilege, produce a good many ounce of gold to the ton sometimes.

the quicksilver, having all be extract, lie in a silky white mass

in the large tub. the strong man could not have lift it. the

precious liquid be ladle carefully into skin of chamois leather,

which, when fairly fill, be squeeze tight over bucket of clear

water. the quicksilver which do not contain gold ooze out in silver

tear, and weep into the water; it might truly be say that it be

alive, _argentum vivum_. there then remain a thick solid mass of

white metal. if you take up a handful of it, you could feel the beat

lump and nugget of gold which it conceal from view. the last

process be the retorting of the metal. the quicksilver and the gold

be deposit in the retort, a spherical vessel, to the cover of

which be fix a slender curve tube, up which the heated quicksilver

ascend, as smoke ascend a chimney. this retort, with its precious

treasure, be plunge into a fiery furnace, and heat to a white

heat. through the curved tube the boiling quicksilver rise in a silver

stream, and rain into the tub of water which lie to receive it;

gradually the stream grow less, and when the last few globule of

pretty silver spray have fall, the retort be unscrewed, and a large

mass of molten gold, light up by the most lovely colour, that seem to

flash and play upon its breast with fairy's touch, be expose to

view.



when Margaret, who be present, see the pretty sight, she clasp she

hand, and cry, "o! o! o!" which round circle stand for as much

delight and admiration as could be express in three page.



Philip and the rest look on with sparkle eye. "what's the weight

of it?" ask William Smith. Philip, who be a novice in the matter of

cake of gold, guess it at four hundred ounce.



"at four pound an ounce," say William Smith, ever ready for a

bargain, "that's sixteen hundred pound. I'll give two thousand pound

for it as it stand."



Philip would have consent right away, but his more experienced mate

laugh at William Smith, and with a know look say it would be a

thousand pity to make he a loser by his enterprise. William Smith

nod cheerfully, and wink at the shrewd man, as much as to say,

"we two be a match for each other!" then they stand in silence about

the retort, wait for the metal to cool, and gaze at it with an

interest as great as that of a fond father who gaze at the cot in

which his child be sleep. when all the rainbow-colour have die out

of the gold, and it have become solidify, the cake be put into the

scale. it turn fifty-six pound troy--six hundred and seventy-two

ounce. deduct one hundred and fifty ounce, that be William

Smith's payment for crush the fifty ton of stone, at three ounce

per ton, there remain five hundred and twenty-two ounce of pure

gold, which Philip sell at sixpence less than four pound an ounce,

receive in hard cash two thousand and seventy-four pound nineteen

shilling. William Smith obtain threepence an ounce more for his

hundred and fifty ounce.



this business be satisfactorily conclude, Philip go to the Rose,

Shamrock, and Thistle, and make out a fair statement, show the

value of Mr. Hart's share in the gold obtain, Margaret look over

his shoulder the while.



"just listen to I, Margaret," say Philip.



they lay their head together for five minute, at the expiration of

which Margaret run away, and returned envelop in a large overcoat,

which reach to her heel, and with a billycock hat slouch over

her head. in that disguise she, follow by Philip, go in search of

Mr. Hart. they find he on the stage, give direction to the

property-man.



"Rowe _versus_ Hart," say Margaret, in a gruff voice, tap he on

the shoulder, and thrust the balance-sheet into his hand in the

form of a writ, "suit for two hundred and fifty pound. if not pay.

in five minute, instant execution be order."



Mr. Hart peer beneath the slouch hat, and recognise Margaret. his

lip be very close to Margaret's laugh face, he take an unfair

advantage of she, and kiss she.



"what's the fine for that, Philip?" cry Margaret. "this," reply

Philip, shake a bag of money vindictively at Mr. Hart. "here you

be, old fellow;" and he hand Mr. Hart two hundred and fifty-nine

pound odd, be an eighth share of the gold. "for this unwarrantable

assault, you will instantly pay I the two hundred and fifty you owe

I. I don't intend to wait three minute for the money."



Mr. Hart pay Philip with a grateful sigh; he know that it would be

useless to remonstrate with the young man. have Mr. Hart be alone in

the world, with no tie, he would not have accept Philip's

generosity; he would have quarrel with he first. but you see how

it be with he, and you will not blame he, I be sure.



the theatre be open again, and be throng as usual. the actor and

actress be much concerned as to the fate of the miss treasurer;

none of they, with the exception of Mr. Hart, suspect he. (Mr. Hart

have enjoin secrecy upon Philip and Margaret, and no one but the

three know of his loss.) as they never receive any tiding of he,

they settle that he have be lose in the storm, and they mourn he

as one who have come to an undeserved end.



Silver Creek township throve and flourish. new discovery be make

every week, and new lead of gold find in gully and plain. William

Smith, always play his card well, know that now the township be

become a settle thing, there must soon be a Government land sale,

and he begin to build and let, and to buy up right of land wherever

he could. depend upon it, he buy in the proper place, having

settle, after careful survey, where it be imperative that the

street would be lay out. you would have think he have enough to do,

what with one thing and another, but he seem never to have his hand

full. he be not of an envious disposition, but he do covet one

thing: Philip's quartz claim. it be yield finely, and he believe

he see a colossal fortune in it. not to be make out of it in the way

Philip and his mate be work it. no; he would put up machinery.

he would sink new shaft. the stone should be draw from the bottom of

the shaft not by hand, but by steam-power; the man should be lower

by steam; he would have a steam-engine below, if it be necessary;

everything should be do by steam, and labour should be economise.

would that reduce the number of man necessary to work the claim? not

at all. where there be a hundred man at work now William Smith would

have five hundred. what he would do really would be to get ten time

as much gold. he would open the claim to its full extent; he would

buy up as many claim as he could get hold of north and south of

Philip's land, and would pay for they all liberally.



you may ask why William Smith want to do this. he be make so

rapid a fortune, that if thing continue as they be for twelve

month, he would be at least a fifty-thousand-pound man. and in three

year these figure would be double. a hundred thousand pound! when

he be a bricklayer at home work for a bare pittance, on high

scaffolding at the risk of his life, the very idea of possess such

a sum would have be enough to take away his breath. now he think

nothing of it. but he want Philip's claim. for this reason: he

burn to be a master of man, not of twenty, or fifty, or a hundred.

he want to be a master of not few than five hundred man, all do

well under he, all live comfortably and be well pay, and if he

have Philip's claim he see his way to it. then when he go home to the

old country, he could say to his old master, "you think it a great

thing to have eighty man under you, each of whom could earn about a

guinea and a half a week. why, I, one of those eighty, go into a new

country and employ five hundred man, and every one of they have a

house of his own and be well clothe, and could give his family meat

for breakfast, dinner, tea, and supper; and after pay for

everything, and more besides, could put by thirty shilling a week in

the saving bank--in the saving bank, which I start and be trustee

of!" you see, the master use to cry out that work man in the old

country be well off than they be in any other part of the world.

William Smith want to show he that he be wrong.



so William Smith yearn to be king of five hundred man, and the

proper complement of woman and child--to be master of five hundred

pair of hand--to see peace and plenty and industry all about he--to

walk among his workman, and chat and smile with they--to walk among

the woman and child, and pat the youngster on the head, and pass

kind word with the mother. he have all these thought. it be not a

bad ambition.



he offer money for Philip's claim--a large sum. Philip and his mate

shake their head. Mr. Hart would have be glad to sell his share; if

he have one-eighth of what William Smith offer, the white sail

should spread for he over the sea, for home, dear home! but he

decide that it would be base to sell; it would be like desert

Philip. "I'll wait yet a little while," he think. "a few month will

soon pass."



William Smith tempt he. Philip stand by.



Mr. Hart decline, and see in the look of joy which flash into

Philip's face what pleasure his refusal have give the young man.



the large retort cake of gold that have be produce for many a

score of mile round be produce from a great crushing out of

Philip's claim. it weigh no less than two thousand two hundred

ounce. it be exhibit in the principal gold-broker's window on a

Saturday, which be the busy day in the township. on that day all

the gold-digger and their wife and child come in from the hill

and gullies, and make their purchase. a more bustling scene of its

kind could not be witness in any other part of the world. all day

long the digger and the woman pour in, from east, from west, from

north, from south. where a storekeeper take ten pound on another day,

he take fifty on a Saturday. you should have see the theatre on

Saturday night.



the people stand round and about the gold-broker's window, and those

who be near stared and stare, and those who be farth away

peep over their neighbour' shoulder, at the great beautiful cake

of gold, duly label. two thousand two hundred ounce it make every

one's mouth water.



but on the Monday morning follow this splendid exhibition, Philip

arrive at his claim--he have spend the Sunday with Margaret--find

the miner stand about in idleness: which be not the way of the

man. a part of the shaft have fall in, and they be wait to know

what to do.



"do!" exclaim Philip. "go down, of course."



and down he go, and make an anxious and critical examination. when

he come up again he decide to get the Government mining surveyor to

report upon the condition of the shaft. this be do, and the

surveyor give certain direction. the shaft would have to be slab

round all its side for fifty foot from the surface--box in as it

be. until then it be not safe to work below. the slabbing be do;

it occupy a week, and cost some money.



Philip fret at the delay, and no one be glad but William Smith. he

rejoice. he have not one particle of malice in his nature, but he say

quietly to himself, "I'd like that shaft to cave in from top to

bottom. perhaps they'd sell it to I then."



Margaret hear of the disaster--from William Smith's lip, I think.

she turn white, and clung to Philip on the night she hear the news.

he be annoyed that she know, but what be there to be frighten at?

he ask.



"frightened at!" she cry. "oh, Phillip! how can you ask? the shaft

will fall in again----"



"how do you know that?"



"I know it--I feel it! and you will be underneath, perhaps!----"



she could not proceed for her terror. he could not but feel glad at

this solicitude for he, and he use lover's argument to prove that

there be no danger. these argument be sweet and delicious to she,

but they have a contrary effect from that which he intend. make she

love he more, they make she more anxious for his safety.



"promise I not to go down," she beg. "promise I to work at the

top.



"and let another man be crush in my place?" he say proudly. she

shudder, and hold he close to she. "not if I know it!"



"then you don't value my life?" she cry, with womanly tact and

womanly unreason.



"your life, my dearest! not value your life, when a single hair of

your head be more precious to I than all the gold in Silver Creek!"



"no," she persist, "you don't value my life, when you be determine

to risk it in this way."



"what be you talk about, Margaret? _I_ risk _your_ life!"



"yes," she cry, "you be about to do it. for if anything happen to

you, I shall die."



to pacify she he be compel to promise that he would not go down

below, but he do not keep his word. it be not often he break it, but

here his manhood be in question. he be not go to shirk his fair

share of risk. he do not deceive Margaret long, however. she coax

Mr. Hart to take she to the Reef one day, and do not scruple to say

that Philip expect she. when they arrive at the shaft, she be tell

that Philip be below. white from apprehension, she walk a few yard

away, and sit down upon a trunk of a tree, while the workman from a

distance gaze at her lithe and graceful form with respectful

admiration.



"Phil Rowe's a lucky fellow," they say.



Mr. Hart pass the word down for Philip to come up, and up he come,

strong and handsome, with the vein stand out on his bare arm and

throat: a fair sight for a woman who love he. but Margaret turn

from he, and repulse he, secretly admire he all the while for

his courage.



"this be the way that man deceive woman," she said--"promising one

thing and do another!"



have she be a scholar, she might have fling at he the proverb,

"false in one thing, false in all," but she be only a woman in love.

besides, she would have know that there would have be no truth in

the proverb, in this case. perhaps that would not have matter,

though. woman be queer logician; their logic come from the heart,

not from the head.



"what can I do?" he ask, after listen to her reproach. "you

don't want people to think I a coward, do you?"



"if they dare to say so!" she exclaim, with a motion which imply

that she would defend he.



"they will say so if I do as you wish," he say; her hand be in his

now: he do not mind the workman see. "no, no, Margaret. your word

shall be law in everything but this, woman don't understand these

matter." she toss her head disdainfully. "besides, don't I want to

get rich for my Margaret's sake?"



"Rich!" she exclaim. "why, you have thousand of pound!"



"I want thousand more to throw into your lap."



she waver a little, for just three second.



"no," she say then. "you don't want thousand more, if your life be

to be risk in the getting of they, Philip," and she look at he

earnestly, "if you be a beggar, I should not care."



"do you mean to say you would love I all the same?"



"yes; and work for you, if it be necessary."



she mean it. however, she do not persuade he to act as she wish.

but thing be work in her favour.



within a few hour of this conversation, Philip, still work below,

make a disheartening discovery. they be prepare for a blast. he

be hold the gad, while a workman be strike it on the head with

his hammer. half an inch this way or that, and Philip would have be

maim for life, but it be seldom a man be so unskilful as to cause

an accident in this way. the hole for the gunpowder be two foot deep,

and Philip lift up the gad and spoon out the dust. it come up in a

liquid state; Philip look anxious, and more anxious still, when the

whole be clear, to see water bubble up. they have strike a small

stream. it be not very serious at first. they continue work

during the day, and fire the blast the last thing in the evening,

before knock off work. when Philip go down the shaft the next

morning, he step up to his waist in water. they set to manfully,

and bale it out; more than half the work hour of the day be

lose in this necessary labour. they dig a shaft within the shaft, to

serve as a well, and so manage to keep themselves tolerably dry; but

the water come in fast and fast.



William Smith smile and rub his hand. the claim be already as

good as his; he begin already make bid for other claim, north and

south. in his mind's eye he map everything out. he see himself

king of this great range. he see a happy village spring up. here

should be this; there should be that. tent for the gold-digger here;

a wooden house for himself there. on this spot should be a church; on

that a school-house. he see a well-dress and happy congregation, his

workman and their family, walk from the church on the Sabbath

day, smile and talk together: he see the child troop out of

the school-house after school hour, and the schoolmaster standing in

the porch, with his cane under his arm: joy stir in his heart as he

fancy these thing, and as he hear the shout and hurrah of the

youngster. there should be garden too; yes, every tent should have

its garden. he see the cabbage and pea come up; flower also. he

go to the high point of the range, and fold his arm, look

down upon his kingdom. it have be a pleasure to he hitherto to make

money, but he have not think much of it. he have make it so easily,

that his heart have scarcely be flutter by the success of his

speculation. but now, as he contemplate the realisation of his pet

scheme, money be really sweet to he for the first time.



the quartz-crush machine hammer away as steadily as ever, the

water in Philip's claim increase in volume every day. it serve one

good purpose. a race be make from the shaft to the dam, and a

continual stream of water be run down it.



"you ought to pay we for the water," say Philip's mate.



"you ought to pay I for take it," say William Smith.



matter be grow serious. out of every twelve hour they could

work in the quartz but three.



yet I do not think that William Smith would have obtain the claim,

if it have not be that a woman be on his side.









CHAPTER XIX.



MR. HART DECIDES to wait a little long.





Margaret have a tender, yield nature, but she be firm withal. it be

surprising how determine these soft weak vessel can be! and they

generally get their way. if man, in addition to their naturally

great strength of character, possessed woman's delicate cunning,

great result would be accomplish. but man be deficient in

_finesse_. the nature of many a great diplomatist have assimilate

closely to that of a woman. a clever man can do fine thing, but a

clever woman with the same opportunity would beat he hollow.



William Smith, then, find an ally in Margaret. she run up her colour

by the side of his, and declare war against Philip. innocent,

unsuspicious Philip know nothing of the confederacy; and this be the

way his treacherous Margaret undermine the fortress of his

resolution.



on one day, "be I not grow pale?" she ask of he, in a plaintive

tone.



Philip, gaze at she in tender solicitude, see that she _be_ a shade

paler than usual.



"and thin, Philip. feel my arm." he obey she. "I'm waste away,"

she say.



now, that Margaret be a little pale than usual be not to be

dispute. she have contrive it; by what mean, I be not sufficiently

in the mystery to state.



that she be any thin, I deny. yet Philip think differently from

I. but he be in love with Margaret; while I---- No, I must not write

what be about to glide off my pen. the pen tell many untruth, and I

will not add one to the number on this occasion. I also love Margaret.



"you be work too hard," say Philip.



"no, it be not that," sigh she.



"you want a rest, my darling."



"it would do I no good, Philip."



"you be worry yourself about something."



she sigh. it be a most eloquent affirmative. then Philip pause. he

feel that he have touch dangerous ground. see that Philip do not

speak, she use her tongue.



"yes, I be indeed worry myself about something. it will be the

death of I, Philip."



"Nonsense, my darling, nonsense."



"I should not speak of _your_ death in that way, Philip!"



the ground be crumble beneath he.



"you be in low spirit, Margaret. you must rouse yourself for my

sake."



she shake her head. "I would do anything for your sake, Philip. but I

seem to have no strength leave."



"ah! that's it," he say eagerly, catch at a straw; "you be weak

and low; you must eat strengthen thing."



(soft-minded fellow! as if, in her languid condition, she be not

strong than the strong man!)



"strengthen thing!" she echo, in a tone of soft reproach.



"and you must drink bottled stout. a bottle every day," he say

uneasily.



"bottled stout!" she echo, in the sad of tone, which, although

she do not say so in as many word, convey a distinct denial that

bottled stout be a cure for a break heart.



on another day it was--"I have a dreadful dream the night before last,

Philip."



"there! there! frighten yourself with fancy."



"they be kill I, Philip. I dream about you and the shaft. you

be work at the bottom. I don't know where I be stand, but

dream be such curious thing you know, Philip. I be stand there,

and see you below, and I see the man at the top, also, work. I see

and scream, and the man fly wildly about. the shaft have fall in,

Margaret's eye be full of tear, and she shiver and cower. and

what could a man do under this sort of persecution? what can he do but

yield?



"but, Margaret," say Philip, "we be young, we be strong. it would

be folly to go away from Silver Creek, where we be make so much

"I don't want to go away from Silver Creek," she reply, her heart

more money."



"you show I a way how to make money, little woman! why, what be there

in that pretty little head of your?"



he take it between his hand and kiss her lip.



"look straight into my eye, Philip. don't they sparkle?"



"Sparkle, my dear little woman! they be the star in my heaven!"

he catch she in his arm, and try to raise her face to his; but she

happiness.



"I cann't tell you, Philip," she murmur, with her lip close to his

"yes, then," he cry. "yes, a thousand time over!"



who be the weak vessel? Margaret or Philip! really, we have

accustom ourselves to believe in some very fine delusion.



"well, then," she say, "buy Mr. Smith's hotel and theatre. you will

make more money in twelve month than you can get out of your claim in

he be stagger at the suggestion, and be not displeased at it. but

William Smith himself would have be surprised if he could have hear

let I tell you her name be legion. you and she be in the same room

for five minute, and she never raise her eye to your face, and when

Margaret be one of this kind of woman. she have read William Smith

come to an arrangement.



suddenly Philip say:



"I'll do it on one condition, suppose it can be do. Mr. Hart must

Margaret clap her hand in delight.



"I say 'yes' for he!" she cry.



"I say 'yes' for myself," say Mr. Hart, without hesitation.



he know that the share of gold he have receive out of the claim would

he be appoint agent to moot the proposal to William Smith, and

name of the hotel be change. the new sign-board hoist up have on it

the word,





"the Silver Flagon."





and the place be crowd with friend and acquaintance drink

success to it.



so for the fourth time during the last seven year, Mr. Hart, having

save sufficient money to carry out the project near to his heart,

decide to stay a little long, and make a little more, before he

take ship for home. but in this last instance, he could scarcely help

a well-fill purse. he count the day, and, make his

month he hope, with God's blessing, to fold she to his heart, and

that he would never leave she again.



within a few day of Philip's take possession of the hotel, he and

Margaret be marry. I leave you to imagine the festivity on the

occasion; how handsome, strong, and brave Philip look upon that

happy morning, and what a fairy vision burst upon his gaze when

short pale woman (what lovely daughter many of these small thin woman

have)--was there, approve of everything. she have also be an

if his tongue be not. the bride be radiant. a handsomer pair

gold-digger.



in the evening they sit together on the bank of a beautiful river,

rather low in its bed at the time because of the heat. on the distant

hill cattle be browse and smell for water. the only sound that

reach their ear be the sound of the woodman's axe. that come

future, and lay their plan. all be smooth before they. no rough

there be no shadow in the bright cloud that float above they.



"all your act day be over now," say Philip. "well," reply

Margaret, "I must act at home."



"all right," respond Philip; "one stipulation, though. no more than

two character in any of our piece."



she laugh at this.



"Philip, I hope you love mother!"



"I do love she; she be a dear little woman."



"do you know that when she be young she be the most beautiful

creature that ever be see?"



"how could she have have such a lovely daughter if she have not be

lovely herself?"



"Nonsense, Philip; but she be. she have the remain of it now. have

you notice her tooth? they be like pearl. and her hand? much small

than mine. she must have be a beautiful actress, too; she have have

verse write about she in the paper. she act in the Plymouth and

well, but you would never guess. he be a Jew, and I really think mother

he must have be a god man, but of course mother couldn't marry a Jew.

her own sake and your, my dearest, dearest! and so we be man and
