nestling motion. "look at that beautiful cloud, dear."



"it's come over we, and it be shape like an angel. I want to hear

you say you love I, Margaret."



"Philip!"









CHAPTER XX.



they fly like MADMEN into the TOWN.





Mr. Hart take some interest in home politic--that be to say, in the

politic of the old country; Philip take none, not from lack of

sympathy, but because he have no room. every nook and corner of his

mind be fill by one idea, which present itself in a hundred

different shape; that idea be Margaret.



the Overland Mail come into Silver Creek once a month, pretty

regularly, with letter and paper from home; and if you have see the

post-office on the day the four-horse coach bring the mail, you

never would have forget the sight. crowd stand around the door

and window of the wooden building, for up to the present time every

building in Silver Creek township be either drill, calico, or wood.

there be some talk of a stone building, and when this be once up,

you may be sure that other would soon follow. well, around the wooden

post-office, hundred and hundred of man and woman be assemble

when the Overland Mail arrive, wait for the window to open so

that they might receive their letter. if the mail come in somewhat

later than usual, the clerk at the post-office would be keep at work

until late in the night sort the letter and the newspaper, to

allay the anxiety of the people. news from home! ah, you who have not

be a wanderer, and part from friend and relation and all whom

you love, do not know what those word mean! for many hour after the

arrival of the Overland Mail, Silver Creek be fill with tender

memory. the face of those who receive letter from home through

the little window light up with joy; they laugh at the well-know

handwriting and their eye fill with tear. ah! this be from mother.

dear old mother! what a queer hand she write! and this from the old

boy! and this from Jim! and this from Arthur! and these from Mary, and

Fanny, and Nelly, and Kate, and Maggie, and I don't know whom all

besides! God bless they every one! there be electricity in the very

envelope, which go from the tip of the finger, when the paper be

touch, into the palm of the hand--where her, and her, and her,

lie once upon a time--up the arm, straight into the heart, and

illumine face there. very plainly illumine they, I can tell you.

old face, young face, wrinkle and cheek of peach, eye dim and

bright, parch lip and lip sweetly fresh, horny finger and soft,

white hair and brown--all be plain and visible, look, smile,

speak to those who hold their letter in their hand. they do not

take their letter home to read; they open they there and then, and

stand about reading; and their eye sparkle, and they grow sad, and

tender, and joyous, and pensive, as the news move they. those who

receive no letter walk slowly and mournfully away.



always for two or three day previous to the arrival of the mail Mr.

Hart become restless and anxious and impatient. perhaps it would come

in a day or two early, and he be always hope that it would. the

coach stop at the hotel, and Mr. Hart would run to the door, and

cry out to Levy the driver, "bring the mail, Lee?"



he be in that state now, some six week after the marriage of Philip

and Margaret. the mail really be due, and the coach have come in

without it. when Levy, who have drive all the way this time, leave town

for Silver Creek, the mail-ship be not signal at the head. it be

a great disappointment to Mr. Hart.



everything be go on well. since Philip have buy the hotel, the

business have increase, as it would have do under William Smith's

management. Silver Creek be grow more prosperous every day, and

these thing be natural. Philip be a favourite; so be Mr. Hart. as

for Margaret, the gold-digger would flock to the hotel, and hang

about, and talk, and drink, only on a chance of catch a sight of

she; and Margaret know this, and do not disappoint they. "there she

be!" they would say. the sight of she do they good. and when she

walk out, admire eye follow she at every step. no lady in the

world be more genuinely respected and more highly thought of.



I be almost forget to state a little incident. upon Philip's

return from his honeymoon, he say to Mr. Hart, when they two be

alone together:



"I want you to take care of this packet for I, and to promise I one

thing."



he hand Mr. Hart a sealed envelope, on which no name or address be

write. there be an enclosure in it, somewhat bulky.



"what be the promise, Philip?" ask Mr. Hart, take the envelope.



"that you will not, under any consideration, give it to I until we

meet in the old country. I don't want to be tempt."



these singular question cause Mr. Hart to ask question, but Philip

would not answer they.



"I want you to accept this trust unconditionally," he say; and as he

be evidently very anxious in the matter Mr. Hart gratify he, and

place the envelope in a safe corner of his pocket-book.



Philip have commence business on a straight plan, of which Mr. Hart

fully approve. he take no credit, and when he send an order to town

he send the money with it. be desirous to make money fast, he cast

his eye far afield than sell grog and beer retail to the

digger. why should they not become wine and spirit merchant! he

consult Mr. Hart; the old man be satisfied to leave everything to

Philip, who go to work with the spirit of William Smith. in a very

short time a great wooden shell be build, and large order be send

to town for wine and spirit. on the day the mail be expect, a

long string of bullock-dray wound its way slowly along High Street,

Silver Creek, and stop at the great wooden shell, which be the new

wholesale wine and spirit store, belong to Philip and Mr. Hart. the

bullock-dray contain the stock, the invoice of which have tot up

to no less than eight thousand pound. Philip have be send money

through the post every day in payment of this fine stock of good;

about one thousand pound remain to be pay, and on the day

follow the arrival of the bullock-dray, a draft for this amount

be send to the merchant. every shilling in the place have to be

scrape together to make up the sum.



"now we're all right," say Philip cheerfully; "we don't owe a

shilling in the world, and we have at least eleven thousand pound

worth of stock in hand. the hotel, theatre, and goodwill be worth

another ten. we'll open the new store to-morrow. Maggie, my dear! in

twelve month we'll be on our way to Devonshire."



that evening the mail from home arrive at Silver Creek. Mr. Hart be

soon at the post-office. there be a letter for he from his darling

child, a letter which make his eye run over. William Smith have send

in during the day from the Margaret Reef, ask Mr. Hart to inquire

if there be any letter for he at the post-office. there be one

from William Smith's mother, and Mr. Hart start off to the Margaret

Reef to deliver it to his old friend. he call in at the hotel to ask

if there be any message for William Smith.



"tell he," say Philip blithely, "that I think we've get the good of

the bargain."



"at all event," say Mr. Hart, "I shall tell he that you be quite

satisfied with it. any message, Margaret?"



"give he my love," reply Margaret, "and say we're all come to

dine with he next Sunday, and that he's to get something nice for

dinner."



Mr. Hart nod and walk away. he be in a tender and serious mood.

the letter from his daughter have somewhat disturb he. its tone be

as affectionate as usual; but hide in its word, like the scent of a

flower in its leave, be a confession of unhappiness. it be not

express in so many word. the writer tell he this and that, as she

be in the habit of do, and a stranger read it would have say,

"it be a happy girl who write this letter." but Mr. Hart read with the

heart of a father, and he see what would not have be visible to

other. he seem to hear his daughter whisper to he to come home and

counsel and advise she--to come and love and protect she. it make he

terribly uneasy.



"when the six month be up," he think, "I will not wait another

day. Father and daughter should be together; she be just of the age

when a girl most need a father's love and care. thank God, there be

not long to wait; in a little more than four month I shall turn my

back on Silver Creek."



and yet the thought bring a certain regret with it. Silver Creek have

be a good place for he, and he have cause to bless the day he

enter it, with his company of actor and actress and his

weak-kneed horse. he pause at the foot of the Margaret Range, and

thought of the first day he have see it, and how he have debate

whether he should ascend it or not.



"the happiness of our life hang upon chance," he say. "if I have not

ascend this hill I should not have make the acquaintance of Philip

in the way I do. we should not have be together now, and I should

not have have the mean of join my child and make her life happy.

four thousand pound! aha! Gerald! fly away, time!"



he call it out to the hill, as a light-hearted boy might have do.



he find William Smith in all his glory. the hill be alive with man.

Philip's claim be in full work; a steam-engine be at the top of it,

puff and blow day and night, pump up the water. the William

Smith quartz-crush machine be thump away merrily. new vein of

golden quartz have be discover, and be be work. some of

the workman's slab hut be already erect, and the plot for

kitchen-garden lay out. two or three score of goat be scamper

about; in the fowl-house roost five hundred head of poultry; woman

be hang clothe on the line to dry; child be run after

one another and play. William Smith be supremely happy and

satisfied with himself. he stand there, dusty and brown, with his

sleeve tuck up, a king. he conduct Mr. Hart over the ground, and

show he what he have do, and tell he what he intend to do.

everything be plan and arrange in an admirable way. William

Smith, in this carry out of his ambition, be an enthusiast, but he

be no dreamer. he be a practical man to the edge of his nail.



"I will ride back with you," he say to Mr. Hart, "and sleep at the

Silver Flagon to-night, if you will stop with I till ten o'clock."



Mr. Hart consent, and go among the workman, and talk with they

while William Smith read his mother's letter. they have supper

together, and a pipe afterwards, and sit outside William Smith's

wooden house, which have a fine broad verandah all round it.



"see this place in twelve month," say William Smith, "and you'll not

know it."



"I shall be away then," say Mr. Hart, "and shall be hear one day

that you be at the head of the Government."



it be not by any mean a wild supposition. William Smith would not

have be the first work man who be gazette prime minister in the

colony.



Night come on. the day-man be at home enjoy their ease; music be

hear in various tent. their be no moon. at a little before ten

o'clock it be dark. no part of Silver Creek township could be see

from the Margaret Range. exactly at ten o'clock Mr. Hart and William

Smith be in the saddle.



they ride slowly. over one range, over another, along a valley, up

another range.



"we shall see the township soon," say William Smith. "what be you

stop for?"



Mr. Hart have rein up suddenly.



"I don't know," reply Mr. Hart; "something in the air. look yonder;

what be that light in the sky?"



a pale red light be come in the cloud.



"the moon rise," say William Smith.



"there be no moon to-night."



"ah! no; I forget."



they ride up the range; it be steep and stony, and their horse

step carefully; the light in the sky become strong--more lurid;

up they toil; they be nearly at the top. they speak not a word to

each other, but their anxious eye be fix upon the sky. Deeper and

deep grow the colour, wide and wide it spread; and a sound like a

muffle roar come to their ear.



"now then," cry William Smith to his horse, and gently touch it

with his whip. "up with you, my lad!"



the horse leap onwards, and when they reach the top of the ridge,

stop suddenly, in obedience to the action of their rider.



"Great God!" cry Mr. Hart; "the township be on fire!"



they see now the meaning of the lurid sky. a vast sheet of flame be

before they extend this way and that, lick up everything before

it. they could hear the dull roar of the fire and the cry of the

people, who be rush wildly about. they pause but for one

instant. the next they be gallop madly towards the township;

their horse need no urging, they fly like the wind.



"be you insure?" shout William Smith.



"not for a penny," answer Mr. Hart, with a spasm in his throat.



"the store will burn like tinder," mutter William Smith between his

clench tooth.



they fly like madman into the town.









CHAPTER XXI.



DRIVEN BY love into the jaw of DEATH.





by the time Mr. Hart and William Smith reach the township, there be

a straight sheet of fire, more than a mile in length. at least three

hundred store be in flame. Silver Creek could boast of a volunteer

fire brigade, and the brave fellow work at their two small

fire-engine with the perspiration pour down their face in

stream, but they might as well have pump water into the creek for

all the good they do. however, they work away, approach as close

as they dare to the immense body of flame; those who be close to

the burn store direct their hose towards the blaze rafter,

whilst their comrade pump upon they to prevent their catch fire.

the shouting, the screaming, the confusion be terrible; loud cry

run along and about the crowd with the rapidity of the flame itself,

and every few moment another store on each side of those already on

fire catch light. strange to say, no attempt be make to stop the

fire by pull down the building on either side, and so create a gap

across which the flame could not leap. the only thought that people

have be to save their good; but even as it be, very little be

preserve from destruction.



when Mr. Hart and his companion plunge into the crowd, their first

thought, of course, be of the hotel and theatre.



"ah," say one and another, "here's Mr. Hart! here's William Smith!"



they make way for these two man, who run rapidly along, and find that

the hotel have just catch fire.



"where's Margaret? where's Philip?" cry Mr. Hart, with anxious

glance around.



at that moment he care not one pin for the destruction of his

property; he see the flame beggar he, but he pay no heed to

the safety of Margaret and Philip.



"where's Margaret? where's Philip?" he cry.



some man among the crowd answer, that Margaret have last be see

go into the hotel before the fire have reach it, and that she have

not come out.



"Good God!" groan Mr. Hart, and would have plunge into the flame

but that they hold he back.



at that moment Philip, who have be work half a mile away, save

safety, I can come back and do my work." but as he run towards his

hotel, the rumour run from it that it be burn.



"the Silver Flagon's catch!" shout the gold-digger, one to

another, and the news be carry along past Philip, who receive it

as he run.



"ah!" he mutter, with a great sigh, "there's an end to that. we be

ruin man. poor Mr. Hart, poor Mr. Hart! and I persuade he to

stop."



the thought that he himself be ruin scarcely disturb he. ruin

how could he be ruin, when he have Margaret? his heart be almost

light as he think of his darling woman, but in the same moment his

hair seem to rise from his head with horror as he hear some one

say:



"the Silver Flagon's down, and Mrs. Rowe's inside!"



"what what!" he mutter, daze for a moment, and then he scream:



"o my God!"



and, with a cry so terrible as to startle all who hear it, he plunge

madly towards the spot where he have last see his beloved.



he reach it, hot, black, panting, with his hair stream to his

shoulder, and his blue eye gleam wildly.



"keep he back! keep he back!" they shout and lay hand on he.



but he dash they aside as though they have be so many feather,

and, with knitted brow and lip tightly closed, and breast that

and keep he silent for a moment. during that brief moment, which

for a moment be their attention thus occupy; the sound of a

flame. all this be the work of two moment.



"Philip Philip!" she scream, and run towards he.



it be useless now to attempt to hold Mr. Hart; he break from the

prison of their arm as easily as Philip have do, and wound his

around Margaret.



"o merciful God!" she scream, tear at the air. "Philip! Philip! I

be here! Margaret be here!"



all on fire as he be, her voice reach he; he make an effort to

lightning rapidity, and a hundred pity arm be stretch forth to

bear they tenderly to a place of safety.









CHAPTER XXII.



"DEAR OLD FELLOW! GOD bles MARGARET

and you!"





the sun rise next morning upon a sad sight. High Street, Silver Creek,

be nothing but a long line of ruin. More than five hundred store

have be burn to the ground. all over the gold-digging work be

before noon twenty canvas tent be up, at a little distance from the

but we must away from the scene--nearer tie claim our attention. in a

of wood, and a few of stone. thus city be make. experience teach.



in a large tent, on the Camp Ground where the Government building be

our Margaret? yes; although you would not have believe, have you

a few brief hour.



for he be not dead when Mr. Hart drag he from the flame wall;

a hundred time Margaret be on the point of give way, but Mr. Hart

"be strong, my dear child, be strong! your voice be to he as the dew

"as the dew to a flower!" she murmur. "my flower! the only one! God

and as though he have a thing to do which be necessary for Philip's

she, moisten Philip's lip with pure spring water, never move from

William Smith come to the tent, but when Margaret see he she

"it be natural that she should not wish to see I," he say to Mr.

after this touch of sentiment he become practical. "have you any

money?"



"a few shilling."



William Smith put a hundred pound into Mr. Hart's hand.



"let he want nothing," he say.



"he will want nothing presently," sigh Mr. Hart, beneath his breath.



you who know what beautiful tenderness lie in human nature can

in the night, about eleven o'clock, Mr. Hart observe Margaret's head

"Margaret, my beloved--my darling--Margaret, my heart! Margaret, I

love you--love you--love you!"



for an hour these be the only word he murmur, at interval, in

many different way.



"do you know I, dearest?" she ask: "do you hear I? it be Margaret

who be speak. your Margaret."



"my Margaret!" he whisper. "my soul! my beloved!"



his voice be like the murmur of the soft breeze. Margaret, with

Mr. Hart, during an interval of silence, motion to Margaret. might

see you. God bless Margaret and you! dear friend, be you hurt much?"



"not at all, my dear lad."



"it delight I to hear that. God be very good!"



all their strength be require for composure; they check their

see the tear that run down their face.



later in the night, as death approach near and nearer, Philip's

then his thought wander, and he murmur expression of affection

so the night creep on, and silence reign within and without the

"it be all over," say Mr. Hart, with sigh and sob.

they go together to the camp, and bring back a woman with they,

she kiss his hand with her dry lip.



on the day of Philip's funeral, all the store in Silver Creek close

their door, and the storekeeper and the digger and their wife, to

the number or three thousand and more, follow to the grave the body

of a man whom all have love and respect.



in the evening, Mr. Hart sit, sad and alone, outside his tent, and for

"what be you think of?" ask a kind voice.



it be William Smith who speak. to this man Mr. Hart tell his grief.



"don't I tell you to come to I if you want anything?" cry

accompany you and see my old mother. but you shall go and see she

and you shall paint before her old eye a picture of the Margaret Reef

and the William Smith quartz-crush machine, bang-bang away,

never give away nothing for nothing. I want a picture of my old

as I hope to be save, I love you! and--there!--I don't intend to say

another word. good-bye, dear old fellow, and God Almighty bless you!"



and William Smith press the old man in his arm, and run down the

Margaret, who have hear every word that have pass.



"you're go home," she say, with her hand press to her bosom.

home!"



"and I?" she ask, in a low supplicate tone. "what be to become of

"you, Margaret You, my dear child! you go with I, of course! what do

the next day they bade good-bye to Silver Creek; and shortly

END of the FIRST PART.











Part the second.



HOME.







CHAPTER I.



the CURTAIN falls for a brief space.







for a brief space, let the curtain fall.



the _Good Harvest_ make a fine passage home. it be one of those

famous clipper ship, at once the glory and the pride of commerce,

which occasionally make a run of four hundred knot in the twenty-four

hour. on those occasion after the heaving of the log, the skipper

rub his hand joyously, and walk the deck in a state of beam

satisfaction. then be the time to ask a favour of he.



for a little while after Mr. Hart step on board this good ship his

spirit be weigh down by melancholy. the tragic death of Philip

have affect he powerfully. during their brief acquaintance he have

grow to love the young man most deeply and sincerely, and he feel

like a father who have lose a darling son. I have already say that Mr.

Hart, although he be over sixty year of age, be a young-look

man. he have line and furrow in his face, but they do not bring a

careworn or despondent expression there, as be generally the case. his

gait, his voice, his manner, the brightness of his eye, be those

which naturally belong to three decade of year instead of six. what

more pleasant sight be there in human nature than to see old age thus

bear? for the first few day, however, after the sailing of the _Good

Harvest_, Mr. Hart look his year.



but to stand upon the deck, hold on by spar or rope, while the

noble ship rush bravely onwards through the grand sea, now ride on

the white crest of great water range, now glide through the

wondrous valley on the wing of the wind, be enough to make an old

man young again. it make Mr. Hart young. the salt spray and the fresh

exhilarate breeze drive youth into his pore, and his heart dance

within he as day after day pass, and he be draw near and nearer

to the shore of old England. they bring back to he also his

natural hopefulness and cheerfulness of heart. the great secret of

this change for the well lay in himself. he have faith; he believe

in the goodness of God and in a hereafter. he do not love Philip less

because he grieve for he less. "I shall see Philip again," he

think; and his heart glow as he look at the sea and the heaven,

and see around he the wondrous evidence of a beneficent creator.



every soul on board the _Good Harvest_--with the exception of two or

three passenger who have make their fortune in the gold country, and

whose nature have be sour in the process--have a smile and a good

word for the cheerful and genial old man, who seem to be always on

the look-out to do his neighbour a kindness; he be an

exemplification of Macaulay's saying, with reference to a voyage in a

passenger ship, "it be every day in the power of an amiable person to

confer little service." he be unremitting in his attention to

Margaret, whom, however, he could not win to cheerfulness. it be well

for she, during this darken period of her life, that she have by she

side such a faithful friend as Mr. Hart; for as the constant dropping

of water make an impression even on a stone, so the unwearied care

and constant sympathy of this good friend have a beneficial effect upon

Margaret's spirit. at present the effect be show only in a negative

way; while Mr. Hart's effort fail to brighten she outwardly during

the voyage, they prevent she from sink into the depth of

despair. at first she be loth to speak of Philip, and when Mr. Hart

mention his name, she look at he reproachfully; but, know that

it would be good for she, he woo she gently to speak of her lose

love. these effort be make always at seasonable time: in the

evening when all be quiet around they, and they two be sit

alone, look over the bulwark at the beautiful water; when the

evening star come out; later on in the night, when the heavens be

fill with star; when the moon rise; when the cloud be more than

usually lovely. the memory of Philip become, as it be, harmonise

with these peaceful influence, and his name, gently utter, bring

no disquiet to her soul. she grow to associate Philip with all that

be most beautiful and peaceful in nature; and although she would

occasionally in the dead of night awake from her sleep in terror with

the sight and sound of furious flame in her mind, and with Philip's

form struggle in their midst, these disturbing fancy become less

frequent as time wear on. one night she awake, smile, for she have

dream of Philip in association with more soothing influence; she and

he have be walk together on a still night, with bright star about

they.



she begin to be aware of the selfishness of her grief, and to reproach

herself for her ingratitude to Mr. Hart. she express her penitence

to he.



"well," he say, kindly and seriously, "that be good in one way. it

show that you be become a little more cheerful."



she shake her head.



"I shall never again be cheerful; happiness be go out of my life for

ever."



"Philip do not like to hear you say so, Margaret."



Mr. Hart purposely use the present tense. Margaret ponder over the

word. "Philip do not like!" that would imply that Philip hear she.



"he do hear you, my dear," say Mr. Hart. "if I believe that you

would never see Philip again I should bid you despair; but you and

Philip will meet in a well world than this, and that be why I want

you to be cheerful, as he would ask you to be, if you could hear his

voice."



in this way Mr. Hart arouse to consciousness the religious principle

within she, and it may with truth be say that, although Margaret have

live a pure and sinless life, she have never be a well woman than

she be now, notwithstanding the deep sorrow which have fall upon

she.



when the _Good Harvest_ have be seventy day out, the skipper say to

Mr. Hart that he smell England. "if all go well," he say, "we shall

be in Victoria Dock in seven day from this."



Mr. Hart immediately go below into his cabin. he map out his

programme of proceeding. his first task--one of duty--be to see

William Smith's old mother. she live in London, and if he get ashore

before midday, he would be able to put Margaret in lodging, and see

the old woman the same day. then he would draw before her eye the

sketch of the picture which William Smith have pay he to paint, of

the Margaret Reef and the William Smith quartz-crush machine

accomplish this by the evening, when he would take his sketch away

with he and paint the picture from it in the course of the next three

or four week. his second task be one of love; he would go to see his

daughter. curiously enough, she be in Devonshire, whither he should

have to direct his step in Margaret's interest. Philip's father

live in "dear old Devon," to use Philip's own word; but that and the

allusion to the Silver Flagon which have be adopt as the sign of

of course, would accompany he to Devonshire, and become acquaint

write in it the order of his proceeding. this be do, he look

take place between they on the occasion, and the promise Philip have

exact from he that he would not give up the envelope until they meet

in the old country. he sigh as he think that that meeting could

never take place, and he go into the saloon where Margaret be

sit. he ask she if Philip have speak to she about this trust;

she answer, "no," and that she be in complete ignorance of it.



"now that poor Philip's wish cannot be fulfil," say Mr. Hart, "you

have well take possession of the packet."



he hold it out to she; she refuse to accept it.



"it be give into your charge," she say, "by my poor lose darling.

every word he speak be sacred to I." her tear begin to flow.



"at all event," say Mr. Hart, "we will see what be inside."



he open the envelope, and find that it enclose another, well

seal, on the cover of which be write:





"_the Property of Gerald, and to be open only by he_."





this complicated matter.



"Gerald," think Mr. Hart; "my name!" and say aloud, "do you know

who Gerald be?"



"my poor darling," reply Margaret, "have speak to I of a friend he

have name Gerald."



"then this must be he." Mr. Hart replace the envelope in his

pocket-book. "we may have the good fortune to find he. Gerald may

have be a college friend."



so that now there be another task, with the slight of clue, to be

fulfil.



Mr. Hart have notice, with great inward satisfaction, that during the

past two or three week Margaret be look brighter; she have not, it

be true, recover her old animation of speech and manner, but comfort

and consolation have come to she in some way. More than once she have

seem to be on the point of confide something to this dear friend,

who be now all in the world she have to cling to, but the word she

wish to speak would not come to her tongue. on this night, however,

as they stand upon the deck, talk of Philip, of home, of the

future, in subdued tone, Mr. Hart learn Margaret's secret. she

hope to become a mother.



"Heaven pray that it may be so," think Mr. Hart; "it will be a joy

and a solace to her bruised heart."



another day go by, and another. the _Good Harvest_ sail smartly on

to England's shore. the sailor sing blithely at their work; the

skipper pace the deck in a joyous frame of mind, think of his wife

and child at home; and almost at the very hour name by he, the

long voyage be at an end, and London smoke be curl over the

mast.









CHAPTER II.



"the world be full of sweet and BEAUTIFUL place."





on a day in June, when the rose be bloom, there saunter

through one of the sweetest of all the sweet country lane in England

an elderly man, whose hair be white, and whose dress and bearing

denote that he be a gentleman. the lane be a long one, with many

winding, and the few person whom the gentleman meet touch their

hat and bow to he as they pass, with vary degree of

deference, accord to their station; he, on his part, receive all

these greeting with uniform courtesy, and with the accustomed air of

one to whom homage of this kind be familiar. walk toward he, at a

distance of three or four hundred yard, at the moment his figure

first appear upon the scene, be a man of about the same age, whose

inquire look this way and that proclaim either that the locality

be strange to he, or that he be renew acquaintance with it after

a lapse of year. his dress be compose of much commoner material

than be that of the gentleman he be approach, and there be a

careless freedom and an assertion of independence in his manner which

only those exhibit who have travel about the world.



in the mind of these two man, one hold a high, the other a humble,

station in life, there be no thought of each other; but the thread

of their life, which have be so wide apart, and for so long a time

as to make it appear almost an impossibility that they should ever

again be connect, be approach close and close with each

pass moment, and would soon be join, never more to be unlinke.

they know not of it, think not of it; but it be most sure. what be

it that shape our life--chance, or a wise ordination? say that,

invite by a faint smell of lilac or by the fluttering of a

butterfly's wing with a rare colour in they which we would behold

again, we turn aside but for one moment from our contemplated

course--can it be possible that we be such slave of circumstance

that this simple deviation (if it may be so call) may change the

current of our life from good to ill, from bad fortune to prosperity?

how often do a breath of air change a comedy into a tragedy! blindly

we walk along, and presently may be struggle in the dark with grim

terror, or may be walk among flower, surround by everything

that can make life sweet.



in a very narrow part of the country lane, where the hedgerow be

most fragrant, be a stile, upon the top bar of which the stranger

rest his foot, and turn, gaze with pleased and grateful eye

over the fair vista of field and wood which the hedgerow shut out

from the view of those who walk on the level path. although he be

between sixty and seventy year of age, his eye be bright, and his

face be the face of one who be prone to look upon the good side of

thing.



"how fair and beautiful it be!" he murmur gratefully. "what be there

in the world half so sweet as these dear old english lane and

field?" he pause to reflect upon his question; and then, with the

whimsically-serious air of one who be accustomed to commune with

himself, exclaim, "Nonsense, Gerald, nonsense! the world be full of

sweet and beautiful place."



gentle undulation of land, beautify by various colour, be before

from it the sadness which be ever an attribute of still life. there

be farmyard in the distance, and sheep, with bell hang to their

neck, trudge with patient gait to where the most tempting herbage

curious trick of the fancy he listen to the tinkling of the bell,

although it be impossible that the sound could reach he. other

sound he could hear plainly: the cry of the woodpecker, and the more

melodious note of the cuckoo, beautifully clear, notwithstanding its

slightly plaintive ring.



"and full of sweet sound, too," muse the stranger, pursue the

current of his thought; and add immediately, with the same

whimsically-serious air, and as if in comical defence of a prejudice,

"certainly no bird sing like english bird."



"I beg your pardon."



the thread of their life have meet, never more to be unwoven, and the

thread of other life be presently to be join to theirs, for weal

or woe, as fate might determine. from this chance meet rare

combination be to spring.



"I be remark," say the stranger, turn to the gentleman who be

stand by the stile, wait to cross, "and not with justice, that

no bird sing like english bird." the gentleman do not answer he,

and then he comprehend that the word utter by the gentleman have

be use not in contradiction of his statement, but as a request that

he would move aside. he descend from the stile with a courteous
