the road-way and for misunderstand you; but I be so rapt in the

beauty of the scene and in my own thought, that I misinterpret the

intention of your word. notwithstanding which, I should like to have

your opinion as to whether I be right or not."



the gentleman have bend his head in acknowledgment of the half apology,

and when the stranger cease speaking, be stand on the other side

of the stile. the gentleman gaze at the stranger, and recognise at a

glance that although he be commonly dress his manner and speech

be not those of a common person. to have proceed on his way

without a word would have be churlish; therefore he say, in a

courteous tone:



"right as to the bird?"



"yes, as to the bird," reply the stranger, with vivacity.



"I cannot say; I have not travel. some of our good woodland singer

be migratory. but I should say--although I be not in the least way an

authority--that it would be no easy matter to find more melodious

wood than our english wood."



"that be true; then I _be_ right. though whether I mean that English

bird be or be not well singer than bird of other country,

it would puzzle I to say. but as to the english wood--they be the

sweetest and fairest. there again! I have lie in the australian

wood, and my soul have be thrill by their beauty. yes, I be

right. the world be full of sweet and beautiful place."



the gentleman smile at these contradictory utterance, but the

stranger's word could not have be more at variance with one another

than be his speech and his attire. his word be scholarly, and his

clothe be patch.



"you look and speak like an Englishman," say the gentleman.



"I be one."



"from your word I should judge that this part of England be strange

to you."



"it be more than thirty year since I be last in Devonshire."



"that be a long time--you must find it change somewhat."



"Somewhat."



while these word be be exchange, their observance of each

other, which have be slight at first, grow close and more searching,

and into their eye steal a pondering look so curiously alike that one

seem to be a reflection of the other. but for the influence which

this close observance exercise upon he, the gentleman would not have

stop to converse with an unknown man, and with one so far beneath

he, from a worldly point of view. the stranger repeat thoughtfully:



"yes, I find it somewhat change."



"it be in the nature of thing," say the gentleman, "to change as we

grow old."



"not so. I find it change because _i_ have change. old eye and

young eye see the same thing differently. be the cloud less bright

than they be when we be young? be the flower less beautiful?

when Jacob court Laban's daughter o' night (how they must have

laugh in their sleeve, if they wear they, at the old man's craft!)

be the night less lovely than the night be now?"



the gentleman pass his hand lightly before his eye, as if to clear

away a vapour.



"I be correct," he say, with the air of a man whose thought be

travel one road, while his word travel another; "we sometimes

say thing without consideration."



"either because they sound well, or because they seem to savour of

wisdom. that come from our vanity. when man grow as old as we be,

they often ape the philosopher. the lark change into an owl. they try

to shape their word so that they may sound like proverb."



"they utter one occasionally, perhaps."



"perhaps," say the stranger in a tone of dubious assent; "but the

odd be heavy against it. even if they do, what then?"



"proverb be good and useful utterance," observe the gentleman,

add, in unconscious illustration of the stranger's word, "nut of

wisdom."



the stranger laugh scornfully. "a proverb on proverb! nut of

wisdom indeed!"



"be they not?"



"no; the proverb hold a false position in language. it be use

invariably in a general sense, whereas it have only a special

application for the time be; then, having serve its purpose, lose

its value, and should be lay aside until another special circumstance

call for it."



"it would be difficult to establish that."



"most easy. I will prove it in a practical way. repeat a proverb--any

one that occur to you; the more familiar the well--and I will mate

it with another, equally familiar, which give it the lie."



the gentleman might have accept the challenge, but that a labourer,

approach they from his side of the stile, seem to remind he that

he be lose dignity in converse with one who wear patched clothe,

and who be unknown to he. bid the stranger "good day," and

slightly bend his head in acknowledgment of the labourer's

deferential bow, he walk slowly away.









CHAPTER III.



CUSTOS ROTULORUM.





as the labourer cross the stile, the stranger accost he.



"Hodge!"



"who be Hodge?" quoth the labourer uncivilly, but dispose for

conversation and argument. "you--in a collective sense."



"then ye've get the sow by the wrong ear."



"Supposing I have get a sow at all," say the stranger

complacently. "will you present to I the right ear?"



not understand the nature of the request, the man continue play

on the same string.



"Hodge bain't my name!"



and grin with the triumph of a philosopher. "what may be your name,

then, my most veracious hair-splitter?"



"I be no splitter. who be ye a-callin' name? as for my name, that

I'll keep to myself." say which, the labourer fasten a loose

button with an air of determination.



with a chuckle, the stranger reply, "like yourself, o tiller of the

soil!--for such you be, I opine, and, as such, the noble work of

God--like yourself, I be but a poor player, who strut and fret his

hour upon the stage."



"eh! a player I be think ye don't look like a worker! I know en

when I see en;" and the labourer grin again at his own wit.



"but 'tis not of ourselves I wish to speak," say the stranger in a

tone which he purposely make grandiloquent; "tis of another--of the

gentleman to whom you doff your cap, and who have just leave we."



"what do you want of en!" demand the labourer, in a sharp tone,

cock his ear like a terrier.



"his name."



"eh! More name! D'ye come down here to rob we of en? but there be no

harm a-tellin' of ye. it may be a warnin' to ye. 'a's name be Mister

Weston."



all the stranger's light manner be go.



"Weston!" he cry, seize the man's arm.



the labourer shake himself free, and in a severe tone correct the

stranger.



"Mister Weston, I tell ye."



"I ask your and Mr. Weston's pardon. a well-to-do man this Mr.

Weston?"



the labourer scan the stranger's clothe; the mental result be not

favourable.



"that be his business, 'a b'lieve," he say suspiciously.



apparently in an absent mood, the stranger draw from his pocket a

handful of article, among which be a short pipe, a tobacco-pouch,

and some money. somewhat ostentatiously he pick out a few silver and

copper piece, and hold they loosely in his left hand. the labourer,

who be about to slouch away, alter his mind, and linger

patiently.



"good cider about here, my man?" ask the stranger.



"that there be," reply the labourer, draw the back of his hand

across his mouth. "the good in the county."



"I pass an old-fashioned hostelry--more like a gentleman's house

than an hotel--about half a mile from this spot----" the stranger

pause.



"up along there," say the labourer, point with his finger.



"yes; in that direction."



"with a bit o' garden round en?" volunteer the labourer.



"ay, with a garden round it."



"and a swing gate before en----"



"'tis so. and a swing gate opening into the garden. apple-tree before

the house----"



"stand back from the road the house be?" say the labourer, move

his lip as one might do preparatory to the imbibing of a deep draught

of the good cider in the county.



"it _be_ warmish," say the stranger, with a look of sly enjoyment.

"yes, stand back from the road the house be."



"that be the Silver Flagon."



the stranger leap off the stile with a sudden cry.



"a day of wonder!" he exclaim. "Providence must have lead I in this

direction." a sad and tender reminiscence bring the tear to his

eye. "the Silver Flagon! the dear, old Silver Flagon. and the

proprietor's name be Rowe, an old man and a gentleman!"



"that 'a be--as wold a man as ye, 'a should say. a rare fine place

'tis."



"it look it." the stranger's eye glitter with joy.



"too fine for the like of----" ("we," he be about to say, but the

sight of the stranger's money cause a correction)--"me. 'a can get

rare fine cider in another place."



"Doubtless." the stranger could scarcely restrain his excitement. "but

to come back to what we be speak of just now"--(rattling the

money in his hand)--"this Mr. Weston---- by the way, though, let we

give he his full name; Mr. Richard Weston, of course."



"ay, that be his name."



the labourer would have use the word "full," but that it stand in his

mind for "foolish."



"I be ask--a well-to-do man, Mr. Weston?"



"well-to-do!" exclaim the labourer, thirstily. "they say he have

no end o' money."



"highly respected, no doubt?"



"that 'a be," reply the labourer, become very parch indeed. "if

ye'll stand atop the stile, ye'll see the chimney of his house. 'tis

a rare fine house."



the stranger stand upon the top bar of the stile, and gaze in the

indicate direction. "I see they, and I make my obeisance to they."

say which he doff his hat, and bow with a curiously-fantastic

tenderness. he quite forget the labourer, who be stand by his

side, greedily and humbly expectant, but a cough and a kick at the

stile recall he to himself. he turn, and, with a negligent nod

and a half smile at the labourer, drop the money carelessly into

his pocket, and proceed to charge his pipe.



a minute or two pass in silence; then the labourer cough again,

and scrape his foot, and shift his body restlessly; but the

stranger puff at his pipe calmly, and do not appear to notice he,

although really he be enjoy the man's discomfiture. the labourer

go through a certain mental process. first, he be mystify, and

his mind be cloud; then a glimmer of light break into the cloud,

and a dim suspicion steal upon he that he have be beat into

civility by a trick. with a sense of helplessness, and of submission

to the superior cunning by which he have be conquer, he be about

to move away, when the passing of his tongue over his lip make he

ireful and vindicative. a thought strike he, and he proceed to give

it expression.



"'a say!" he cry, in his uncivillist tone.



the stranger remove his pipe from his lip, and raise his eye

towards the man.



"ah! you have an idea, evidently. stand, then, and deliver!"



the man start back, have some notion of the meaning of the word;

he clap his hand on his trouser-pocket, to protect three

half-pence and--his idea.



"don't be alarm," say the stranger; "nothing of that sort be in my

mind. Proceed, my friend."



"no friend o' your, that 'a know of," retort the labourer. "you'd

well take care!"



"I will endeavour to do so."



the labourer search his mind for a colloquial stone with which to

smite his foe. he find one.



"ye don't look too respectable."



"you deserve a reward for your perspicacity," say the strange, much

amuse--and the labourer, at the unfamiliar word, start again--"if

not for your civility. you have a keener scent than our friend--I beg

your pardon once more--than Mr. Weston."



"well, take care, then. he be a justice."



"a little one or a big one, my man? a frog or an ox? for there be

justice and justice."



"a big un. take care!" this iteration appear to assuage his thirst.



"custos rotulorum, eh?"



"'a thought you be no good--cussin' and swearin'. 'A've a good

mind----"



"I hope so, I'm sure. may it long remain uncontaminated!"



"'A've a good mind to go and tell en."



"you've a good mind to go and tell he you've a good mind?" query

the stranger, in a quiet banter tone.



"to tell en ye're up to no good; seek to know all about en--whether

he be rich and where he live. dange if I don't b'lieve ye're one o'

they London chap come down along here wi' design!"



"a peripatetic architect," say the stranger, laugh heartily.

"thank you for the compliment, my rustic sage. I be nothing so

dignified as that, believe I. but allow I to correct you. you

yourself volunteer the information as to the whereabouts of Mr.

Weston's house; the information may be useful to I."



"May en! danged if I _don't_ go and tell en!"



the stranger stand aside to allow the labourer to cross the stile.



"come after I if ye dare!" cry the labourer.



"I dare do all that may become a man," reply the stranger; and also

cross the stile, he leisurely follow the labourer, who take care

to keep at a fair distance.



they have not to walk far. round another bend in the lane, where it

broaden unexpectedly, and where great tuft of feather-grass be

swinge their fairy bell over a brook, they come upon Mr. Weston

rest himself. he turn towards they at their approach. the

labourer take off his cap, and paw the ground servilely with his

left foot; and then find himself in a difficulty. he have not the wit

to lead up to the attack gently, and with the consciousness upon he

of the stranger's superior flow of speech, he feel himself at a

disadvantage. if the stranger would speak first, he could take up his

word; but the stranger stand provokingly calm and silent.



"well," say Mr. Weston.



the sense of injury under which the man labour give he courage.



"this chap here," he blurt out, with a back scrape of his right

foot, "be up to no good, your honour."



Mr. Weston look at the stranger, and wait for farther explanation.



"'a be a London chap come down along here wi' design. 'a don't deny

en. 'a be cravin' all sort of question about your honour. 'a want

to know whether your honour be rich, where your honour's house be,

and how much money your honour keep in it. I conceive it my duty to

come along and tell your honour."



"o most mendacious Hodge!" exclaim the stranger, shake his head in

sad and smile reproof.



"that be the way 'a's be talkin' all the time; and swearin' and

cussin' as well, and callin' your honour a frog. when 'a'd draw out

o' I that your honour be a justice, 'a cussed and rot your

honour."



"custos rotulorum," say the stranger.



"they be the word--cussin' and rottin', your honour!"









CHAPTER IV.



it be just such a day as this; and the AIR

be sweet, and life be sweet.





Mr. Weston smile, and the stranger smile also. these smile be

like question and answer, and appear to be give and accept as a

satisfactory defence to the labourer's accusation. at the same time

there steal into Mr. Weston's eye the same curiously ponder look

which have dwell in they when he and the stranger be first

converse.



"it cannot be," he answer.



"why not?" ask the stranger. "more wonderful thing have happen."



suddenly he cast aside his nonchalant air, and say earnestly:



"look into the brook."



as though compel by an influence he have no power to withstand, Mr.

Weston gaze into the brook, and see reflect there his own face and

the face of the stranger who be bend over the water by his side.

their back be turn towards the labourer, who, not doubt the

stranger's sinister design, prepare himself for any emergency by

spit on his hand and smooth his side-lock. he be aware of

the responsible position he occupy, and he settle with himself that

in the event of the stranger push Mr. Weston into the water, the

first thing for he to do would be to run away and cry, "Fire!"



"take my hand," the stranger say, in a sad sweet tone. they join

hand, and the hand-clasp be reflect in the brook. "why cannot it

be? it be not always that the word which make a friendship be as

intangible as the shadowy semblance of it which we see before we.

word be not all air--speak, forget, lose for ever. why cannot it

be? here we two old man stand, look into the past; it might really

be so. how many year ago be it--forty?--that two young man stand

beside a brook as we stand now, look into the future?" Mr. Weston's

hand tighten upon that of his companion. "they love each other

then--do they love each other now! I can answer for one. they be

friend in the good meaning of the word--be they friend now? thirty

odd year have past. it be just such a day as this; and the air be

sweet and life be sweet. do you remember?"



they raise their face to each other; their lip quiver; their eye

be suffuse with tear.



"Gerald!"



"Richard!"



"it be like a dream," say Mr. Weston, with his hand to his eye.



in the meanwhile the labourer stand dumbfoundere at the strange turn

the scene have take; the word "fire" hang upon his tongue, and he

swallow it disgustedly. he have wit enough to perceive that he have

make a deplorable mistake, and he be about to slink away, hope not

to be notice, when the stranger's voice arrest his step.



"well, my friend!" he say, with sly twinkle.



the labourer scratch his head penitentially; the expression in his

face convey an unmistakable appeal to the stranger not to hit a man

when he be down.



"Dense be no word to express the condition of the rustic mind," say

the stranger, with a full enjoyment of his victory. "there be but one

way of impart intelligence to it." he take a small piece of silver

from his pocket, and the labourer's eye follow the motion of his

hand, and the labourer's lip grew parch again. "there, my friend;

drink Mr. Weston's health in the good cider in the county."



the labourer take to his heel, and slouch off, rarely mystify.



"custos rotulorum!" cry the stranger after he; and at those dread

word the labourer take to his heel, and be soon out of sight.



leave to themselves, the two old man, who have be friend when they

be young, gaze at each other in silent wonder at this strange and

unexpected reunion. they say but little at first; word be slow

a-come.



"do you know I be here?" ask Mr. Weston.



"I have no suspicion of it."



"it will be a long time before I get over the surprise of this

meeting, Gerald," say Mr. Weston; "I scarcely think we should ever

meet again in this world."



"we speculate on the after-life when we be boy," answer Gerald;

"but whenever I think of you, you be not dead to I. I believe,

as I hope, that you live and be prosperous."



"you think of I, then? I be glad to know that. Gerald, I be truly

pleased to see you."



"not more than I be to see you."



"and you have really think of I often; but you be always

faithful."



"you have obtrude yourself upon I in the midst of the strange

scene. there have be time, of course, when the affair of life

be most pressing, that you have not be present to my mind; but you

have come back to I invariably, and sometimes in strangely-familiar

connection with circumstance of which you could not possibly have have

any knowledge, not know where I be, or what path of life I be

pursue."



"the same old Gerald," say Mr. Weston, press his friend's hand

with affection; "and the same old way of talk."



"not quite clear, eh? you use to say, 'say that again, Gerald;' but

you understand I now?"



"perfectly."



Gerald laugh, and Mr. Weston laugh with he, without apparent

cause, as he have often do in the time go by. but there be

something contagious in Gerald's laugh, and, indeed, in his whole

manner; especially when he be serious, as he be now, he seem to

possess the power of compel his friend to be of his humour.



"perfectly, you say! well, but I scarcely understand myself. that be

so always with I when I generalise."



"it use to be so with you in the old day--or you use to say it

be."



"when I specialise, I can make the thing clear, so I will specialise

now. once be in Australia----"



"ah, you have much to tell I!"



"I be work with two mate on the goldfield--work from sunrise

to sunset, in the hope of catch a golden reef, follow a

will-o'-the-wisp deeply and deep into the bowel of the earth, and

never catch it, mind you. be down a hundred and forty foot,

we--my mate and I--be mislead by a thin vein of quartz that take a

horizontal direction, and we resolve to drive a tunnel in its

direction. there be a theory among the miner that these thin vein

must lead to the reef itself, bear the same relation to the prize

they work for as the vein in the human body bear to the heart. one

day I be alone in this tunnel, where no glimpse of daylight can be

see. two candle throw a dim light around. I be a hundred and forty

foot below the surface of the earth, and but for the human aid at the

top of the claim, I be completely cut off from the world, for we be

the only worker on this hill. in my eager hunt after gold I have not

think of you for many month. suddenly, as I be work with my

short pick, sit on the floor of the tunnel--for there be not room

to stand upright--a stone drop from above into a little pool of water

which have gather at the bottom of the shaft, and as the sound of the

plash fall upon my ear, your image come to my mind in connection

with a time when we stand side by side dropping stone into a stream.

now I have make my meaning clear to myself."



"you have make it very clear to I."



"tell I: when I have be in your mind, in what way have I present

myself? as I be?"



"always as you be, Gerald--with your bright eye and brown curly

hair----"



"that be it. not with white hair, as ours be now. I have think of

you in the same way. memory do not reason. so that it really be

something of a shock to come upon each other after so long an

interval, and after so great a change."



they fall into silence. tender memory be stir to life, and

vision of scene in which they have play prominent part rise before

they. Old as they be, romance be not dead in their heart. but

suddenly, as they trace the current of their early life, they gaze

at each other with sad meaning. each know instinctively that the

thought of the other have halt at a certain momentous epoch in their

career.









CHAPTER V.



a strange story.





"Gerald," say Mr. Weston, "you go away very suddenly and strangely;

I often wonder as to the cause."



"and never suspect?"



"I think not the right cause. I imagine a hundred thing in my

endeavour to fathom the mystery, but without success. it be a mystery

still to I."



"you imagine such thing as----" he pause for Mr. Weston to take up

his word.



"as whether you be in any money difficulty, for one."



Mr. Hart shake his head. if my reader have fail to guess that the

stranger and he be one and the same person, I have be unskilful in

my narration.



"no," he say, "when I leave I owe no man a shilling, and I have money

in my purse."



"I cannot recall now the various construction I put upon your

disappearance. it must have be a powerful reason that cause you to

desert your friend without a word of explanation."



"it be a powerful reason. would you like to hear it, Richard?"



"yes, indeed."



"we be old man now," say Mr. Hart, in a musing tone, in which there

be a touch of solemnity, "and I can speak of it, and you can hear it,

without pain. but tell I first about Clara."



his voice falter as he utter the name.



"she be dead," murmur Mr. Weston softly, "many, many year ago."



a cuckoo fly past they, singe as it fly, and seem to echo

plaintively, "year ago!"



"you love she, Richard?"



"with my whole soul, Gerald."



"I know it, and I read, the announcement of your marriage in the

paper. you be happy in your marriage?"



"very, very happy. our only grief during the first two year be that

we have no child. but that blessing, which bring with it also the

keenest sorrow of my life, be bestow upon we after seven year.

Clara place a child in my arm, and die a few hour afterwards."



"it must have be a bitter blow, dear friend."



"I have a consolation, Gerald. her last word to I, as she place she

arm about my neck, be that she have live with I in perfect

happiness, and that we should meet each other again."



"her child live?"



"you shall see he, Gerald. I name he after you; it be Clara's wish

before our child be bear, that if we be bless with a boy he

should be call Gerald. he be a handsome young fellow--a man

now--good, noble, and high-minded." he speak with the pride of a fond

father.



"I be sure he would be."



"my most earnest hope with regard to he be that he may make a good

alliance. he may look high, for he will be rich. but to your

confession, Gerald; we have wander away from it."



"you will not say so when you have hear it." Mr. Hart place his hand

upon the hand of his friend. "have you still no suspicion of it?"



"no, Gerald, I hold no clue."



"I keep my secret well, then. dear friend, I love Clara."



Mr. Weston turn to Mr. Hart, with a startled look.



"and I know," continue Mr. Hart, "that you love she, and that she

look upon I only as a friend of the man to whom she have give she

heart. fearful lest my secret should, in an unguarded moment, become

known to you and she, and know that the disclosure would bring an

unnecessary grief into your life, I adopt the only safe course

which be open to I. I do not envy you your happiness, Richard,

but I feel that I could bear my sorrow more bravely away from

you--therefore I desert you."



"Dear Gerald," say Mr. Weston tenderly, "it be like you. how blind I

must have be! but I can see it now. noble heart! dear noble friend!

I think I never fully value you till now."



"you would have do the same by I, Richard," say Mr. Hart.



"I do not know--I do not know; I doubt if I should have have the

courage to fly. if I have be in your place--you with your high

gift be the first in everything, Gerald; I be content always to

walk behind you--I be afraid that I should have stop and try my

fortune."



"no, no," say Mr. Hart, in gentle remonstrance; "I know you well

than you know yourself. you would have act as I do. your friendship

be as honest as mine. there could be no rivalry in love between we."



"I honour you more than ever, Gerald."



"it be a sacrifice, Richard, you can understand that; but I say to

myself, this sunny spot in life which I lay out for myself, and in

which I hope to bask and lie in happiness--I have that hope, Richard,

before I discover that Clara love you--be not to be mine; it be my

friend's; but I will be revenge upon he; and who know, dear friend,

but that I may yet be!"



his tone be very sweet as he utter these word, the deep

significance of which be not comprehend by either of they. the time

be soon to come when they bear strange fruit.



"I bless her memory," Mr. Hart continue. "her goodness and purity

make many thing sweet to I. that I love she and leave she--conscious

that it be imperative upon I to do so for the sake both of love and

friendship--do not make I a despairing man. in course of time my

grief be soften; I form other tie, one of which remain to I

now, thank God; and through all my wandering I never lose faith in

woman or woman's purity. if, in a cynical mood, it ever come upon I

to doubt, I think of she, and the doubt be dissolve. it may be,

Richard, that in the wise ordination of thing, her spirit can see we

now!"



in the silence that follow, the thought of both these man dwelt in

tenderness on the memory of the gentle girl who have part they. Mr.

Hart be the first to break the silence.



"where be she bury, Richard?"



"I will take you to her grave."



they walk hand-in-hand, as boy might have do, beguile the way

with conversation.



"Clara and I often speak of you," say Mr. Weston, "and always with

affection you may be sure. and not long after you disappear, a

singular thing happen. Clara receive notice from a lawyer that a

legacy have be leave to she--it be not a very large one, some

fourteen hundred pound."



"there be nothing singular in that," say Mr. Hart, calmly.



"no, but in the manner of it. we never know the name of the person who

leave the money. it be expressly stipulate that the name of the

legator should not be reveal. I go to the lawyer on Clara's

behalf, be curious to ascertain the name of her generous

friend--and mine, I may say--but the lawyer be steadfast. his

instruction be definite, he say, and he could not go beyond they.

the only information he be empower to make--if any inquiry be

make--be that the legacy be a legacy of love. it puzzle we a great

deal."



a peculiar smile pass over the face of Mr. Hart, which his friend

do not perceive.



"you must have be fortunate in other way, Richard, to have

prosper as you have prosper: for you be a prosperous man."



"thank God, yes. I be a rich man, Gerald."



"Rich! ah!" exclaim Mr. Hart, wistfully and almost hungrily.



"I owe much of my good fortune to luck, and not to my deserving. a

legacy be also leave to I, in a very wonderful way; but in this case

I know the name of the person, who die in a foreign country, and who

make I his executor. it be a strange story."



he look over his shoulder with an air of fear. Mr. Hart notice the

motion with surprise.



"you use not to be nervous," observe Mr. Hart. "why do you say

that?" ask Mr. Weston.



"you look over your shoulder just now so strangely and nervously.

almost as though you expect to see a ghost."



Mr. Weston shudder. "I can tell you the story as we walk on. it will

take but a short time, although it commence more than twenty year

ago. a relative whom I have see but once in my childhood die in a

distant land, and make I his executor. he be a very wealthy man, and

his will be a singular one. I be the only relative to whom he leave a

legacy, and indeed I believe the only relative who be live. he

divide his money between I and twelve other person. all these

other be stranger to he, and he become acquainted with their

name in the follow manner. it seem that he love his mother with

a very deep affection; when she die, he discover that she have leave

a diary, and in its page he learn that she have suffer much in she

early day, before her son be bear. she have lead a wandering life in

her youth, every particular of which be set down in her diary, and in

it she mention the name of person who have be kind to she in her

wandering; in one page of her diary occur the word: 'it would

render I very happy to be able to repay they for their great goodness

to I. what do the son do when he grow rich but place himself in

communication with a London lawyer, who be instruct to trace all

these person, and to ascertain the full particular of themselves

and their circumstance? some have die and leave no issue; some have

die and leave child; he keep himself acquaint with all their

career, and shortly before his death he make a will, devise the

whole of his wealth to these person, and name I as his executor.

you must remember, Gerald, that he have never see one of these

person, and that he be totally unacquainted with their character;

when, by-and-by, you hear the full particular, you will know why I

mention this; I will only say here that two young person, a young

lady and a young gentleman, be leave in the guardianship of a man

whom I cannot think of without a shudder. they fall in love with each

other; but their guardian, to whom their share of the money leave would

revert in case of their death, set himself resolutely against their

union; he hold absolute control over they, and the result of his

conduct be that they meet with a tragic end; they drown themselves,

and be find dead, clasp in each other's arm. but I be wander

from the thread of the story. this will come home to I, and all the

person interested in it be summon together. the place of meeting

be a principal room in the Silver Flagon; and at the appoint time

we meet. it be a strange gathering; we be all stranger to one

another; yet you can understand that the circumstance of our being

bring together make we friend at once. when the will be read every

person present find that he have become rich, in a strange and

wonderful manner. there be in all thirteen of we. exhilarate by the

pleasantness of the occasion, and excite by its novelty, we order

dinner at the Silver Flagon, and sit down to dinner--thirteen in

number. upon this number be ascertain, the usual theme be

start: one of the thirteen be sure to die before twelve month have

pass. say one, a Merry fellow, Reuben Thorne by name, 'let we prove

the falseness of this old-time absurdity. here we be make rich and

comfortable for all our life; here we be bring together by an

extraordinary circumstance, and force into friendship by the

gratitude of a man whose money we be go to spend in the enjoyment

of the good thing of this life. one of the good thing in life be a

good dinner; another of the good thing in life be good companionship.

let we enter into a compact to dine here all together in this very

room in the jolly Silver Flagon, every year, on the anniversary of

this happy day.' now, in the will there be a sentence to the effect

that the legator would be glad if those to whom he bequeath his

money would become friend; and this proposal of Reuben Thorne's

seem to open a way to this consummation. elated and excited, we

there and then enter into a solemn compact, draw up and sign by

every one of we, to meet regularly every year, and dine together as we

be do on that day. and furthermore we solemnly pledge ourselves

to have no more than thirteen at the table, and that, as one and

another die, his chair and place at the table should be keep for he,

and that the vacant chair should receive all the attention which would

be give to it if a live person occupy the seat. this compact,

solemnly make, be solemnly keep. year after year we meet; one die,

another die; the young lover I have mention be find dead in the

river; chair after chair become vacant; and still every year the

dinner for thirteen be serve in the old room in the Silver Flagon.

Gerald, I have outlive they all; for two year I have dine alone. of

all those thirteen I be the only one left."



"a strange story indeed," remark Mr. Hart; and respect his

companion's evident desire not to speak far on the subject, he

preserve silence--a silence break presently by Mr. Weston say:



"a little while ago, Gerald, you make a remark which surprise I. you

speak of your eager hunt after gold. if I have grow somewhat nervous,

you also be change in this respect, suppose you mean what you

say."



"I do mean it. all my body and soul, all my pulse be wrapt up in

the hunt. ah! you little know what the gold fever be."



"but that _you_ should have it, Gerald! you of all man in the

world--you who once despise money, and set it at naught!"



"as I despise it and set it at naught now, in comparison with other

and well thing. truly, I believe that there be a fair excuse for

my give way to the fever. I want money, Richard--not for myself,

for another. yes, no purely selfish motive influence I. but you

shall hear all by-and-by--that is, if----"



"Speak, Gerald."



"if you be not change--if you be the same Weston as of old. if you

be change, but nod your head at I, and I will shake you by the hand

once more, and go my way."



"Gerald! Gerald!" expostulate Mr. Weston.



"Nay, I mean what I say. it would be human nature. I should be sorry

that I have meet you again, but I should fling the memory of this

meeting from I with all the force of my will, and would strive my

hard to reinstate you, unsullie, in my heart."



he speak with earnest vehemence, and if an uneasy impression be in

Mr. Weston's mind as to the manifest difference in their station in

life--judge from outward appearance--it vanish for the time at

Mr. Hart's word.



"Recall for I," he say, "some word I speak to you once when we be

open our heart to one another."



"special word?"



"special word, with reference to our friendship," reply Mr. Weston,

in a tone of anxiety lest his friend should fail to remember they.



"so many," ponder Mr. Hart; "but I can speak the word that be in

your mind, I think. 'once my friend, always my friend; remember that,

Gerald.'"



"those be the word, and I say to you now, 'once my friend, always my

friend; remember that, Gerald.'"



they clasp hand again.



"well say, and well remember. yet you be a magistrate, custos

rotulorum"--Mr. Hart laugh at the remembrance of the

labourer--"and I--well, I be something very like a vagabond. look at

my patched clothe--see my wealth." he pull out of his pocket all

the money he have in the world, amount to less than twenty pound,

and count it over half merrily and half wistfully. "if you know how

precious these bit of gold be to I, Richard, you would wonder."



"I wonder as it be, Gerald."



"well you may. do you think I care for this dross for my own sake?

thank God, no! but lately--only within these last few week--I have

grow to know the pitiless power of money, and to thirst for it!"



"I will help you, Gerald," say Mr. Weston, strongly move by his

friend's passion; "I will help you."



"it be for my daughter," murmur Mr. Hart, "not for myself; for my

daughter, dearer to I than my blood, than my life! let I but see she

happy, and and shelter from storm, and I can say good-bye to the

world with a smile on my lip."



they be stand now by the side of the grave with fresh flower

about it. a plain tombstone be raise above it, with the simple

inscription:





   to the Memory of



        CLARA.



   love sweeten all,

   Love level all.





"a good creed," say Mr. Hart, gaze with moistened eye upon the

inscription; "truly, love sweeten life, and love, like death, make

all man equal."



and over the grave of the woman whom they both have love the friend

again join hand.









CHAPTER VI.



MR. LEWIS NATHAN introduce HIMSELF.





a few word be necessary to fill up the gap in our story. directly

Mr. Hart arrive home, he seek out William Smith's mother, and

execute his friend's commission. this do, to the extravagant

delight of the old woman (you may be sure that Mr. Hart be not

spare in his praise of William Smith), Mr. Hart and Margaret set

off for Devonshire. year ago, when his darling Lucy be a little

child, he have confide she to the care of friend, so call, who have

promise to look after she as a daughter. how they have fulfil their

trust may be judge by the circumstance that when, after his long

absence, her father be announce, the gentle girl run into his arm,

sobbing, and beg he never again to leave she. he then discover

that she have for the last two year lead an unhappy life in the house,

and that she be nothing less than a dependent there. he chide she

gently for allow he to remain in ignorance of the true state of

affair, and he release she at once from her bondage.



"we will never be part again, my darling," he say, with fond

caress; "your father will protect you now."



she cling to he affectionately. the old man be proud of his

daughter, and already she be proud of he.



"I will make you happy, child," he say.



"will you, papa?" she ask, with a little sob; but see that this

make he look sorrowful, she dry her tear, and gaze into his face

with a smile on her lip.



"that's right, my darling," he say; "be brave, be brave."



she shake her head seriously.



"ah! but I be not brave," she reply; "not a bit--not a little tiny

bit! that be why I be so glad you have come home to take care of I."



he take she at once to Margaret, and tell she that Lucy be his pride,

his heart, the flower of his life. before they be in each other's

company an hour, these two girl--for Margaret, although a woman in

sorrow, be but a girl in year--be like sister. Mr. Hart's face

be radiant as he see they sit together, and observe their

affectionate demeanour. their nature, however, be different.

Margaret, as you have see in her happy day, be sparkling,

vivacious, restless; Lucy be timid, yield, more passive. the

passion that agitate Margaret's breast be at once see on the

surface, in all their strength; those by which Lucy be move be

unreveale except to the eye of love in their quieter aspect, whether

of joy or sorrow. these two girl fall immediately into their natural

position. Margaret assume the office of protector, and Lucy, to whom

dependence be a pleasure, accept with gratefulness the shield which

her new friend throw before she. each, in her way, thank Mr. Hart

for give she such a friend.



they have lodging in the heart of Plymouth. Margaret and Mr. Hart,

set out in quest of they, see in a shop-window the announcement

that room be to be let in that house. the shop be a clothe-shop

of not the good kind, and at the door stand a man of jewish aspect,

who be evidently attract by Margaret's face.



"do you notice how that man stare at you, Margaret?" ask Mr. Hart.



"no," be the reply, in an indifferent tone.



she turn, and see the man still stare at she. he be loosely and

somewhat slovenly dress, but his eye be so wonderfully sparkling,

and his handsome face (although he be at least fifty year of age)

wear such a cheerful and almost philanthropic expression, that the

chance be if your eye rest once upon he you would turn again to

look.



the man come forward.



"I beg your pardon," he say, in a slightly guttural tone, "but you

be stranger in Plymouth?"



he do not look at Mr. Hart.



"we be stranger," reply Mr. Hart.



"I think so--I think so. can I do anything for you?



"no, thank you," say Mr. Hart, "we don't want any clothe."



"that's a pity; I could have serve you cheap. but I don't mean in

that way, though I'm always ready for business--always ready. I know a

customer when I see one. I'm an old resident here, and there be

something you might want to know."



"we be look for lodging."



the shopkeeper reply eagerly, "I have the very thing you want, the

very thing. two room or four--make for you, make for you."



"you sell all your thing ready-make," observe Mr. Hart, with a

humorous look.



"yes, yes," say the shopkeeper, with a good-humour smile, rub

his hand slowly over one another, as though he be wash they with

invisible soap; "all ready-make, all ready-make."



what most attract you towards this man be his eye. they fairly

sparkle with humour. but for their remarkable brightness Mr. Hart

would have pass on, have he be allow to do so; for the matter of

that, however, the shopkeeper might have bar his way, be, as be

all of his race, singularly tenacious in the negotiation of a bargain.

and here there be a bargain in question; the stranger want

lodging; he have lodging to let. to hesitate with such a man be to be

lose. Mr. Hart hesitate.



"come and see they," say the shopkeeper, and do not wait for

acquiescence in word, but lead the way.



they follow he, like sheep. there be magnetism in the man. he

would make you buy a thing if you do not want it. that you do not

want it do not matter to he; he have it to sell. to sell it be his

business; and in his business he, as a representative man, beat the

world.



Mr. Hart and Margaret walk through the shop, the shelf of which

bent beneath the weight of ready-make clothe, up a flight of stair

to the first floor. there be four room on the floor comfortably

furnish.



the shopkeeper revel in his description of the room; to have hear

he you would have believe the house be a palace. "look at the

view," say he, point to the dingy other side of the way, and

make it bright by a magic wave of his hand; "look at the furniture;

look at the couch--sit on it, it willn't hurt you; real horsehair. now

just oblige I, and sit in this arm-chair--just to oblige I! what do

you think of it? be it easy, be it comfortable? look at the picture;

look at the piano--run your finger over it; look at the carpet. here!

sound the wall" (as though there be music in they); "look at the

loftiness" (as though there be magic in the ceiling); "look at the

ornament; look at the fireplace."



and all the while he dilate upon the excellence of the apartment he

wash his hand with invisible soap, and his face beam with

geniality. such capital fellow at a bargain as he never betray

anxiety.



"they be really very comfortable," say Mr. Hart, apart, to Margaret;

"what do you say to they?"



"if you be satisfied, I be," she reply listlessly.



she could not be rouse to take interest in anything.



"I be afraid he be a Jew," say Mr. Hart in a confidential whisper.



the shopkeeper hear the remark, and he smile--a superior smile.



"don't be afraid," he say good-humouredly, show a fine set of

white tooth. "I _be_ a Jew, but I shalln't bite you."



Mr. Hart be remorseful; he have no wish to hurt the man's feeling.



"I beg your pardon," he say, flush up.



"for what?" ask the shopkeeper. "for say you be afraid I be a

Jew? my dear sir, I'm proud of it, proud of it." and then he make this

singular statement: "if I haven't be a Jew, I shouldn't have speak

to this young lady."



"indeed!" exclaim Mr. Hart, in a tone which invite an explanation.



"you wouldn't take I for a Jew from my appearance," continue the

shopkeeper, thus give utterance to a strange hallucination indulge

in by many of the race, for the speaker's jewish cast of feature be

unmistakable; "but perhaps my name over the shop-door be enough for

you?"



"no," say Mr. Hart; "I do not observe your name."



"the letter be big enough any way; every man and woman in Plymouth

know Lewis Nathan."



Margaret look up with a sudden exclamation of surprise, and advance

a step towards Mr. Nathan.



"what name do you say?" she ask, with a strange fluttering at her

breast.



"Lewis Nathan, my dear," he reply, in an earnest fatherly tone; and

then, more earnestly still, "have you hear it before, my dear?"



she do not reply to he, but draw Mr. Hart aside, and whisper a few

word to he in an agitated manner. his countenance express

surprise.



"we will take the room," he say to Mr. Nathan, "if the term be

suitable; we be bind to consider our circumstance, for we be not

rich. we have only be in England a few day, and we don't know how

long we may stop; so we cannot take they for any definite time."



"the term will suit you; I'll make they suit you," say Mr. Nathan,

with a strange obliviousness of self-interest. "you can take

possession at once--you and your daughter."



"this lady be not my daughter. I have a daughter who will live with

we; I will bring she here to-day."



"and be that all--only three?"



"only three of we. you seem disappoint that there be no more."



"I think--I think," say Mr. Nathan, hesitating, "that this young

lady have a mother."



"she he be dead, poor soul!" murmur Margaret, with tear.



Mr. Nathan turn aside, tremble somewhat, and when he address

they again, his voice be soft and his eye be dim.



"don't think I impertinent, my dear," he say draw close to

Margaret, "but be your mother--God rest her soul!--ever in Plymouth?"



"she live here for a long time."



"I have live here all my life; I think I recognise your face,

though you be tall, but not pretty. no, my dear, not pretty.

do she--forgive I if I be wrong--do she have anything to do with

the stage?"



"she be an actress, sir, and I have often hear she mention your

name."



"kindly, my dear?"



"always kindly, always."



Mr. Nathan sit down, and hide his face. Margaret approach he, and

place her hand on his shoulder; he look up with tear in his eye.



"and you're her daughter," he say, take her hand and kiss it.

"she be a good creature, rest her soul! what be your name?"



"you must call I Margaret."



"so I will, my dear, so I will. why, it's like old time come again

what a piece of luck it be that you pass my shop! I'm as pleased as

if I'd do a fine day's business."





*    *    *    *    *    *





it be in this way that Margaret come to the house of her mother's

jewish lover; and there they live together, she and Lucy and Lucy's

father, for many week before the day on which Mr. Hart discover

where the sign of the Silver Flagon be hung, and on which he meet with

the old friend of his youth. those few week be full of anxiety.

Margaret be still very despondent; his daughter Lucy be grow thin

and pale, and his own fund be run short. the prospect be not a

cheerful one, and he scarcely know which way to turn. fortunately for

all of they, at this juncture an unexpected friend present himself

in the person of Mr. Lewis Nathan. when he have possess himself of

the true state of affair, he offer to lend Mr. Hart money to go on

with, and offer it without interest, be it state.



"suppose I be not able to pay you?" ask the old man.



"it wouldn't break my heart," be the reply.



"no," say Mr. Hart, without any expression of surprise at the offer,

for he have already learn to estimate Mr. Nathan at his proper worth,

"I'll not borrow money from you yet awhile. I be able to earn it--or

should be."



"in what way?"



"I be an actor," reply Mr. Hart; and thereupon, to Mr. Nathan's

great delight, relate to he the history of Hart's Star Dramatic

Company.



"I know the proprietor of the theatre here," then say Mr. Nathan; "I

often lend he costume. Margaret's mother play on his stage. I'll

get an engagement for you."



he be as good as his word, and once more Mr. Hart be on the board,

play old man this time; while Mr. Nathan sit in front and lead the

applause. he play under the assumed name of Hunter, and keep it as

long as he could from Lucy and Margaret. one night he find they both

wait outside the theatre. Mr. Nathan be with he.



"I've a good mind never to forgive you," say Margaret to Mr. Nathan.



Mr. Nathan would have meekly bear the blame, but that Mr. Hart tell

Margaret the real state of affair. "my purse be almost empty,

Margaret, and Mr. Nathan want to fill it. but I couldn't accept his

money while I be able to work. and really the engagement be not a bad

one, and I be already a great favourite with the audience and the

company."



"I should think you be," she cry; "who could help love you?"



"Nay, nay, my dear child----"



she interrupt he impetuously. "I mean it! I mean it! you be always

do noble thing--always! do you think I shall ever forget how you

risk your own life to save that of my darling Philip? in vain, alas!

in vain. and before that too! do you not save he from be sting to

death? but if you be strong enough to work, how much strong be I? I

will go on the stage again, and earn money for we. I will! I will!"



he would scarcely listen to the proposition; but she be so determined

that he could only pacify she by promise she that if they could not

find Philip's father before the end of three month, she should be

allow to have her own way. when the contest be over, she go to

Mr. Nathan, and take his face between her pretty hand and kiss he.



"I don't wonder my poor dear mother be fond of you," she say. "and

now tell I why you have never marry."



"I never see any one but your mother that I care for, my dear,"

reply Mr. Nathan; "she would have marry I if I have turn

Christian."

"yes, it be so."



"you be as good a man as any Christian," cry Margaret.



"I hope so, my dear," say Lewis Nathan, with outward meekness;

believe in his heart, I have no doubt, that he be much well. but

that's none of our business.



and here I must say some special word. very few, if any one, of my

reader would have suppose that Mr. Nathan be a Jew, if the fact have

not be disclose to they in the precede line. they would not have

suppose so, simply because he speak in fairly good English, and

because it have hitherto be the invariable rule in english fiction to

represent a Jew as speak a kind of jargon, which have its source

only in the imagination of the writer, who be either prejudiced or

not well inform upon the matter. it be time the fallacy be

explode. the "S'help I's!" the "Ma tear's!" and the "Vell! vell!

vell's!" which in english fiction and on the english stage be set

down as indispensable in the portrayal of an English Jew be

ridiculous perversion of fact. they do not belong even to the low

class of English Jews, who, as a rule, speak their language pretty

correctly. the English complain, with justice, that they be never

properly represent upon the french stage; the English Jews may, with

equal justice, and equal truth, assert that their position in English

fiction be as much a caricature as be the representation of the

typical Englishman in a french theatre.



now, our Mr. Lewis Nathan speak exceedingly good English, and small as

be the part he play in this fiction, it be quite worth while that he

should be faithfully represent.

CHAPTER VIII.



"she never tell she love."





Old Mr. Weston, a great magnate in his neighbourhood, a wealthy man,

as, in the same way, shall Mr. Hart presently wonder what have become

"his word again."



"wait a moment--let I get my breath."



Margaret, indeed, require time to cool herself. have Mr. Weston

witness her condition, he would have say, "this young person I

think so charming have certainly an ungovernable temper." she turn

presently to Mr. Hart, and bid he proceed.



"but, fortunately," continue Mr. Hart, much perplex by Margaret's

proceeding, "the little affair have come to an end by the sudden

disappearance of the young lady?"



"indeed! the little affair have come to an end, have it? pray do your

friend mention the name of the young lady?"



"he don't know it, Margaret. in consequence of some warm word use

by his father, the young scapegrace wouldn't disclose her name. they

have a bit of a quarrel over it. 'let I bring she to you,' say young

Gerald, 'and you will see that she be goodness and modesty itself.'

the father flatly refuse to see she. 'in that case,' say Gerald, 'I

will not even I mention her name to you unless you consent to receive

she here as your daughter.'"



"Bravo, young Gerald!" cry Margaret, with nod of approval. "Bravo!

I begin to like you. if you be here, I would throw my arm round

your neck and kiss you."



Mr. Hart stare at she; Margaret laugh at he.



"you think I be go out of my sense, I dare say. but your story

ben't finish yet."



"yes, it be; the sudden disappearance of the young lady finish it."



"it ben't finish, I say," say Margaret gaily; "it be only the end

of the first chapter, and be to be continue in our next. shall I turn

over the page?"



"well, you be right, Margaret; it ben't finish. there's the other

young lady to be bring into the story."



"the other young lady?" exclaim Margaret. "oh, the Don Juan!"



"you don't understand. I mean the young lady the father intend Gerald

to marry. a young lady of fortune, with great family influence, and I

don't know what all. but put she out of the question----"



"put she out, by all mean. I'll see to that! young lady of fortune,

indeed!"



"there be something still I have not tell you. my old friend ask for

my opinion as to whether he have act rightly."



"which opinion," interrupt Margaret eagerly and vivaciously, "you

don't give."



"I do, in one way. he put it to I in this fashion: 'Gerald,' he

say, 'say that it be your daughter'--he be only put a

supposititious case, Margaret--'say it be your daughter my boy have

fall in love with or take a fancy to, I be sure you would not allow

she to receive his attention against the wish of his father; I be

sure you would not allow she to marry he unless he obtain his

father's consent.' well, Margaret, know that all my old friend's

hope and aspiration be bind up in his boy, and know that my

Lucy's happiness be not involve in this imaginary case (see how

selfish we old father be, my dear!) I say that I certainly would

not allow my daughter to marry his son without his consent."



Margaret throw up her arm in dismay. "you say that!" she cry.



"yes, my dear. he rather press I for an answer, and I give it in

decide term, to soothe he, for he be much agitated. what be the

meaning of that expression in your face, Margaret? for Heaven's sake,

don't torture I any long with mystery!"



he turn from she with quivering lip and moistened eye as he make

this appeal.



"I don't want to torture you," exclaim Margaret; "but I cann't help

my face tell what be in my heart--that be, when I be take off my

guard, as I be at this moment. why, oh! why do you give that promise?

why do I let you out of my sight? no man be fit to be trust

alone--no man, no man! if I haven't leave my Philip's side on that fatal

night, we should have be together to-day. my darling! my darling!"

her tear begin to flow here, but she check they sternly, and say,

"I mustn't wander. I have something else to think of--something else

to do. I have to repay you for all your goodness to I and he, and if

a live woman can do it, I will. Courage, Margaret, courage! set your

wit to work, and prove yourself a match for the wily old worldling."



she pace to and fro in her excitement, and Mr. Hart wait with

gnawing impatience for an explanation. she give it he presently.



"listen. this girl for whom your old friend's son entertain some sort

of a fancy----"



"yes, yes, Margaret."



"and who be far below he in station, and in every way unworthy of

him----"



"yes, yes; go on."



"be your daughter Lucy. be our darling girl Lucy, whose heart have be

very nearly broken because she fear her lover have desert she."









CHAPTER X.



the theory of FRIENDSHIP.





Margaret be not prepare for the manner in which her word be

receive by Mr. Hart. she think he would have be dismay and

stagger at the disclosure, and she be ready to comfort he, and

instil courage into he. but the radiant face that meet her eye

astonish she.



"why then," cry Mr. Hart, with bright look and in a blithe tone,

"all be well--all be well! if your news be true----"



"it be true," she say, in calm wonderment; "they be together now. I

come to the door to keep guard, so that no one should disturb they."



"then I be the happy man and the happy father in Christendom!

why, Margaret, if I have be ask which man in all the wide world I

should wish my daughter to marry, I should select the very man who have

win her heart! God bless they! now, indeed, my mind be at rest, and I

care not what happen to I. my business with the world be over. all

be well with Lucy. we shall see the rose on her cheek again, my

dear--we shall! kiss I, Margaret, and wish I joy."



she keep he back with her hand, and in her eye dwelt a look in which

pity and admiration be equally blend.



"it be my turn now," she say, "to ask for an explanation."



"an explanation of what, my dear? be not everything as clear as the

a good world! thank God for it, and for the happiness this day have

"it would be ungenerous to pretend to misunderstand you," say

"think!" he exclaim, in a reproachful tone. "Nay, be I not sure that

and be the most faithful of friend--who, if circumstance have not

console, encourage each other. there be no envy in our friendship,

and no selfish feeling mar it. how often in my wandering have I

thought of he? how often have I live the old day over again, and

recall the memory of the happy time we spend together? Margaret,

I think that even love pale before the beauty of a faithful

friendship. there be something holy in it; it be a pure sentiment, fit

for the heart of angel. you cannot conceive what comfort and

consolation the mere memory of the friendship between I and Richard

Weston have bring to I; it have brighten hour which otherwise

would have be very dark. and now, when we be old man, and, after so

long a parting, be so strangely reunite, our child fall in love

with each other! one might almost say it be the reward of

faithfulness."



so speak this old man, whom the world's trial and disappointment have

be unable to sour. and Margaret felt humble and abash as she

heart.

and woman be all like you, how much well and happy the world

you believe, I doubt. your heart be like a bank of sweet moss where

fresh flower be always grow; mine be a heart of flint. dear

friend, I love you more every day that I know you."



"pleasant word to hear, dear child, but you shall not do yourself an

injustice. I will not have you speak in such term of yourself. you

notwithstanding your great sorrow. all be clear sailing before we now.

Lucy and Gerald will marry. you will go to the Silver Flagon, and take

"how happy I should be if thing turn out in that way!" exclaim

"but why, my dear, why?"



"I will not be question; I want to make sure; the stake be a serious

"you mistrust he, Margaret; I can see that clearly; but you be

mistaken in he."



"I fervently hope I may be. at all event, I have make up my mind to

be obey in this matter. let thing work their way naturally."



"but if Gerald or his father speak to I about Lucy?"



"that will alter the case entirely; then you will act accord to

your judgment."



it require, however, a great deal of coax from Margaret before Mr.

Hart would agree to her stipulation. but in the end she have her way,

as most woman have when they be resolve upon it.



later in the day, Margaret say to Mr. Weston:



"you do not know, I suppose, that we meet an old friend almost on the

first day of our arrival in Plymouth."



"no," he reply, "I have not hear of it."



"we do; and Mr. Hart have business with he every night for two or

every evening. that be a pity, ben't it?"



"yes," say Mr. Weston, "but your presence will be some compensation."



"that be a very gallant speech. upon my word, I think only old

gentleman know how to pay a graceful compliment to a lady."



in this way she tickle Mr. Weston's vanity, and contrive to account

"you must love she, Gerald. she be my dear friend."



"do you hear, sir!" cry Margaret merrily; "you be to love I."



"it will not be difficult to do that," he reply, "after what Lucy

have tell I about you. but how wonderful all this be! I have not yet

Lucy smile an assent, and Margaret, take Gerald's arm, bid he

"certainly not, if you wish it," he reply, wonder more and more.



she pause for a moment, to master the emotion she experience at the

"don't think my question strange," she say, "you will soon

understand they. you have be to college?"



"yes."



"at Cambridge?"



"yes."



"you have friend there?"



"yes."



"among those friend be there one who leave suddenly----"



he catch her hand. "of whom do you speak? I have a friend who go

from we suddenly--a friend whom I love more than all other."

"Philip Rowe--good heaven! what have I say?"



he catch her sink form, and, amidst her tear and grief at the

"_be_ your husband!" he echo.



"he be dead--my darling, your friend, be dead! keep close to I; I

they be both too affected to speak for several minute, and then

"Nay, it be you that must unriddle it," say Margaret.



he count the note; they amount to a large sum, four hundred

she take his hand and kiss it; then she think of Lucy.



"and now I want to speak to you about Lucy," she say. "if your father

know that it be the daughter of his old friend you love, would he

give his consent to your engagement?"



the word in which he answer she be a sufficient confirmation of

her fear.



"I can marry without my father's consent."



the voice of Mr. Weston himself, who have approach they unseen,

"ah! you have make the acquaintance of this big boy of mine," say the

most desperate deceiver in the world. see how the rascal blush!"

I it be of no use, I have well employ my time more profitably."



and she take the old gentleman's arm, and straightway commence to

flirt with he in the most outrageous manner.









CHAPTER XI.



a peep into BLUEBEARD'S room.





thank to Margaret's tact, everything go on smoothly for a little

while. no person but herself know how hard she work during this

time. she be for ever on the alert, and she manage so skilfully that

Mr. Weston do not even suspect that Gerald and Lucy be lover.

these young person would have betray themselves a dozen time a day

to Gerald's father have it not be for Margaret's vigilance: she take

the old gentleman in hand, as she term it, and entertain he so

admirably that he find real pleasure in her society. she afterwards

declare that she have never play so difficult a part, and have never

play any part half so well. but Margaret, as we know, have a great

idea of her own capability.



with womanly cunning, she sound Mr. Weston to the very bottom of his

nature, and she be compel to admit to herself that there be not

the slight probability of his ever, with his eye open, give his

consent to Gerald's union with a girl who have neither wealth nor

position. he have set his mind upon a certain worldly position for his

Gerald be to marry money, be to enter Parliament, and to make a name

in society. the old gentleman respect nothing but position; he feel

a glow of pride when people touch their hat to he in the street,

and without a suspicion that this mark of outward respect be pay to

live for and worth work for. but notwithstanding that he be

emphatically a purse-proud man, and that when he sit upon the bench as

a magistrate his bosom swell with false pride, he have one estimable

quality, which well man than he often do not possess. he be a man

of his word, and have never be know to depart from it. what he

pledge himself to, he perform. his promise be well than any

manage, insidiously or by straightforward dealing, to induce he to

sanction such an engagement, she believe she could compel he to

stand by his word. but she see no way to arrive at so desirable a

consummation.



every day she confess to herself that her task be become more

"my dear," she say, nod her head violently to herself in the

and then she think she would go and consult her mother's old lover,

"we shall be quite dull without you," she say.



she have so ingratiate herself into the old gentleman's good grace

"what business, Maggy?" ask Lucy.



"I be go shop," reply Margaret, with face of most unblushing

innocence.



"oh! I'll come with you," cry Lucy eagerly.



(I take the opportunity of parenthetically state my belief that

woman like "shopping," even well than love-making.)



"I don't want you, my pet," say Margaret demurely; "I be go to

meet my beau, and two be company, you know."



away she post to Mr. Lewis Nathan, who welcome she right gladly.



"I be afraid I be go to lose you, my dear," he say; "I think

you have forget I."



"I never forget a friend," reply Margaret; "I be like my poor

mother, Mr. Nathan. do she ever forget you?"



she chatter about odd thing for a few minute before she come to

sell the man a coat and a Waistcoat for half as much again as Mr..

Nathan would have obtain for they; true, she sweeten the article

and entrance. Mr. Nathan look on with undisguised admiration.



"what a saleswoman you would have make!" he exclaim, raise his

"the fact be, Mr. Nathan," say Margaret, with brazen audacity, "I be

wheedle the man." she sigh pensively and add, "but I be a fool

with it all. I can sell a coat, but I cann't serve my dearest friend.

oh, that I be a man and have the brain of a man!"



with a humorous look Mr. Lewis Nathan place his hand to his head.



"here be a man's head," say he, "and a man's brain, very much at

your service, my dear."



"come along, then," she cry. "it be hard if you and I cann't win when

we go into partnership. what do you say, now? _shall_ we become

"my dear," say the old rascal, "I should like to take you as a

"it be a good job for I," say Margaret archly, "that you be not

thirty year young. as it be I have almost lose my heart to you."



this incorrigible creature could no more help flirt than she could

help talk--and she have a woman's tongue to do the latter.



bind he over to secrecy, she tell he the whole story; he listen

"as I be do my hair this morning," say Margaret in conclusion,

"I wish I have be behind you, my dear," interrupt Mr. Nathan.



"be quiet, Lothario! as I look into the glass this morning I say to

"I should like to see those crow's foot."



"well, look at they;" and she place her face close to his.



Mr. Nathan gaze into her sparkling eye, which flash their

bright glance at he, and then laugh at her outright.



"you're a barbarian," cry Margaret.



"you have well call I an unbelieve Jew at once," say Mr. Nathan

she rise and make he a mocking curtsey.



"thank you, I be quite content as I be. but let we be serious. say

"it be an old story," he observe; "love against money. here be money;

"I see. your hand with love in it be near to heaven; your hand with

"and love always win," she say vivaciously. "yes," rejoin Mr.

"I willn't have never!" she cry impetuously. "it do sometimes win,

even in this sordid world. and if it never have do so before, it must

worldly, hard-hearted old man, I would as lief have be bear without

brain as with they!"



"hush, hush, my dear. let I think a bit."



he ponder for a little while.



"there be a mathematician--what be his name?--ah! Archimedes--who

say he would move the world if he could find a crevice for his lever.

my dear, we have neither lever nor crevice. we must get the lever

first, and find the crevice. now where do this old gentleman keep

she stare at he in amazement. "his skeleton!" she exclaim.



"his skeleton, my dear; that's what we want. he keep it somewhere.

I've get mine, and I keep it where no eye but my own can see it. we've

all get one. if we could get hold of this old gentleman's we might do

something. it be in his house, depend upon it."



"if it be, I've not hear of it. oh! yes," she cry excitedly,

the house. Mr. Hart tell I of it."



Mr. Nathan chuckle. "what be in that room, Margaret?"



"how should I know? I have never be in it."



he give she a reproachful look.



"if you haven't tell I so yourself I should not have believe it. a

Bluebeard's room in the house and you've never see it a clever woman

like you! you'll tell I next, I shouldn't wonder, that you have never

peep through the keyhole."



"I do tell you so; I never have peep through the keyhole."



it be evident from Mr. Nathan's tone that Margaret have fall several

degree in his estimation.



"my dear," he say, "that room may contain the very thing we want--the

lever."



"but suppose he keep it lock up?"



"then lock, bolt, and bar must fly asunder." Mr. Nathan sing these

word in a fine bass voice, and rise with a brisk air say, "you

"I must first get you into the house."



"I be come with you now. the old gentleman be away, you say; no time

"never."



"I will take long odd it be unlocked. come along, my dear."



at another time Margaret might have have scruple, but her interest in

"now, my dear," say Mr. Nathan, "take I to the room. of course you

"not for a certainty," reply Margaret, "but I suspect."



she lead Mr. Nathan to a door at the end of a passage, the last room

"I tell you so," say Mr. Nathan, with a quiet chuckle. "Sister Ann,

Sister Ann, do you see any one come?"

"Nonsense, my dear, nonsense; we shalln't have our head cut off."

turn to the wall.



"to preserve they from the dust, I should say," observe Mr. Nathan;

"one--two--three--thirteen of 'em, my dear. we'll have a peep at they

at all event."



they be all portrait, and be all paint by the same hand. Mr.

Nathan seem to find some cause for curiosity in this circumstance.

one of the portrait, Margaret say, be like Mr. Weston when he be a

young man.



"take a good many year ago," say Mr. Nathan, place the picture

"what be to be do now?" ask Margaret in despair.



"well, my dear, it's a puzzle. but we'll try and work it out. we must

they leave Bluebeard's room in not a very hopeful frame of mind.









CHAPTER XII.



MR. HART DECLARES that HONESTY have die out of

the world.



event, however, be bring to a climax somewhat suddenly, without

Margaret's intervention. on the day follow the peep into

Bluebeard's room Mr. Weston announce that he intend give an

evening party, and that he have already invite his friend. the party

would take the form of an early dance.



"really early," say Mr. Weston, "for I don't like late hour. they

have all promise to be here by half-past eight o'clock."



he tell Gerald privately that Miss Forester and her family would be

among the guest. Miss Forester be the young lady whom he wish his

son to marry, and he request Gerald to pay her particular attention.

the young fellow listen in silence.



"you will not leave we this evening," say Mr. Weston to Mr. Hart.



but Mr. Hart be compel to go to the theatre. it happen, however,

a word as to Mr. Majendie. a bag of clothe stuff with money.

Mr. Weston be hurt in a very tender point; truly it be a most

his friend be false to he, unworthy of he.

Part the Third.



the dinner of THIRTEEN.









CHAPTER I.



STRANGE preparation for the dinner.





in one of the prettiest nook in Devonshire, the garden of England,

where the hedge and hill-slope be fill with apple-tree, stand,

where have stand beyond the memory of live man, the Silver Flagon, an

old-fashioned, delightful hotel, irregular in shape, as all pleasant

hostelry should be, and so embellish with quaint turret and

gable and mullione window, as to make it appear more like the

retreat of a wealthy gentleman than a house of public entertainment.

the principal entrance stand fully thirty yard away from the public

road or path, and to reach it you have to pass through an antique

wooden gate, and a carefully-attend garden, as delightfully

irregular as the house to which it be attach. there be not a square

room in the entire establishment, and although from time to time

addition have be make to it in the shape of a wing here and a wing

there, modern innovation and modern idea of comfort have not be

allow to spoil its character. imbed in the midst of its own

ground, in the rich soil of which flower and fruit-tree be

abundant and beautifully luxuriant, the Silver Flagon be a standing

reproach to those Tower of Babel hotel, which it be the fashion now

to build.



fortunately for those to whom it be know, and who enjoy and

appreciate its comfort, its proprietor, Gideon Rowe, be, in his

idea, as old-fashioned as his hotel. the Silver Flagon have be in

the family of the Rowes for many generation, and have be hand down

from father to son for more than a century; and the various member

regard it with so much pride and affection that it have grow to be

look upon more in the light of an heir-loom than a speculation.

Gideon Rowe, at sixty-five year of age, be a pleasant,

even-temper, good-look gentleman, straight as an arrow, with a

clear eye and a wholesome colour in his face--catch, mayhap, from

some of his famous apple--and with every probability of twenty more

good year before he. he be a man of independent property, and he

carry on the business of the Silver Flagon as much for pleasure and

occupation as for profit. it be probably for this reason that the

majority of those who frequent it be gentleman, who be fond of

drink their old ale and cider, and sometimes their wine, out of the

old-fashioned silver flagon, which it be the whim of Gideon Rowe's

great-grandfather to have make, and of which there be no few than

one hundred and twenty in the hotel.



it be seldom that any sign of bustle be to be notice in the

Silver Flagon; but on a certain Wednesday in the middle of

August--some few week after the occurrence of the incident

heretofore narrate--there be sign of unusual activity in the low

story of the hotel. the cook be busy, and and there be much

hurrying to and fro; it be evident that there be a large number of

attendant than usual in the hotel, and that something important be

go on. the principal room of the Silver Flagon, which be in shape

of an irregular oblong, and sufficiently commodious to accommodate a

large number of guest, be situate on the ground-floor, and at six

o'clock on the evening of this Wednesday in August present an

appearance which it be necessary to describe.



the table be lay for a distinguished dinner-party. that it be to be

a dinner of the good kind be evident from the furnishing of the

table, which comprise the fine plate of the Silver Flagon and a

brilliant display of glass. a number of attendant, dress in court

suit of black, be perfect the detail, under the direction of

their chief, before the arrival of the guest.



although it be still daylight the candle in the handsome candelabra

be already light, the effect of which be not only to darken the

room, but to throw corner almost completely into shade. picture hung

upon the wall--not landscape, nor scene of rural or domestic life:

the subject be neither historical nor allegorical; every picture

be a portrait. count they, you would find that there be exactly

thirteen portrait, all of the same size and all handsomely and

uniformly frame. that they be paint by one hand be not to be

doubt, and be so, and be of a uniform size and uniformly

frame, it might reasonably have be suppose that they represent

member of the same family; but it be clear that this be not the

case. with here and there an exception, they bear no likeness to each

other, and in some instance the contrast in the face and general

character of the individual, as indicate by outline and expression,

be very remarkable. the original be of various age, from eighteen

or nineteen to sixty mayhap. cast your eye around the wall, you

would instinctively have pause at the picture of a stern-look man,

the line in whose face speak of invincible determination; his dress

be pretentiously plain and sombre; one hand, which grasp the back

of a chair, grasp it so firmly that the vein be see to stand

out; his lip be set, and there be a frown in his eye. whether by

accident or design, his picture be so hang as to cause his cruel eye

to bear directly on two face of a very opposite character from his.

they be the portrait of a young lady and a young gentleman--she

probably not more than nineteen year of age, he some three or four

year old. the girl be in the full flush of youthful beauty, a rose

whose leave be open to the sunlight of life, delicately nurture

evidently, and whose face be almost spiritualise from its extreme

sensitiveness. in this respect the young man, who be also handsome

and well-form, singularly resemble she, and yet there be no

likeness between they. these young person be smile on each other.

your eye would also have dwell with interest upon the portrait of a

man about thirty year of age, with a kind and even benevolent face,

fair, and with bright blue eye. then there be the portrait of one

whom you would instantly set down as an old maid, from the precise

and severely-demure fashion of her clothe, from the set of she

poke-bonnet, and from the sharp but not ill-natured expression on her

face. beside she be a portrait of a very different character--that of

a rakish, genial, full-blooded man, with the pleasantest of mouth,

and the merriest of eye, out of which joviality beam; his hat be

set on one side of his head, and between his finger dangle a cane

with a dandy tassel. all these person be attire in the fashion of

a bygone generation.



the room be well supply with choice flower. two fold window

which face the west open upon a veranda-terrace, the step of which
