be comfortably furnish with easy and other chair and convenient

exactly as a quarter past six o'clock be proclaim in thin, silvery

with his eye he make the circuit of the table, and count the chair

"one--two--three--four--five--six--seven--eight--nine--ten--eleven

therefore it be clear that thirteen person be expect to dine.

then he run his eye over the attendant, and count they, from one

name of Steele, Gideon Rowe call he to his side.



"your arrangement seem to be perfect, Mr. Steele."



"I think you will find they so, sir," reply Mr. Steele.



"this be--let I see--the eighth year you have officiate."



"this make the eighth year, sir."



"we have see some change, Mr. Steele."



"we have, sir."



"I know I can depend upon you to carry out the affair with discretion,

whatever happen."



"thank you, sir."



there be the slight tinge of surprise in Mr. Steele's tone, which

"whatever happen. after all, it be an exceedingly simple affair, and

I shall be glad to see everything well and discreetly do. you have

the entire superintendence. even if I wish, I could not undertake

the management, be, as it be, one of they."



this with a glance at the portrait on the wall.



"you shall have no reason to complain, sir."



"the dinner will be serve at seven precisely. there must be no

mistake about that especially. when the clock strike, we will

"it shall be do, sir."



"have the man be instruct in their duty?"



"yes, sir."



but Mr. Rowe deem it necessary to address a few word to they

"Mr. Steele have explain to you what your duty be. you all of you

"we do, sir."



"there be something for you to understand more necessary than the mere

what be require of you be implicit silence and attention. at whatever

occur you will exhibit no wonder or astonishment, but you will

steadily and decorously follow out the instruction give to you by

upon your mind. you understand I, I dare say."



"yes, sir."



"then I need say nothing more to you."



Gideon Rowe do not consider that his manner of address the

attendant, no less than his word, be sufficient to arouse within

they a curiosity which they otherwise would not have feel.



he turn his attention again to Mr. Steele, and ask about the wine.

Mr. Steele point to the iced pail, liberally supply with bottle,

and to other bottle which do not require ice; these be place

as he do so, the silver tongue of the black marble clock proclaim

half-past six.



"be the doorkeeper here?" ask Mr. Rowe.



"he be without, sir."



"let I see he."



Mr. Steele hesitate a moment.



"I have be disappoint in the man I wish to engage for the

service."



"but you have another?" say Mr. Rowe quickly.



"oh! yes."



"and a dependable man?"



"quite dependable, to all appearance, and from his credential."



"that be all that be necessary. his duty be onerous, but not

burdensome. let I see he."



Mr. Steele go out by the door behind the screen, and return with

"your name is----"



"Michael Lee."



"you answer promptly, like a soldier."



"I be not one, sir."



"you be an elderly man--about my own age, I should say. be your

eyesight good?"



"fairly good for my age."



"I ask because in the place where you will stand the light be rather

dim. I must test you."



he look around for a newspaper or other print matter, and find

"be your eye good enough to read this?" Michael Lee change colour,

and his lip tremble as his eye fall upon the writing.



"you can read it?"



"I can read it quite well," reply Michael Lee, and continue, in a

gentle, sad tone, read from the letter: "so now, my dear old dad,

good-bye, and God bless you. with fond love, your affectionate

scapegrace of a son, Philip Rowe."



Gideon Rowe pause before he speak again.



"that be a good credential for your eye."



"the letter be from your son," observe Michael Lee respectfully.



"yes, from my poor boy. write a long time ago. he be dead. thank you

for that mark of your sympathy."



"I also be a father."



"you can understand then the kind of grief that oppress a man when

he lose an only child, whom he love very dearly. but we be

Michael Lee make an effort to shake off his sad humour, and answer

"so that some good come to one for be old. though I should rather

say that I should be all the well for be a little young. I

should have no objection to my ripen time come over again. but

time that ripen we, wither we; time that wither we, kill we."



"ah, well," say Gideon Rowe, with reflective nod, and gaze in

surprise at Michael Lee, "we must drop away and make room for other."

he cast a strangely-serious look at the thirteen chair arrange round

the table. "you be a superior man, I perceive."



still strive to rally his spirit, Michael Lee say:



"one other man besides yourself, sir, have sometimes think so."



"any one whom I know?"



"yes, sir; you know he slightly."



"who may he be?"



"I, myself."



Gideon Rowe smile.



"Mr. Steele do well to select you. now pay careful heed to what

I be about to say. your duty to-night be not heavy. you be to

stand as doorkeeper, and all you have to do be to act strictly in

accordance with the instruction I give you. your position will be

there"--pointing to the door at the north end of the room, which lead

on to the veranda. "you will stand outside that door, and admit only

those who establish their right to enter. and only those have the

right of entrance whose name be write on this paper."



Michael Lee receive the paper from Gideon Rowe, and read the name

aloud:





     Reuben Thorne.

     James Blanchard.

     Henry Holmes.

     Rachel Holmes.

     Thomas Chatterton.

     Ephraim Goldberg.

     Dinah Dim.

     Stephen Viner.

     Caroline Miller.

     Edward Blair.

     Clarence Coveney.

     Frederick Fairfax.

     Richard Weston.





"you will keep the paper as a guide," say Gideon Rowe, over whose

countenance shade of vary expression have pass as the name be

read, the most noticeable be one of sad pity at the name of

Caroline Miller. "not another person but those whose name be set

down there must be allow to pass in under any pretence. but you may

still be liable to make a mistake, as you have never see these lady

and gentleman. that contingency be provide for; examine this."



he place in the hand of Michael Lee a small piece of ivory in the

shape of a heart. Michael Lee examine it with curiosity. Gideon Rowe

continue:



"you will neither admit nor announce any lady or gentleman who do

not produce a heart shape like this in ivory, with his or her name

write upon it in red letter."



"that be lucky," observe Michael Lee.



"what be lucky?"



Michael Lee quickly answer: "my grandmother wear an ivory charm,

with sign upon it, which be give to she by a gipsy woman; she have a

superstitious regard for it."



Gideon Rowe consider for a few moment whether Michael Lee's word

be intend to be take in jest or earnest, but he could not resolve

the point.



"very well," he say, "now you can go to your post. here be a seat,

you see. you may find your work somewhat dull, but you will contrive

not to fall asleep."



"when all the person," say Michael Lee, "whose name be set down

here have arrive, will it be necessary for I to keep to my post?"



"no," reply Gideon Rowe, with another strange look; "when all the

person whose name be on that paper have arrive, your duty be at

an end."









CHAPTER II.



ARRIVAL of but one guest AT a dinner for THIRTEEN.





leave Michael Lee at his post outside the door, Gideon Rowe go to

the fold window, and draw the curtain over they. he linger at

the window to inhale the faint perfume of lavender which the breeze

bring into the room.



"summer be die," he murmur.



beautiful as be the evening, there be something inexpressibly sad in

the appearance of this room, with its dim light, and the black

clothing of the attendant, who move about like shadow.



"Mr. Steele," say Gideon Rowe, "you understand that the first guest

who arrive will preside at the head of the table. I will wait upon

he myself."



"as heretofore, sir?"



"as heretofore."



all the arrangement be complete, the attendant stand in silence

behind the chair, form a black hedge around the table. Gideon Rowe

glance anxiously at the clock. the hand indicate eighteen minute

to seven. that he be singularly and powerfully agitated be evident,

but he control his excitement by a strong effort. another minute

pass and another. the clock strike three-quarter past six, step

be hear on the veranda, and almost immediately afterwards Michael

Lee open the door by which he be station, and advance a step,

call out:



"Mr. Richard Weston."



the sound of Michael Lee's voice afford relief to every person in

the room, for all be begin to be oppress by the gloom and

silence which prevail. Mr. Weston, as he enter, glance before he

with a shrink, air, and, grasp Gideon Rowe's hand firmly, as

though he derive comfort from the contact, shade his eye with his

not see in the uncertain light.



"only the servant," observe Mr. Rowe, answer the look; "I be glad

to welcome you."



"thank you, Mr. Rowe, thank you," say Mr. Weston. "I be the first

then?"



"you be the first," reply Mr. Rowe gravely.



"I be almost ashamed to confess it," say Mr. Weston, "though I don't

know why I _should_ be ashamed to confess it to you, for we be old

crony, eh, Rowe? old crony--but before I enter the room, and

indeed for many day past, I have have a fearful and unreasonable fancy

that, that----"



Gideon Rowe, with a serious smile, supply the word which Mr. Weston

be at a lose to utter. "that some one might have be before you, and

deprive you of your position at the head of the table."



"it be so, I assure you," assent Mr. Weston; "but I have be much

upset lately--cross and thwart on all side, and where I have the

good right to expect obedience."



"I have hear something--rumour be many-tongue, you know."



"yes, yes; and tell lie, and invent, and make black white. I can

speak to you as an old friend. tell I what you have hear."



"it be an impertinence for people to speak of these thing, for they

be family matter; and, indeed, it be difficult to bring vague

rumour into definite word. briefly as I understand it, Gerald----"



"my son--yes."



--"Refuses to marry the lady you have choose for he, love another

lady, and having pledge himself to she. that much have reach my

understanding, through the rumour I have hear. be it true? have

Gerald really pledge himself to a lady of whom you disapprove, and

do he really love she?"



"love she! no. it be a fancy which will be go in a few week. the

boy don't know his own mind."



"that be not the impression I have form of Gerald. he be somewhat

obstinate in his like and dislike. and he really have pledge himself

to this lady, and she really be a lady?"



"she be the daughter of an old friend of mine," reply Mr. Weston,

with nervous hesitation; "of an old friend who have inflict great

pain upon I. she be a good girl--a good girl, I do believe--but not

the wife for Gerald."



"why not? because she be poor?"



"ah! you have hear, then. can you not see that Gerald have a position

to maintain, and there be duty which society exact from we?

class must be keep apart. but do not speak any far of this now;

it be not the time. on the anniversary of this night my mind be

occupy by but one subject." he glance at the table. "it might be

but yesterday! the same old silver--the same old service--and some of

the same old wine, eh, Mr. Rowe? the same old wine."



"the same, Mr. Weston: there be but little of it leave. but it will

last our time, and then will come new wine, new fashion, new man and

woman, new everything, to grow old as we have grow old, and to make

way for other fashion and other man and woman, as our fashion and

ourselves be make way for they."



"there be some thing that do not seem to change," say Mr. Weston,

look towards the clock, and feel in his pocket. "the same old

clock, too. but I cannot see the hand. ah, here they be!" he have

be search his pocket for his spectacle, and he now produce the

case. "look at my eye now, you wouldn't think that I be grow

more short-sighted every day, eh, Mr. Rowe?"



"your eye be as bright as they be thirty year ago."



"so they may appear, but they deceive I--as everything else do.

bless my soul! they be go!"



he refer to his spectacle; his spectacle-case be empty.



"shall I send for they?" ask Gideon Rowe.



"no, no; they would not be find, perhaps. I must do without my eye

to-night. the clock be right, eh? what do it mark now?"



"thirteen minute to seven."



"thank you. as I be say, there be some thing that do not change.

the Silver Flagon, for instance--there be no change in that."



"there be no change in it from my first remembrance of it. I should

like it never to change. I use to wish that it might be carry on in

exactly the same way, and in the same old fashion, as it have be

carry on during this last hundred year. but it be in the nature of

thing to change, and my wish will not be fulfil. have other thing

turn out as I hope, my desire would almost certainly have be

frustrate by the new scheme for the branch railway that be be

talk about. I be tell that its course be design immediately in the

rear of the garden." he look regretfully towards the folding

fancy one's self almost out of the world here; but if the railway

scheme be carry out, good-bye to the charm of perfect peacefulness

which rest upon the Silver Flagon. good-bye, perhaps, to the Silver

Flagon itself. the thought hurt I, but not as much as it would have

do have my dear boy be alive."



"Rowe!" exclaim Mr. Weston, in a sympathising, wonder tone, "you

have have news of Philip, then?"



"he be dead, poor lad! you know how I love the boy, and how my heart

be bind up in he. I cherish the hope that, when his wild fit be

over, he would come and take my place here. the dear lad be work

to bring home a hatful of money to repay I for what I have do for

he. as though I need repay! Shame drive he away, and keep he

away while he be poor. he do not know his father's heart."



"how do the news come?" ask Mr. Weston softly.



"his wife bring it--a dear good girl. she be in the house now, and

will remain here as my daughter. you shall see she in good time, and

hear the sad story from her own lip. I think the news would have

kill I but for she."



"my Gerald and your Philip be good friend," murmur Mr. Weston.

"Gerald will grieve, indeed, when he hear the news."



"life be full of disappointment, full of change. man propose, God

dispose. I hope that I should die with my Philip by my bedside in

this peaceful spot, and he die at the other end of the world, sixteen

thousand mile away, while I be still a hale old man. I have the

comfort of know that his heart be beat with love for I--the

dear lad!" he pause for a moment. "notwithstanding this grief, I

still have something to be grateful for, and I bow with submission to

the Divine will. I have a new daughter, such a girl as I would have

choose for he, and mayhap a great blessing will be bestow upon I

in the course of a couple of month, and my Philip may live again in

his son. and have I not still the dear old Silver Flagon? I look upon

it almost as part of my own flesh and blood. my life be wed to it

by sweet and solemn memory. why, I remember these old flagon when I

could scarcely toddle! I use to look at my face in they when I be a

boy; there be one with a long dent in it--here it be now on the

sideboard--which seem to split my face in two." he gaze wistfully

into its polished surface. "it ben't the same face as it be then."



"what do the clock mark now?"



"eight minute to seven."



"how slowly the time pass! the moment be clog with lead."



"it be only the year that fly," say Gideon Rowe. "we watch the

minute and the day, and the year slip by without our heed they.

but all at once we wake to the fact, and a sudden shock come upon we.

truly 'we be such stuff as dream be make of, and our little life be

round with a sleep.'"



there be nothing singular in the perfect familiarity that exist

between the speaker. Gideon Rowe come of an old family (though if he

have come from a new family--a phrase I cannot quite understand--it

would have be all the same) who have acquire their money honestly,

and he have live a blameless life. such a man be the equal of a king.

it be to be especially note that the present conversation

be carry on with a careful avoidance--by Mr. Weston most

certainly--of a subject which must have be uppermost in their mind,

and that directly one pause, the other take up the cue, as though

they be desirous that not a moment should pass in silence. another

thing to be note be, that frequently in the middle of a sentence,

Mr. Weston--whether he or his companion be speak--turn his head

over his shoulder toward the door by which Michael Lee be station,

with a timid, nervous, frightened look, as if expect to see an

apparition there. still more conspicuous be his studied avoidance of

the picture that be hang on the wall. if in an unwitting moment

he happen to raise his eye towards the portrait, he turn they

away again with visible agitation. the attendant in the room

preserve silence while their superior be converse. they stand in

their place like statue.



"and we fret ourselves so unwisely," continue Mr. Rowe, with

something of a wary look towards Mr. Weston. "we torture ourselves so

unnecessarily. instead of enjoy the opportunity which good

fortune have place in our hand, we bring unhappiness upon ourselves

by set our mind upon the accomplishment of certain wish which

we deem to be good, notwithstanding that they distinctly clash with

the hope of those who be dear to we. we forget that life be

short. let I give you a bit of my philosophy, and apply it to

ourselves. here we stand, having grow from youth to manhood, from

manhood to old age, march from our very cradle into our grave.

the change that come naturally upon we we bear, if we be wise, with

patience and resignation; with hope, also, that carry we in our

life to the contemplation of other sphere beyond the grave. there be

a wonderful amount of goodness and sweetness in life, with all its sad

change. what good reward we--what bring we the most pleasure and

satisfaction--be to enjoy this good, in so far as it affect ourselves

and other, and to make the very good use of it which lie in our

power. you cannot deny that this be a sensible philosophy."



"it sound so."



"it be not only a sensible, it be a wise philosophy. let I apply it.

say that I have a child whom I love"--the memory of his Philip bring

a touching sadness into his tone--"say that this blessing, which I

have unhappily lose, be mine. if by any action of mine I can make that

child happy, it be surely good and wise in I to do so, and add to my

enjoyment of life. say that this child, having grow to manhood, with

a man's intelligence and a man's hope, have set his heart upon a

certain thing--say, plainly, that he love a girl who be both virtuous

and good, whom he wish to make his wife, and that I constitute it my

business to thwart he--it be surely unwise in itself, if only in the

fact that it bring discomfort to I, that it fill my day with

uneasiness, and make my home unhappy. now, this be a selfish view,

but it be one which occur to I by way of illustration."



"but say, for the sake of argument," say Mr. Weston, somewhat

uneasily, "only for the sake of argument, mind----"



"very well, for the sake of argument."



"that this child's fancy be a foolish one, and unwise in every

sense."



"I don't admit that; but we be only argue. pray proceed."



"and that you, his father, see another and a well way of bring

happiness into his life."



"who judge that my way be the well way?" demand Mr. Rowe.



"Yourself."



Mr. Rowe shake his head, and take a pair of spectacle from his

pocket, ask Mr. Weston to use they. Mr. Weston put they on gladly,

but they do not suit his sight; all be dim before he. he return

the spectacle to Mr. Rowe.



"I cannot see through they," he say.



"nonsense, nonsense," reply Mr. Rowe; "you be mistaken. you can."



"I tell you I cannot."



"yet that be just what you insist other can do. you insist that they

can see through _your_ spectacle."



"I say nonsense, nonsense to you! I understand your trick, but it do

not apply in this case. I say that in the difference of opinion

between you and your son which you have speak of you be the well

judge. you be the old of the two by forty year. you know the

world; you have experience its trial, its temptation, its

disappointment; you have see its folly, its delusion. therefore

you have a perfect right to say to your son, 'my boy, you be wrong!

you must conquer your idea--your fancy. be patient, and time will show

you its folly; and one day you will thank I for oppose your

wish.' why," exclaim Mr. Weston, raise his voice slightly in his

excitement, "do you not love your son?"



"that it be not to be doubt."



"and what you do in this matter, be it not for his good?"



"ah, my friend, my friend! I may think so, in my obstinacy, but it be

I who be wrong. let we speak plainly. you know it be of your Gerald we

be speaking----"



"of course I know it."



"what more can you desire than his happiness? the girl he love, and

have pledge himself to, be poor, it be true; but she be a lady, and be

in every way worthy of he. why embitter your life and his by stand

"one moment, Mr. Rowe," interrupt Mr. Weston; "how do you know all

this? have you see the girl?"



"I have."



"and her father, have you see he?"



"no, but I hope soon to do so. from what I have hear, he be a man

whom it would be a proud privilege to call friend."



Mr. Weston make a movement of uneasiness.



"the subject annoy I," he say, "let we cease discuss it."



"we have no time to continue it," say Gideon Rowe, glance at the

clock, "or, despite your wish, I should not allow it to drop. we

ourselves be young once, and look at thing with different eye

from those with which we view they now."



"how near to the time be it?"



"but one minute."



during this minute there be silence in the room. Michael Lee's voice

be not hear. Mr. Weston move slowly to the head of the table. the

attendant stand in silence behind the empty chair. presently the

clock strike the hour of seven. as the sound of the last stroke be

die away, Gideon Rowe say to Mr. Steele:



"serve the dinner."



Mr. Richard Weston be the only guest.









CHAPTER III.



ARRIVAL of unexpected guest.





stand behind the twelve empty chair, the attendant perform

their duty with as much ceremony as could have be expect from

they have they be wait on the most exacting and punctilious

guest; but it be not difficult to see that they do not like the

service in which they be engage. from time to time they gaze

furtively at each other, and accord to the susceptibility of their

temperament, be more or less disturb by the strangeness of the

scene. there be something so ghostlike in this silent dinner, that

when the attendant move they step lightly, as though they be

fearful of raise the dead. the only person who be not dismay at

the sight of the empty chair be Mr. Weston, Mr. Steele, and the

proprietor of the Silver Flagon. indeed, that the chair be empty

appear to afford satisfaction to at least one of the party--Mr.

Weston.



"what have become of your unreasonable fancy?" ask Mr. Rowe.



"Gone, thank God!" reply Mr. Weston, with a sigh of relief, drain

his glass. "but I have it very strong upon I. we cannot help these

superstitious feeling, and in my case there be a distinct cause for

they, in word once utter by Reuben Thorne."



"poor Reuben! he be the merry soul I ever meet."



"a careless, ne'er-do-well!" exclaim Mr. Weston.



"no man's enemy but his own," add Mr. Rowe quickly. "the merry

with we. what word do you refer to?"



"it be on the fourth anniversary of this day, and all the thirteen

be present. death have not take one of our party. I be sit next

to Reuben, and the conversation be loud and jovial. all be in high

spirit with the exception of three--Caroline Miller, Edward Blair,

the dead, I could find it in my mind to couple the name of Stephen

"you couple his memory with bitter thought. why spare the word? he

be a cruel man, with an unfeele heart."



"hush! hush! he have go where he will be judge."



"and where," say Mr. Rowe, in no way soften, "the spirit of

Caroline and Edward rise in judgment against he. I be glad you feel

as I do toward the man who destroy the happiness of two young

person whose only fault be that they love each other too well."



"you have make I," say Mr. Weston, with a heighten colour, "wander

from my theme."



"you wander from it yourself," retort Mr. Rowe, "by mention the

name of Stephen Viner."



"be it not," say Mr. Weston, with mark of agitation in his face,

"I have a design to speak plainly. if we can learn a lesson from the

dead which it would be good to learn, it be bad than folly to reject

it. the parallel be a strange one. Caroline Miller and Edward Blair

be not the only young lover who have be parted----"



"Stop, Rowe," interrupt Mr. Weston, in a tone of suppress passion.

"I desire that you will not continue the subject. it be unkind, cruel

of you, and the conclusion you draw do I great injustice."



he again empty his glass, and the next few moment be pass in

silence.



"I beg your pardon," then say Mr. Rowe, more gently; "I be betray

out of myself. you be speak of Reuben Thorne."



"all at the table be converse loudly together," say Mr. Weston,

continue his reminiscence with visible effort: it be evident that

be that you say?' 'I say,' he reply, that if I happen not to be

and 'I will!' I raise my hand for silence. 'it be,' I say, 'too

grave a subject to jest upon.' but Reuben be not to be divert from

his light humour. 'I have promise,' he say; and there be an end of

the matter. little do I think, when those word be exchange, that

I should be the last survivor, and that Reuben Thorne's promise would

make such an impression upon I."



Mr. Weston eat very little, but he drink a great deal of wine, and

mantle the face of a brunette; from that to purple, fringe by

dark blue; thence by delicate and sad tint, melt one in the

other, into quieter shade, until the fiery sky grow calm, and

herald a lovely and peaceful night. as daylight disappear,

additional candle be light, and the room would have present a

cheerful aspect but for the empty chair and the serious face of the

attendant. then, for the first time, Mr. Weston purposely raise his

eye to the portrait which hang upon the wall.



"ah, I!" he sigh. "and this be all that remain of they--paint

canvas! I cannot distinguish their face without my spectacle, but I

can see they in my mind's eye. all dead, all dead, but ourselves!"



"Few live to our age," remark Mr. Rowe.



"how many--how many? let I see. one--two--three; no more. you be

right when you say 'tis only the year that fly. and some die very

young. whether be it for good or ill, Rowe, that we, stranger to one

another, should have be bring together by one unknown to all of

"it can scarcely have be for good," reply Mr. Rowe. "look back,

as we can look back, upon the life of those to whom the money be

bring happiness? to some it bring a curse. too well do we know the

story of those two hapless one, Caroline and Edward, whom it drive to

an early grave. leave to the absolute guardianship of a man whose heart

be stone, those orphan meet and love. in all human calculation, no

lot in life could be happy than that of these lover would have be

strange spell upon his delicate, susceptible ward. his strong mind and

will dominate her sensitive nature absolutely. he win from her a

solemn promise that she would not wed without his consent. Dinah Dim,

that kindly old maid, tell I that Viner make Caroline swear this upon

first give into the guardianship of this man--what wonder that they

love as they grow to man's and woman's estate? we all know of their

love, and intercede for they, vainly. prayer, entreaty,

remonstrance--all be useless. you yourself be one of the most

earnest in your entreaty, but Stephen Viner turn a deaf ear, and

so arrange that the lover be to be part. Edward be to be send

to India, 'where he would get over his foolish passion,' Stephen Viner

say. of my own knowledge I be aware that Edward want Caroline to

marry he and defy her guardian. but her oath, which she be never

allow to forget, be of too solemn a nature to permit of this; and

besides, she have a clear and painful remembrance of privation endure

by her parent when she be a child, and, know that they have

marry for love against the wish of their friend, she refuse to

bring a similar suffering as her dowry to Edward. you know the sad

end. drive to despair, the young lover drown themselves--at

least, so it be suppose, when their body be find in the river.

you remember the gloom the news cast over our party when we meet, and

the savage look and word which be cast at Stephen Viner. who that

be acquaint with this sad story can doubt that the money leave so

strangely bring a curse to these two innocent young soul?"



by this time it be night. the dessert be now on the table, which

require but guest around it to make a very charming scene. Mr.

Weston have drink a good deal of wine, and be in a feverish, excited

condition. Michael Lee still keep watch outside the door. the only

this latter person be evidently determined not to lose sight of the

"at such a time as this," he say, "it be but natural that our

thought should revert to those who be go. I be think now of my

dead Philip, with reference to worldly thing. do you know, friend,

that I would cheerfully live the rest of my day in poverty if the

sacrifice of my worldly good could bring my son to life?"



"they be the natural feeling of a father," respond Mr. Weston.

"be I in your place, I would surely feel the same."



"and yet how strangely do we regulate our action with reference to

those we love! while they live, we thwart their dearest hope; when

they be go, we be ready to make the extreme sacrifice upon the

altar of our affection. but then it be too late."



he would have proceed far but that a sudden spasm from Mr.

Weston divert his attention. follow the direction of Mr. Weston's

eye, he turn toward the fold window.



"do you hear nothing?" ask Mr. Weston in a low tone.



"no."



"I fancy," murmur Mr. Weston, in explanation, "that I hear a step

upon the veranda."



Mr. Rowe go to the window, and partly draw the curtain aside. the

moon be rise, and the soft light could be see through the opening.



"there be no one there," say Mr. Rowe, return to Mr. Weston's

they live, we beat our breast and reproach ourselves----"



again he be interrupt. Michael Lee, the doorkeeper, enter the

room, and follow Mr. Rowe's last word, come Michael Lee's

"Mr. Reuben Thorne."



Mr. Weston's face grow white as the person announce approach and

bow.



"I be late," say the new-comer, drop into a chair; "but well

late than never, they say."



he pour out a glass of claret, and rise, say, with another bow to

Mr. Weston: "your health;" and again resume his seat.



"be I dream?" ask Mr. Weston, in a low tone of fear, address

himself to Mr. Rowe.



Reuben Thorne hear the word, and before Mr. Rowe could speak,

himself reply:



"no, faith; it be I who have be dream--dream for many year.

Life be a dream; and death!--but we will not speak of that. live and

learn, they say. let we correct the maxim. die and learn, be

infinitely truer, as all man will find. if we could live and unlearn,

it would be well for we. 'tis a conflict, from the cradle to the

grave--heart against head. and head win, the rule be. man would be

happy be it otherwise. well for we to go back, and play at

child over again."



he be so exactly the counterpart of one of the portrait on the wall,

in every detail of dress and personal appearance, that he could not

have be more like have he actually be the live presentment of the

picture. but the portrait be there and the man be there, and the man

look up at the paint likeness of himself with some kind of

satisfaction.



"if my memory serve I," he continue, still address Mr. Weston,

"it be a good old fashion for the chairman to welcome his guest as

they arrive. you have not address to I one word of welcome. at all

event, we will drink wine together."



he raise his glass, and Mr. Weston mechanically raise his. bow to

each other, they empty their glass simultaneously. then Mr. Weston

speak for the first time, in a hush, awe-strike tone.



"I remember the word you utter on the anniversary of our fourth

gathering. I recall they before you enter. you promise to visit

the last of the thirteen who be leave and take wine with he. you

ask if the other would join you; all, or nearly all, promise to do

so." he shudder as he speak.



"the promise will be redeem by our friend," say Reuben Thorne, "as

it be redeem by I. but I have another purpose in come to-night."



"what purpose?"



"a purpose in which I be not the only one engage. other be with I.

you will know more presently. do you see any change in I?"



"none. you be to I the same as when I last say you. not a day

old--not a day." he, also, glance at the portrait for confirmation.



"that be many year ago now. I see a change in you. your hair be

white; you be an old man. perhaps in another year you, too, will have

pass away from among man. it will be well for you if you have sow

no seed of unhappiness, which may grow into life-misery when you

have go. even I, with no human tie, even I, who have no wife or

child, would, if I could, live my time over again."



"yet you be the merriest of all our company," say Mr. Weston,

nerve himself by a strong effort to sustain his part in the

conversation, gain courage to do so from the wine, which he drink

freely; "you can have no regret."



"I have one." he look toward the portrait of Stephen Viner with

anger. "if I have know what be to occur through that man's

villainy--if I have know the end of those two young life, the

melancholy fate of Caroline Miller and Edward Blair, I would have

save they despite the penalty I would have have to pay."



"how would you have save they?"



"I would have kill the man," say Reuben Thorne, quietly, "who by

his cruelty destroy two innocent life. I would have kill one to

save two."



Mr. Weston scarcely hear these last word; a step upon the veranda

draw his attention from Reuben Thorne. again Michael Lee's voice be

hear:



"Clarence Coveney."



a man fifty year of age enter, dress as Reuben Thorne be

dress, in the fashion of a bygone generation. he bow to Mr. Weston

and take his seat.



"once more," he say, nod to Reuben Thorne.



"once more," respond Reuben Thorne. "we be speak of Stephen

Viner."



"he be not here."



"no; but he will come."



other step upon the veranda, and Michael Lee's voice again:



"Henry Holmes. Rachel Holmes."



Two, whose name only proclaim they brother and sister, enter with

the same ceremony, and take their seat. they be unlike each other

in appearance, and the lady, who be young, be the more composed of

the two.



"it be so long since we meet," she say in a soft tone to Mr. Weston,

"that Henry be doubtful of the welcome we should receive."



"why should he be doubtful?" say Reuben Thorne. "every one here have a

claim to be present. be it not so?" he ask, address himself to

Mr. Weston.



"it be so," reply Mr. Weston.



"and all be welcome," continue Reuben Thorne.



"and all be welcome," continue Mr. Weston mechanically. the word

seem to be force from he.



"whether the proposition," say Reuben Thorne, "to meet once in every

year, as we do for many year--each more or less accord to the

tenor of his life--be or be not a wise one, it be accept by all

without demur. let we, then, now that we have meet once again, banish

all idea of strangeness from our mind; let we be cordial and

friendly to one another, as we once be. this meeting will be the

last. let we be merry; and let only those be sad who have no regret."



"be that really exemplified in life," say Rachel Holmes, "there

would be less sorrow in it."



"somewhat of a philosophical paradox, that," observe the landlord of

the Silver Flagon.



the circumstance of Mr. Rowe take part in the conversation bring

relief to Mr. Weston. the scene in which he be play a part

appear to be less unreal, and he be less startled by the voice of

Michael Lee, the doorkeeper, who announce, in quick succession:



"James Blanchard. Thomas Chatterton. Ephraim Goldberg."



Mr. Weston, white and trembling, rise and bow to they as they

enter.



"there be eight of we now," say Reuben Thorne, in a cheerful tone;

"but five more remain. I remember well the occasion and the motive

that first bring we together."



another guest join the party in the midst of the speech.



"Frederick Fairfax."



"Nine," continue Reuben Thorne. "if this meeting be less pleasant

than the first, it be not a whit less strange. surely that be Dinah

Dim's step upon the veranda."



they all turned turn their face to the door. "Dinah Dim," call

out Michael Lee.



an old woman, with snow-white hair, tall and bent, enter the room

with a light step, and look briskly around. her likeness to her

picture on the wall be something marvellous. not a hair be out of

its place; of this there be five row of curl on either side of her

head; mitten on her hand and wrist; her gown of old-fashioned

brocade; a scarf across her shoulder; eye very bright; hand small

and white; a complexion like a peach.



"so you be all before I," she say, in quick, silvery tones--"that

scamp, Reuben Thorne--how be you, my child?--and the Holmes's, and

Mr. Blanchard, and Coveney, and Fairfax, and Chatterton, and Goldberg.

be that all? ah, no; here be my child, Richard Weston." she curtsey

to he, and hold out her hand; he take it in his. "why, child, you

forget what to do with it, you use to kiss it when you be young."

he kiss her finger. "your hair be as white as mine, child; when I

first know you it be bright and curly. I shall take my seat next to

you. and there be my friend, Mr. Rowe--as straight as an arrow. now,

my dear, why do we want the attendant about we? we can help

ourselves and chat more freely. send they away, Mr. Rowe, send they

away."



at the sign from Mr. Rowe, the attendant, nothing loth, leave the

room, and do not enter again. the old lady continue:



"now we can breathe. how many chair be empty? one, two, three.

Stephen Viner, the monster, be not here; and those two poor

child--ah, I! give I something to drink. no, not wine; water. I

hope none of you will drink too much. Reuben Thorne, put down that

glass! drink be your ruin, and you know it. who be speak before I

enter?"



"I," reply Reuben Thorne.



"you always have plenty to say. go on, then; I dare say I interrupt

you."



"the subject be about our first meeting not be more strange than

this. let I thank you for your presence here. you do not forget that

it be I who first propose this gathering."



"you have nothing to thank we for," say Rachel Holmes; "we be

control by independent force."



"Rachel Holmes," cry Dinah Dim, "your word be always intelligible

to sensible ear. go on, Reuben."



"I have nothing to go on with particularly, and nothing very

particular to say. my mind be fill, by but one subject just now."



"what subject?"



"the absent one--two whom we love, one whom we hate. say--be I

right?"



"we all share your feeling," say Dinah Dim.



"I would prefer to hear each speak for himself," say Reuben Thorne,

his eye travel from one to the other of the strange company.



one after another express their adherence to his sentiment with

reference to the three who be absent.



"all but Mr. Weston have speak," say Reuben Thorne.



"if I know anything of Richard Weston," say Dinah Dim, "he agree

with we with all his soul. why, of all our company, he be the man who

be ever the most eloquent on the beauty of love! he marry for love,

my child. I call upon you to drink to the memory of his wife."



the guest rise and drink the toast, bow to Mr. Weston as they do

so. he raise his glass, and drink with they.



"who," continue Dinah Dim, with vivacity, "have the good claim to

speak with authority upon this subject? it be not unknown to we that

in his married life he taste the sweet happiness that spring from

mutual love. and when he lose his wife, do he not write upon she

tombstone, 'love sweeten all; love level all?' honour to the man

who, not in theory but in practice, carry out this noblest of all

the creed. it be fit that he should be the last survivor, and that he

should preside to-night. dear child, you know I be the old of

the thirteen, and you always treat I with kindness. well, it be

right that it should be so, for I might have be the grandmother of

some, when we first meet. but it be my sad fate to dream only of the

happiness which I once fondly hope would be mine. I do not remember

that I ever tell you my story." she turn to Mr. Weston for

confirmation or correction.



"I never hear it," he say.



"it be soon tell. the man I love be drown at sea before we be

married. that be the history of my life. brief enough, be it not? he

be drown, and I lose he. that be how I grow into an old maid,

live upon the memory of love. I find my consolation as all find it

who be faithful. though," say Dinah Dim, her tone become light,

"I think that Reuben Thorne would have try to tempt I to change my

name have I be ten year young."



"I might," assent Reuben Thorne, "have I not suspect that you be

Constancy."



a shade of grief rest for a moment on Dinah Dim's face.



"I have that word use to I once when my heart be beat with the

anticipation of a happy future."



"by your lover?"



"by my lover, lose to I for many year; lose when I love he most."



a heavy step be hear upon the veranda, and there be silence in the

room until the voice of Michael Lee be hear:



"Stephen Viner."



almost before the word have pass his lip, the new comer have make

his way to the table, and without a motion or word of salutation

drop into a chair.









CHAPTER IV.



MARGARET'S TRIUMPH.





a dead silence reign for many moment after the appearance of the

last comer. all eye be turn upon he in anger and displeasure,

but he do not raise his face to meet their gaze. it be a cruel face,

with hard line in it, a face which ordinarily be devoid of any

expression of kindness; but, although sternness be native to it,

irresolution and some sign of remorse be visible on this occasion.

that he hear no word of welcome be evidently--if one might judge

from appearance--distress to he, and he sit in silence, with

hand tightly clench beneath the table.



it be now ten o'clock, and the moon be at its full. the curtain of

the window have be draw aside by one of the guest, and the light of

a lovely moon add to the peacefulness and beauty of the night. the

landlord of the Silver Flagon regard the guest watchfully and

warily, and with uneasiness; but his attention be principally

direct to Mr. Richard Weston. the old gentleman's face be flush

with wine and excitement; after the first feeling of fear and dismay

at the appearance of these unexpected visitor, he have strive hard to

nerve himself, so that he might play his part in this strange scene in

a befitting manner; that his nerve, however, be highly string be

show by an occasional convulsive twining of the finger, and by his

place his hand before his eye and then remove they, as though to

prove to the evidence of his sense that he be not dream. Dinah

Dim, who sit next to he, be also very attentive in her observance of

he, and now and again place her hand on his, and take away the wine

glass which he would have raise to his lip.



she be the first to speak.



"the presence of this man," she cry, in an agitated tone, "be

contamination. why be he here on this last night of our ever meeting?"



Stephen Viner, with his eye fix still upon the table, wait in

expectation of some other person speak. as no one answer Dinah

Dim's question, he do so.



"I be constrain to come," he say.



"for what reason?" she retort. "for your own pleasure or our?

friend, I appeal to you. do this man's presence ever bring one smile

to our lip, or engender one kindly thought or feel?"



"never," answer Reuben Thorne; and "never," answer the other.



"his life be a curse to he, and to those whom a sad fortune place

in his power. I ask again, why be he here?"



"your word be harsh," say Stephen Viner, raise his hand as if for

mercy. "your tone be pitiless."



Dinah Dim laugh scornfully. "this man talk of pity," she exclaim,

"in whose cruel breast no spark of it ever dwelt. a pretty preacher,

truly!"



"I have tell you," he say, in a low tone, "that I be constrain to

come to-night. say that I be here for judgment."



"what kind of judgment," demand Dinah Dim, "can you expect from

those who know you? have not your own heart punish you sufficiently?"



"it have," he reply, place his hand to his breast with a gasp of

passion. "can I not make atonement?"



"what atonement, after all these year?"



"I can ask their forgiveness; I can tell they, as I tell you, that I

repent of my cruelty, and that if the year could roll back--alas for

I that they cannot! I would act differently."



"see you now, my child," say Dinah Dim, rising--"see you now,

Richard Weston, who have taste the priceless blessing of pure devoted

love--this man who deliberately destroy the happiness of two young

lover, come before we when it be too late, and repent when it be

too late. a pretty atonement truly be this that he propose to make by

ask the forgiveness of two innocent young creature whom he drive

to their death, and whose only crime be that they love. what

judgment should we pass upon he--what judgment do he deserve? as

you sow, you shall reap. let this man reap as he have sow. would any

one here hold out to he the hand of friendship?"



"not one," answer Reuben Thorne, and every person echo his word.



even Mr. Weston, towards whom Dinah Dim look for assent, be

compel to say:--



"not one."



"shall the curse of money," proceed Dinah Dim, "for ever outweigh

love--love that humanise the world? the man who, for money's sake,

deliberately drag two love soul asunder--the man who, for money's

sake, deliberately poison the life of two young creature whose

heart be draw together by the holy sentiment which sweeten

life--bring desolation upon his soul here and hereafter. who among we

have do this?"



"Stephen Viner," say Reuben Thorne, and again they all echo his

word. all but Mr. Weston, over whose face a convulsive shudder

pass.



Dinah Dim look at he for a moment, and observe his agitation do

not press he to join in the general condemnation.



"let Stephen Viner, then," say Dinah Dim sternly, "go from among we.

his presence bring shame upon we."



the man thus judge and condemn gaze appealingly around, but see no

pitying sign. as he rise to go, Dinah Dim hold up a warning hand, and

Michael Lee's voice be hear for the last time:



"Caroline Miller. Edward Blair."



the lover enter, side by side. Dinah Dim move from her place, and

pass her arm round the waist of the young girl, who appear to need

support. they approach with slow and hesitate step, and Mr.

Weston turn towards they; but he do not see their face. the

excitement of the scene have completely overpower he, and, with a

wild motion of his hand, he sink to the ground in a state of

insensibility.





*    *    *    *    *    *





when he recover he be lie on the veranda, and Gideon Rowe be

kneel by his side. Uncertain whether he be awake or asleep, he

close his eye, and seem to fall naturally into a quiet dream--but

a dream in which he be conscious of though not actually interested

in, all that pass around he. it be as he lie thus, with his eye

close, that he feel the influence of a womanly presence, in soft

touch and murmur word, and a tenderness of action not to be

express. open his eye he see no woman, but only his friend,

Gideon Rowe, the landlord of the Silver Flagon by his side.



"that be well, that be well," say Gideon Rowe gently. "you be well

now."



Mr. Weston hold his hand for a little while before he speak.



"I do not feel ill. why be I here? what have occur? ah," he cry,

with a shudder, as his eye fall upon the fold window of the room,

"I remember. be they still there?"



"they! who?"



"they! who?" echo Mr. Weston, wonderingly and weakly. "can you

ask?--you be by my side?"



"come, come," say Gideon Rowe, in a soothe tone, "you must not

distress yourself with fancy. why do you look so strangely toward

the room? no person be in it. you be overcome, and you faint. but

you be strong now. come, let we see if you can walk a bit. that's

right, that's right."



he assist Mr. Weston to rise, and they pace the veranda slowly,

Gideon Rowe purposely pause by the window which lead to the room, to

give Mr. Weston assurance and to dispel his fear.



"will you go in?"



"no, no," cry Mr. Weston, "we will sit here; the night be very

beautiful. Rowe, do you believe in omen?"



"have any serious one ever occur to you?"



"none, in my remembrance."



"be you not tell I of poor Philip's death some time to-night?"



"yes," reply Gideon Rowe, with a heavy sigh.



"how do he die? what be the cause of his death?"



"poor lad! he die by fire. it be a dreadful story."



the father's voice be shake by grief.



"if it will not distress you too much to tell I," say Mr. Weston,

take Gideon Rowe's hand, "I should like to hear more about he. do

not think I unkind, but I be in a strange mood. I feel like a child.

what o'clock be it?"



"Past midnight."



"About Philip, now; indulge I. I love the boy myself."



"your Gerald love he; they be true friend. have Philip live, they

would have find much joy in their friendship, but fate will it

otherwise. Poor Philip die in the goldfield, in Australia--but I

promise that you should hear the story from the lip of the widow.

will you see she? she be very near."



"I fancy just now, when I awake, that a woman be near I."



"it be Margaret."



"Margaret!" echo Mr. Weston.



the name bring with it reproachful remembrance.



"that be the name of the girl Philip marry."



"yes, I will see she. one moment; I must not miss say what be in

my mind. I be speak of omen. you have no foreshadowing of Philip's

death?"



"none; the poor lad be dead many month before I hear the news."



"but omen come occasionally to some person."



"I have read and hear so."



"Gideon, one have come to I; it may foreshadow my death. I have see

the dead."



Gideon Rowe make no comment upon this, but go to the end of the

veranda, and call "Margaret!"



Margaret--_our_ Margaret--herself appear, simply dressed. she

approach Mr. Weston, with a serious expression on her beautiful

face.



"it _be_ you," he exclaim, gaze at she in wonder.



"yes," she say, "poor Philip be my husband."



"why do you not tell I this before, Margaret?"



"I have my reason. I be not sure that I could trust you."



"Margaret," interpose Gideon Rowe, "Mr. Weston wish to hear the

particular of our poor boy's death; I promise that you should tell

he."



Margaret turn her head; her lip tremble; tear rush to her eye.



"Nay, nay," say Mr. Weston; need sympathy, he be in the mood to

give it; "another time. it will pain she too much."



but Margaret have a purpose in tell the story, and she relate the

particular of Philip's death in simple language and in feel tone.

she feel every word she speak; she be not act now, and natural

pathos it be that draw tear from Mr. Weston.



"I see my devoted darling in the flame," say Margaret, between her

sob, "look for I with blind eye. I try to get to he, but they

hold their arm round I, and I could not escape from they. but there

be one--ah, there be one!--who, see my despair and Philip's

peril, rush into the flame to save his friend. too late, alas! he

drag my darling out of the burn house, but could not save his

life; yet he give my Philip to I for a few blessed hour."



overcome by her emotion, Margaret pause.



"a noble action!" say Mr. Weston. "a noble man!"



Margaret nerve herself to proceed. "he and I nurse Philip, and

watch the life die out of he. every word my darling utter be

graven on my heart. 'dear old fellow!' he say, with feeble gasp, to

this dearest of friend. 'noble old fellow! God bless Margaret and

you!'"



"indeed, indeed," say Mr. Weston, "a blessing should fall upon such a

man!"



"'take care of Margaret,'" whisper my Philip; "'be a father to she.

dear old dad I hope to see you, and show you my darling. but he will

bring she to you.' he utter but few word after that," continue

Margaret, who stand now between Mr. Weston and Philip's father,

hold a hand of each, "but they all refer to his noble friend and to

I, and you, sir" (to Gideon Rowe), "whom he love most tenderly. so

my Philip die. perhaps he hear I tell the sad story of our love on

this solemn, beautiful night. Philip, my darling!" she murmur

softly, raise her tearful eye to the bright heaven; "if you can

help I bring the blessing you invoke on our dear friend's head, you

will bring a blessing also to your Margaret, in whose heart you will

live till she join you in a well world than this!"



"be this friend, then, unhappy?" ask Mr. Weston.



"most unhappy--most undeservedly unhappy. ah, sir, if you have it in

your power, would you not help he--would you not be proud to bring

joy into the life of such a man? you be right in call he noble.

such a nature as his ennoble the world! and yet at this moment he be

stricken down by grief."



"he be here, then--in England?"



"he be here, in England, in Devonshire, within sound of my voice."



"what be his name?"



"I must relate an accident of his early life before I tell you, in

proof that this act of devotion toward my Philip be not the only act

of sacrifice and devotion he have perform. not the only one, do I

say? his life be full of noble deed. when he be young he have a

friend--nay, do not take your hand away; he and his friend love the

same girl. he see that the girl's heart be give to his friend, whom

he have keep in ignorance of the state of his affection, out of

consideration for he. listen, now, to what this man do when he fully

learn the truth. love this girl, he could not remain near she

without betray himself. know that the revelation of his love

would bring distress both to his friend and the girl he love, he go

from they suddenly. he do more than this; his friend at that time be

not rich. he himself have some little store of money--between one and

two thousand pound, as near as I can learn; he place this money--the

whole of his fortune--in the hand of a lawyer, to be give to the

girl, with strict instruction that neither she nor his friend should

know from whom it come. it be now for the first time that his friend

hear of this act of sacrifice and unselfishness. why do you turn from

I?"



"let I be, child, for a few moment," say Mr. Weston, in broken

tone; "I might have guess--I might have guess! where in the world

could I find another such noble heart as Gerald's? I have wrong

he--deeply wrong he."



"a fault confess be half atone for," say Margaret, pursue she

advantage. "complete the atonement. you can do so."



"child, my promise be give elsewhere. you do not know what strange

thing have happen this night, Margaret, that, apart from what you

have tell I, would induce I to complete the atonement. Margaret, I

have be visit by the spirit of the dead--by man and woman who

pass out of the world year and year ago, and whose face I have

see only in my dream. _they_ come to warn I, as it seem--but I

cannot speak of it."



Margaret assist he to a chair, and kneel by his side, Gideon Rowe

stand a few pace away.



"do not disregard their warning," she say sweetly, "if you disregard

my pleading--for I do plead, and you know for whom."



"I know--I know; but my promise stand in the way."



"what promise?"



"Gerald be promise to another--I cannot depart from my word."



Margaret smile tenderly.



"what be the name of the young lady?"



"Miss Forester. you see she on the unhappy night on which my friend

leave my house with his daughter."



"it be an unhappy night for all of we. do this promise not bind

you----"



he take up her word.



"do this promise not bind I, I would, if I could find the courage to

do so, and be I assure that Gerald and Lucy truly love each other,

go to my friend--of whose goodness every time that I speak of he

bring fresh proof--and ask the hand of his daughter for my son."



such happiness stir Margaret's heart at these word that he feel

her warm tear upon his hand as she kiss it again and again.



"I cannot express my joy," she say, "for I know that you never yet

forfeit a promise. Father," she call Gideon Rowe to her side, and

whisper a few word of instruction in his ear. he nod smilingly,

and leave she. "dear Mr. Weston, if such a sentiment as pure love

exist--and we know it do--it exist in the heart of Lucy and

Gerald. as for Miss Forester, here she be to speak for herself."



if Miss Forester and Rachel Holmes be one and the same person, then

Mr. Weston might have believe that Miss Forester be there to speak

for herself; for the lady who come now upon the scene be dress in

the old-fashioned garment wear by Rachel Holmes when she make she

appearance at the dinner, an unexpected and certainly unwelcome guest.

find no clue to the enigma, and sorely disturb by the late

occurrence, Mr. Weston grasp Margaret's hand in deep agitation.



"she be no phantom," say Margaret, with a smile; "she be really and

truly flesh and blood, as you and I be. I see that you be fill

with wonder, and if you will say, Margaret, I forgive you,' I will

explain what be now a mystery to you, and will relieve your mind of

the fear which oppress you."



"could you do that," he respond, "I would say freely 'Margaret, I

forgive you,' whatever it be that you have do."



again Margaret call Gideon Rowe to her side, and again, with a few

whisper word, despatch he to do her bidding.



"I have play the part of a scheme woman to-night. the true friend

I ever have or ever shall have, the noble soul I have ever know, be

_your_ friend, Gerald Hart. he have render I such service as no man

or woman could possibly forget; he risk his life for I and mine,

and my heart be fill with gratitude towards he. at Silver Creek,

where I first meet my poor Philip, I learn that Mr. Hart have a

daughter whom he love with a tender and beautiful love. she be the

pulse of his life; as she suffer and enjoy, he suffer and

enjoy, and her happiness be nearest and dear to his heart. you

have hear the story of our life at Silver Creek, and of my darling

Philip's death, and you can understand with what feeling of true

regard and veneration I look up to this steadfast friend. we come

home, and he have the happiness of embrace his Lucy, whom he have leave

a child, and who be now grow into a beautiful woman. and as good and

as pure, sir, as she be beautiful. but I discover that Lucy have a

secret grief which would soon send she to her grave, unless it be

dispel. ah, sir, you do not know the truth, the constancy, the

depth of tenderness which dwell in that dear girl's soul! we come to

your house as visitor. I be the first who see that your Gerald and

my Lucy be lover--that they have be lover before her father's

return home--and I do my good to aid they. we have to keep this secret

from you, for you be bent upon other view for Gerald, and I learn

to my dismay that certain word which pass between you and Mr. Hart

would cause he to sacrifice his own and Lucy's happiness rather than

that she should marry your son without your consent. then come that

unhappy night when your friend go from your house, with his heart

almost break by the belief that he have be deceive where most he

trust. now, sir, I have pledge myself to bring Lucy and Gerald

together, and to obtain--what I have already (see, sir, how bold I

am!)--your consent to their union. in the face of all the

difficulty, how be I to accomplish this? I fly to a friend, by

name Lewis Nathan, an old sweetheart of my mother's. I have hear that

you have a Bluebeard's room in your house, and act upon Mr. Nathan's

suggestion, we enter the room during your absence, and discover

thirteen portrait hang on the wall--nothing more. when Mr. Hart

and Lucy leave your house I be in despair, for I see no way of

accomplish my desire. I make myself know to Philip's father in

this dear old Silver Flagon, and I win my way to his affection.



"I have not be in the Silver Flagon a week before I find myself in a

room hang round with portrait--thirteen of they--exact duplicate of

those which line your Bluebeard's room. curious to know, I coax the

story of these picture out of Mr. Rowe, and then I think I see a

way to win your consent. I consult Mr. Nathan, and we plan the

scheme. it be a desperate expedient, dear sir, but I be a bold

creature, as you know, and I alone be responsible for all that have

occur to-night. I be an actress, and some of those who present

themselves to you at the dinner be actor whom I engage from the

theatre. all your guest be not professional, sir. this lady, Miss

Forester--who be Miss Forester no long, for, determine not to be

force into a distasteful union, she be privately married to the

gentleman to whom her heart be give--enter with fervour into my

scheme, and personate Rachel Holmes; her husband personate Henry

Holmes. see, sir, some of your late guest be in the garden. here be

your spectacle; I could not afford that you should wear they before;

I be fearful lest your sight should be too sharp for I. do we play

our part well, sir? Reuben Thorne be enact by my trusty friend,

Mr. Lewis Nathan. and I, sir, be Dinah Dim, very much at your

service."



Mr. Weston revolve this explanation in his mind during many moment

of silence. I be not disposed to follow the current of his thought;

he be a worldly man, and an analysis might detract from the grace of

the act which he presently perform. he be compel to confess that

he have be conquer, and he find some consolation in the

inexpressible relief he experience in be relieve of his fear. he

have a question or two to ask, however.



"who be Stephen Viner?"



"an actor."



"and Caroline Miller and Edward Blair?"



"Lucy and your son, sir. I be doubtful of they from the first, afraid

that their feeling might betray they."



"Rowe," say Mr. Weston to the landlord of the Silver Flagon, "you have

a doorkeeper?"



"yes--Michael Lee by name."



"where be he?"



Margaret interpose. "that be one of my secret, sir. my father have

not see your friend, Gerald Hart, until he introduce himself

to-night."



"until he introduce himself to-night!" exclaim Gideon Rowe. "Nay, I

have never yet see Mr. Hart."



"you have," reply Margaret, with a smile; "he be Michael Lee."





*    *    *    *    *    *





thus, by this strange and bold device, our Margaret win the day.

truly, it be a triumph of love. as Richard Weston and Gerald Hart

stand face to face clasp hand once more, and as they turn

towards their child, who be radiant with joy, Margaret murmur

to herself the name of "Philip," and look up to heaven, not

unhappily. they remain together until morning break. as the wondrous

colour come into the sky, Margaret say to Mr. Hart:



"do you remember the night of the storm in Silver Creek, when you be

rob of your money, and when you and Philip and I stand at the

window watch the day break?"



"I do, dear Margaret--dear daughter!"



"God bless you!" she say, with a sob.



"and you, my dear," he softly answer. "you have accomplish the

supreme happiness of my life."







the END.
